Abstract 
 
English version 
This project documents the process and work of the thesis which completes the Master of Science in 
Architectural Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The thesis deals with the structural 
analysis and design of a multi-story building in timber structures. The main objective of the thesis is to 
redesign a traditional concrete structure in 9 stories by use of timber structures. Challenges due to the 
change of structural material is addressed and possible solutions are investigated. Advantages and 
disadvantages of using timber as a structural material have been determined, and an insight in what it 
requires to design highrise buildings in timber structures are provided. 
Due to the height of the building, which increases the demands on the stability against horizontal forces, an 
engineered wood product denoted cross-laminated timber or CLT is chosen for the main load-bearing and 
stabilizing elements. The cross-laminated build-up of massive CLT panels provides good properties in terms 
of strength and stiffness. In particular the high shear strength in the element plane makes CLT 
advantageous for stabilizing diaphragms in highrise buildings. 
The focus of the thesis is on the structural topics, but both relevant topics from other engineering 
disciplines and more holistic topics are addressed too. These topics include fire safety, building envelope, 
acoustics, sustainability, and costs. Throughout the thesis the design of the original building is maintained 
to enable the most precise comparisons between the original building in concrete structures and the 
redesigned building in timber structures.   
  
Danish version 
Dette projekt dokumenterer processen og arbejdet med specialet, som afslutter civilingeniøruddannelsen i 
Bygningsdesign på Danmarks Tekniske Universitet. Specialet omhandler en statisk analyse af en fleretages 
bygning med konstruktioner af træ. Hovedformålet med specialet er at omprojektere et traditionelt 
betonbyggeri i 9 etager ved brug af trækonstruktioner. Udfordringer grundet skiftet af 
konstruktionsmateriale er behandlet, og mulige løsninger er undersøgt. Fordele og ulemper ved brug af træ 
som konstruktionsmateriale er blevet bestemt, og et indblik i hvad der kræves for at kunne designe et 
højhus ved brug af trækonstruktioner er givet. 
Grundet højden på bygningen, hvilket øger kravene til stabiliteten mod vandrette kræfter, er et træprodukt 
kaldet krydslamineret træ eller CLT blevet valgt til de primært bærende og stabiliserende elementer. Den 
krydslaminerede opbygning af massive CLT-plader giver gode egenskaber med hensyn til styrke og stivhed. 
Især den høje forskydningsstyrke i elementets plan gør CLT fordelagtigt til stabiliserende skiver i højhuse. 
Specialets fokus er på de konstruktionsmæssige emner, men både relevante emner fra andre 
ingeniørdiscipliner og mere helhedsorienterede emner er også behandlet. Disse emner inkluderer 
brandsikkerhed, klimaskærm, akustik, bæredygtighed og omkostninger. Igennem hele projektet er designet 
af den oprindelige bygning blevet bevaret, for at muliggøre de mest præcise sammenligninger mellem den 
originale bygning i betonkonstruktioner og den omprojekterede bygning i trækonstruktioner.

1. 
Introduction 
This master thesis deals with the structural analysis and design of a residential tower in timber structures. 
The thesis is based on a building named Tower 2 in a project named Skovkvarteret II, which is constructed 
in Copenhagen, Denmark. The building is originally designed as a traditionally multi-story residential 
building in prefabricated concrete elements. Architectural drawings and project basis from the original 
project is provided by NIRAS A/S and is used for the basis of the thesis. The original building got a squared 
floor plan and include 9 floors with residential apartments.  
The scope of the thesis is to redesign the original building by use of timber structures, and address the 
challenges connected to the change of the structural material. It has been the objective to obtain a greater 
insight of what it requires to design highrise buildings in timber structures, and which advantages and 
disadvantages that are associated with timber as a structural material. To be able to compare the 
redesigned building in timber structures with the original building in concrete structures, the original layout 
and appearance of the building is maintained throughout the design process. 
The thesis address topics associated with designing a modern structure. The focus has mainly been on the 
structural topics, but topics belonging to other engineering disciplines have also been addressed in a 
relevant extent. 
The motivation for the subject comes from the increased focus on sustainability in the building industry. In 
particular the increased focus on sustainable materials and building methods have had a motivational 
effect on the choice of subject for this master thesis. In the future the environmental impacts of materials 
may have an increasing significance on the design of new building projects. Timber is generally assessed as 
our most sustainable and renewable building material, and therefore got a large potential for future 
projects. Due to this, it have been assessed as highly relevant to investigate the material and obtain a larger 
insight in the structural behavior of timber when applied for highrise buildings. 
Initially a general introduction to timber as a building material is provided. The chapter includes a brief 
introduction to the history of timber as a building material, as well as an introduction into the natural 
structure of timber and the most common timber products utilized for structural purposes. Subsequently 
the project basis for the redesign is provided, including description of the building layout, the structural 
system, and the building elements. The chapter also include information about the design limit states, the 
structural materials, and the loads acting on the building. The focus in the thesis is mainly on cross-
laminated timber, shortened CLT, which is an engineered wood product in the shape of massive panels. CLT 
is used for most of the main load-bearing elements in the redesigned building. CLT is chosen due to a good 
weight to strength ratio, and an ability to resist shear forces in the plane of the elements. 
After the project basis the vertical and horizontal load distribution have been performed. In these chapters 
the identification system for the structural elements is described, while the primary span direction of the 
slabs, as well as the location of the vertical load-bearing and stabilizing walls is illustrated. The critical 
horizontal loads and the distribution of loads and forces to the vertical shear walls and the horizontal 
diaphragms are also provided in this part. Subsequently the stability of the shear walls is investigated both 
regarding overturning, sliding, and deformation. 
 
 
==PAGE:14==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
2 
 
When all loads have been determined and distributed, the structural elements is investigated and 
designed. The chapter includes the design of slabs, vertical load-bearing walls, stabilizing walls, lintels, and 
beams. Initially the theory of the design and verification methods are provided. Subsequently the design of 
the most important connections are addressed. These include connections included in the stabilizing 
system of the building, connections for the support of the slabs, and connection used to anchor the facade 
and roof against suction from the wind. The connections included in the stabilizing system are the 
connections for the anchoring of the shear walls, and the connections for the joining of the CLT panels in 
the horizontal diaphragms. 
After the connections, the robustness of the building is addressed. To verify the robustness it have been 
ensured that a loss of a structural elements does not lead to a progressive collapse. Subsequently the fire 
safety with focus on the structural fire safety have been investigated. The structural elements have been 
designed to withstand the exposure from high temperatures in both the case of the standard fire and the 
parametric fire. In the chapter the temperature curves for both design fires are determined, and the 
impacts on the cross-sections and material properties of the structural elements have been determined. 
After the fire safety have been investigated, four non-structural topics is addressed. These topics include 
heat and moisture, acoustics, sustainability, and cost estimate. These topics are included to ensure a 
complete design of the building, as well as creating topics for comparison of the redesigned project with 
the original project. The topics that allow comparison of the projects include thermal insulation, 
environmental impact, and costs. 
Finally a few things regarding the use of timber as a structural material are discussed, before the most 
important results and findings from the thesis is summarized in a conclusion.            
           
1.1. 
Project boundaries 
In this section the main project boundaries are listed. Minor project boundaries are described in the 
concerned sections. 
• 
Structures below the ground floor level are not addressed. 
• 
The structures in the core located centrally in the building is not addressed. 
• 
Load from stairs is included in the load definition and load distribution but is not designed and 
verified. 
• 
Railings for stairs, balconies, and French balconies are not designed, but horizontal loads acting on 
the balcony railings are considered in the design of associated structural elements. 
• 
Anchoring of parapets have not been addressed. 
• 
Anchoring of the balconies on the facade have not been calculated and designed.

Andreas Juul Larsen

3 
 
2. 
Timber as a building material 
Wood has been used as a building material from the earliest known years of human existence and is the 
oldest material capable of transferring both tension and compression [Foliente, 2000]. The use of wood as 
a structural material have evolved through the years, and many wood based products have been invented 
to meet the needs of the building industry. The new products has made it possible to design and built 
complex structures that meet the requirements of architecture, stability and load-bearing capacity given by 
the designers and engineers throughout the history. Wood as a building material have achieved its success 
through its many advantages, which include a very high strength to weight ratio, a high workability and 
versatility, effective thermal insulation properties, and a status as the only renewable industrial product 
[Foliente, 2000]. 
 
2.1. 
Brief history 
Wood have had a big impact on human life from the very beginning, including in the building industry, 
where the use have varied a lot through the years. In the following subsections the history of timber as a 
building material is briefly reviewed. 
 
2.1.1. Early shelter 
From the very start of human existence the need for shelter to provide defense against enemies and the 
outdoor climate have been an important priority [Foliente, 2000]. Wood, together with other materials 
varying through the history, have been used to comply with this need. The earliest known use of wood in 
shelters, is as beams to support earth covered roofs in pit dwellings or as posts in wood or earth lodges 
(figure 2.1c). In the initial phase of human existence large-diameter tree trunks was avoided because of the 
lack of tools to cut across the grain. Instead smaller branches was used to build round huts of different 
types across the ancient world (figure 2.1a-b). About 8000-7000 BC the need for bigger shelters and heavier 
horizontal beams arose. This need started the use of rectangular shaped buildings and encouraged the 
development and understanding of multi-story systems (figure 1d-e). By 6500-6000 BC the first two-story 
buildings where built in Anatolia, Cyprus, and Greece, with the upper floor made by wood materials, while 
the lower floor and foundation consisted of various other materials depending on the building culture of 
the particular region. Parallel to this, other building forms developed around the world, including cones of 
wooden poles called tepees used by the Indians of North America, demountable huts of pivoted wooden 
sticks carrying a cone of poles called yurts used by tartar tribesmen in Central Asia, and lake-dwellings on 
poles in England and many other locations [Foliente, 2000]. 
 
Figure 2.1 - Types of early shelters: (a) Paleolithic round hut; (b) South African hut; (c) Circular earth lodge; (d) Lashed frame barrel 
vault with bark sheets; (e) Lashed heavy pole frame with thatched roof [Foliente, 2000].

4 
 
2.1.2. Early timber constructions 
In the Middle Age and into the renaissance, wood was the leading building material in Northern Europe, 
and carpenters was in the front of technical invention. The development around the world did not occur 
successively, because the possibilities depended on the timber available and the culture of the concerned 
region. Some techniques got passed from one region to another, while techniques in other regions 
developed independent from others [Foliente, 2000]. One of the earliest known forms of heavy wooden 
buildings is called palisades or stave constructions (figure 2.2a) and was dominant in Northern Europa from 
the Iron Age to Norman times. The palisades was built with solid walls of vertical trunks, either embedded 
in the ground or placed on horizontal sills of wood or masonry to avoid dry rot. Later from about 700 BC 
buildings with walls of horizontal logs placed above each other was introduced. These is known as log-cabin 
constructions (figure 2.2b). At the beginning round logs was used, but after the establishment of sawmills, 
square-hewn logs or sawn planks got popular. The rectangular logs made it possible to have joints along the 
building lengths and allowed better transfer of loads. Due to this, larger structures with up to six stories 
become realistic. The log-cabin construction is thought of as the forerunner of the modern shear structural 
system [Foliente, 2000]. As the demand for timber increased, both in the building industry and other parts 
of human society, new types of timber construction was introduced, where the solid timber walls got 
replaced by walls of spaced posts with infill of other materials. Examples of these are the straight-braced 
frame with post and pan construction (figure 2c) and the square-paneled frame construction (figure 2.2d) 
[Foliente, 2000].  
 
Figure 2.2 - Types of timber constructions; (a) Palisade or stave construction; (b) log-cabin construction; (c) Straight-braced frame 
with post and pan construction; (d) Square-paneled frame construction [Foliente, 2000]. 
Timber frame constructions had been practiced from as early as 6500 BC, developed from early tent-like 
frames, to simple cruck frames, to cruck frames with collar and tie beams, and eventually to post and 
rafter/truss frames (figure 2.3). Most building types cannot be connected solely to one part of the world, 
but many types origins from Central, Northern or Eastern Europe, where wood always have been a widely 
used building material. To join the different wood elements a wide range of joints was invented, including 
mortised joints, joints with wooden dowels and later joints with iron ties or dowels [Foliente, 2000]. 
 
Figure 2.3 - Evolution of heavy timber construction [Foliente, 2000] 
==PAGE:17==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
5 
 
2.1.3. The industrial revolution 
In the beginning of the industrial revolution bricks were used extensively in Europe, while wrought iron was 
used mainly to strengthen timber and masonry systems. Wooden trusses with improved bolting and forged 
straps were used for roofs and bridges. Bigger and taller constructions was made with the use of wrought 
iron and steel including the Eiffel Tower in Paris, and skyscrapers in New York and Chicago. Reinforced 
concrete was introduced and developed, which together with an increased use of wrought iron and steel, 
crowded the market for building materials [Foliente, 2000]. 
During the late 18th century, wooden beams and posts started to be replaced by cast iron elements in 
multi-story buildings. This was mainly due to lower fire insurance premiums, when using iron compared to 
wood [Foliente, 2000]. The insurance premiums reflected the experiences from city fires in the past and 
trough the industrial revolution. These city fires includes, among others, the fire in Rome in year 64, the 
1871 Chicago fire, and the three Copenhagen fires in 1728, 1795 and 1807. All these fires had a large 
impact, destroying great parts of the cities, with many human casualties and economical loses as a result. 
Common for the buildings in these cities was that they were mainly build of wood and located close to each 
other at the time of the fires. In many cases the fires had consequences for the future building laws, as a 
way of securing the city against similar fires. After the fire in Rome the empire introduced rules about how 
to separate buildings with double firewalls in stones and how to separate ground floor and 1st floor with 
masonry vaults above stores on the ground floor [Hertz, 2019]. In Chicago, the burned wooden buildings 
was re-built in iron to reduce the risk and the fire load. New construction in the US cities afterwards 
followed the trend af using steel and reinforced concrete [Foliente, 2000]. The first two Copenhagen fires 
was ignited by accident, while the last was ignited as a result of the first terror bombing in world history 
performed by the Englishman William Congreve, that had just invented the war rocket. The Copenhagen 
fires lead to rules about minimum width of roads and angled corners on buildings to allow fire services to 
pass more easily. After the fires, most buildings in the large cities of Denmark was built by non-combustible 
materials like concrete and masonry [Hertz, 2019]. 
Even though the reputation of wooden structures decreased due to the severe city fires, the invention and 
development of new building techniques and systems continued, in particular in the US where the wooden 
age reached its peak at the start of the 19th century, about two hundred years after it did in Europe 
[Foliente, 2000]. Beam trusses was developed as opposed to the European arch trusses. With the 
introduction of mechanical sawmills and mass production of nails, new framing systems like the balloon 
framing system and platform framing system was invented. The new framing systems consisted of studs of 
uniform length, which was precisely spaced, braced and nailed together. The systems introduced a greater 
flexibility with prefabrication options and reduced the need for skilled workers [Foliente, 2000]. 
The introduction of new building materials, and the increase of size and height of new constructions 
introduced the need for engineering calculations and knowledge of mechanical properties. Developments 
of structural theory and strength of material increased significant during the end of the industrial 
revolution. The publication of the method of truss analysis by Whipple in about year 1850, is through to be 
the start of the modern age of structural engineering [Foliente, 2000]. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:18==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
6 
 
2.1.4. Modern products 
At the start of the 20th century systematic research on wood as a material started. From wood mechanic 
studies a system of grading rules, working stresses, and design specifications emerged. These formed the 
basis for the use of wood as a structural material and started the move from a trail-and-error approach to a 
more scientific approach. Research included studies of the effects from fire, drying and preservation of 
mechanical properties. Also research on connectors and assembly systems was made. The knowledge 
obtained was published in standards and codes in Europe, The US, and Australia to provide practical 
information to engineers, builders, and homeowners [Foliente, 2000]. 
Trough the 20th century many wood-based products was introduced to the building market. Two of these 
products stand out by their impact on the building industry: glued-laminated timber and plywood [Foliente, 
2000]. A third wood-based product called cross-laminated timber is increasingly used around the world and 
is expected to have a very large impact on the building industry in the future [Harris, 2015]. 
• 
Glued-laminated timber, also named glulam or GLT, is made of parallel wood lamellas glued together 
with adhesives, and mainly used for beams, arches, and columns. The first known construction with use 
of early glulam elements was in Wiebeking’s bridge with a span of 42 meters in Altenmarkt, Bavaria in 
1809 [Foliente, 2000]. In 1910 the German carpenter Otto Hetzer got the first known patent on glulam 
construction, and in the next years he realized at least 50 projects with relatively long spans [Norske 
Limtreprodusenters Forening, 2015]. However it was not until the 1930’s that the extensive application 
of glulam in construction started to be realized. This was due to the introduction of wood adhesives 
able to produce bonds stronger than the wood cells [Foliente, 2000]. The use of glulam increased the 
possible element lengths and cross-section dimensions, while also introduced a greater design 
flexibility including curved and/or tapered elements. Glulam have since then been used for a large 
number of innovative buildings around the world including as arched trusses above roofed stadiums, 
suspended shell-rib or grid-shell structures above halls, and numerically controlled and 
premanufactured timber shells above large open spaces in various buildings like the health spa in Bad 
Duerrheim, Germany (figure 2.4). Glulam members is not limited to double curved elements but can 
also be shaped almost arbitrary with respect to curvature and torsion. By 2012 there were 10 glulam 
manufacturers in the Nordic countries with a yearly production of about 200.000 m3 [Norske 
Limtreprodusenters Forening, 2015]. 
 
Figure 2.4 - Glulam column and roof structure of health spa in Bad Duerrheim, Germany [Foliente, 2000]. 
==PAGE:19==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
7 
 
• 
Plywood, which is boards consisting of multiple thin layers glued together perpendicular to each other, 
was introduced to the building market in the start of the 20th century. The main structural use of the 
material is to transfer in-plane shear forces in shear walls or diaphragms. In the start, animal and 
vegetable glues was used and limited the use to interior purposes. In 1935 the use of synthetic resin 
adhesives introduced plywood boards suitable for exterior purposes [Foliente, 2000]. 
 
• 
Cross-laminated timber, also called CLT or X-Lam, is made of thick wood lamellas glued together with 
adhesives in a perpendicular fashion. In some extent CLT can be described as a crossing between 
plywood and glulam, or just as very thick plywood boards. CLT is mostly used as panels or plates, but 
can also be used as beams, columns, and girders. CLT was developed in Austria and Switzerland in the 
mid-1990s, as a result from research concerning finding a better use for outer layers of logs, that until 
then had no value for the sawmillers. By their mechanical properties these ‘side-boards’ are actual the 
best part of the logs, and therefore the research led to a laminated product called CLT, with boards 
oriented perpendicular to improve the stability [Harris, 2015]. Construction with CLT started slowly, but 
due to the green building movement, better efficiencies, and product approvals the use increased 
significantly during the early 2000s [Karacabeyli, 2013]. In 2013 there were 20 European 
manufacturers, and in 2015 the yearly CLT production was estimated to about 550.000 m3 [Harris, 
2015].      
 
 
Figure 2.5 - Multi-family building made by CLT elements in Judenburg, Austria [Stora Enso, 2015]. 
==PAGE:20==
Andreas Juul Larsen

Documentation report

DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
8 
 
2.2. 
The material 
Timber is our only basic renewal material, and, if it is from well-managed forests, one of the most 
sustainable materials available [Porteous, 2013]. The material is produced by cutting down trees and 
splitting the trunk into logs and planks of different shapes and sizes. Timber is used for all types of 
structural elements including beams, columns, and walls. The advantages of timber as a structural material 
is, among other things, its high strength to weight ratio, its ability to transfer both tension and compression 
forces, its high durability, and its insulating properties against heat and sound [Porteous, 2013]. In the 
following subsections timber as a material and building product is described. 
 
2.2.1. The structure 
A tree consist of three main parts: Crown, Trunk, and Roots. The crown comprises branches supporting the 
leaves. The leaves produce chemical reactions that convert carbon dioxide (CO2) in the air and water (H2O) 
to mainly carbon (C) and oxygen (O). The tree uses these to form cellulose, hemicellulose, and lignin, which 
are the molecules the tree consists of and that makes it grow [Munch-Andersen, 2016]. The roots work as a 
foundation for the tree, and transport minerals and water from the soil to the rest of the tree. The trunk 
resist loads to wind and gravity, and transport water and minerals from the roots to the crown [Porteous, 
2013]. 
The outside of the trunk is covered with bark that can be divided in an outer and an inner layer. The outer 
layer is a dead and dry layer that protects the inner living layer. On the inside of the inner bark layer the 
cambium is located, which is the growth center of the trunk. On the outside of the cambium new bark cells 
are grown, while new wood cells are grown on the inside [Porteous, 2013]. The center of the trunk consist 
of a number of visible concentric rings called annual rings. A new ring is grown every year from the inside of 
the cambium and increases the diameter of the trunk. In most trees each annual rings consist of both 
springwood and summerwood. Springwood consists of large hollow cells grown in the fast growing season, 
while summerwood consist of cells with small cavities and thick walls due to a slower growth. Due to this 
the denser summerwood got a larger density then springwood. The cells in the central part of the trunk are 
in general long, thin, and tubular. These cell are more or less parallel to the trunk axis, except so called rays 
cells that run radically across the trunk and transfer food around the layers of the tree [Porteous, 2013]. 
The outer layers of the annual rings are called sapwood, which consist of both dead and living cells that 
transport sap from the roots to the crown. If the tree is old enough a heartwood zone of the trunk is 
formed, which consist of dead cells in the center of the trunk. The annual rings just around the central pith 
is called juvenile wood and is formed in the first years of growth. The number of annual rings in the juvenile 
wood area depends on the tree type and the growing conditions. Juvenile wood are weaker and more 
flexible than the mature part of the annual rings [Porteous, 2013]. A typical cross section of a trunk is 
illustrated in figure 2.6. 
==PAGE:21==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
9 
 
 
Figure 2.6 - Cross-section of tree trunk. [Porteous, 2013] 
2.2.2. Types of timber 
Trees and commercial timber is divided into softwoods and hardwoods. The terminology does not directly 
refer to the softness or hardness of the wood, but only on the botanical origin of the timber [Porteous, 
2013]. Softwood trees have needle-like leaves that does not fall of at the end of the growing season. 
Examples of softwood trees is spruce, larch and pine. Softwood are characterized by a quick growth, low-
density, relatively low strength, and poor durability qualities. The quick growth allows the trees to be felled 
after 30 years, which makes them readily available and relatively cheap. Hardwood trees generally have 
broad leaves that fall of at the end of the growing season. Examples of hardwood trees is oak, ash, and 
birch. Hardwood are generally characterized by a slow growing rate, high density, relatively high strength, 
and good durability qualities. Because of the slow growth it often takes at least 100 years of growth before 
the tree can be felled, which result in lower availability and higher costs compared to softwoods [Porteous, 
2013]. 
 
2.2.3. Natural characteristics 
Due to timber being a natural material the structure is highly varied and dependent on natural 
characteristics or defects introduced during growth and industrial processing. These defects often reduce 
the strength and/or weaken the appearance [Porteous, 2013]. Here some of the most important natural 
characteristics or defects are listed and briefly described. 
• 
Slope of grain: The general direction of the arrangement of fibers in timber is parallel to the trunk axis 
and the longitudinal axis of the sawn timber. Most often the direction is not truly parallel due to 
deviations generated during the growth of the tree. These deviations result in cross grains, where the 
grain direction have an angle to the longitudinal axis of the sawn timber. The slope of the grain have big 
impact on the material properties and even small deviations reduce the strength significant. A slope of 
1 in 10 is accepted for high grade timber, while a slope of 1 in 5 is accepted for low grade timber 
[Porteous, 2013]. 
 
• 
Knots: These are very common in timber and is developed as a result of branches growing from the 
trunk. The knots are originating from the center of the trunk and is enclosed by the growth of the tree. 
Knots affects the fibers around then, creating discontinuity and stress concentrations. Then produced 
as sawn timber, the distorted fibers are often cut, which decreases the strength properties. The size, 
frequency, shape, and location of the knots influence the quality of the timber and is important when 
grading for structural use [Porteous, 2013]. 
==PAGE:22==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
10 
 
• 
Reaction wood: When subjected to strong wind forces, reaction wood is produced in tree trunks to 
prevent the tree from excessive bending and cracking. The location and composition of reaction wood 
in softwoods and hardwood are different but in common the density is higher, the longitudinal 
shrinkage is greater, and it tends to break in a more brittle manner than normal wood. The amount of 
reaction wood affects the quality grading [Porteous, 2013]. 
 
• 
Cracks, fissures, and fungal decay: Cracks and fissures is caused by fibers along the grain getting 
separated. These can continue from one side to the other but not necessarily. Presence of cracks and 
fissures can indicate decay or the beginning of it. If fungal decay, or decay indicated by cracks and 
fissures is present the timber should be rejected [Porteous, 2013]. 
 
2.3. 
Solid timber 
Due to timber being a natural material the strength and stiffness properties varies a lot, not only because of 
differences between species and regions, but also because two trees of the same species, age and location 
will often grow significantly different. The properties are a function of several parameters including density, 
species, moisture content and presence of natural flaws [Porteous, 2013]. To be able to assess the strength 
properties a strength grading system is used. The grading system consist of 26 strength classes, divided in 
12 for softwoods and 14 for hardwoods [DS/EN 338, 2016]. For softwood, the strength classes are defined 
as C14 – C50, where the C stands for coniferous species, and the number indicates the characteristic 
bending strength in MPa. Similar for hardwoods the strength classes are defined as D18 – D80, where the D 
stands for deciduous species. The grade of the timber is decided by either visual or machine grading, and 
helps the manufacturers, designers, and engineers by assigning a number of properties including density, 
strength, and stiffness [Porteous, 2013]. The use of solid timber is limited by the element lengths and cross-
section sizes available. Generally elements with a height above 225 mm, width above 75 mm, and lengths 
above 5 meters is difficult to obtain. Sections with larger dimensions would properly suffer from defects 
obtained during conversion and seasoning. To obtain larger dimensions an engineered wood product must 
be used [Porteous, 2013].  
 
2.4. 
Engineered wood products 
To exceed the limits of solid timber, a wide range of engineered wood product, also called EWPs, can be 
used. EWPs are more expensive to produce than solid timber but have many advantages varying from 
product to product. In general EWPs can be a more economical solution when produced in large sizes, 
because of a lack of trees suitable for cutting large sections [Porteous, 2013]. Many EWPs is produces by 
use of adhesives to bond smaller pieces of wood together. Some of these are made by thin veneers bonded 
together in different layouts, like plywood mainly used for boards subjected to in-plane shear, and 
laminated veneer lumber (LVL) mainly used for beams and columns in low-rise buildings. Others are made 
from chipped wood in different sizes, like oriented strand boards (OSB) with properties and use similar to 
plywood. Different EWPs can also be combined to form composite members like I-beams or box beams. 
These can either be made with thin webs and thick flanges or the opposite. For the thin parts plywood and 
OSB boards are often used while the thick parts often consist of solid wood or LVL elements. The composite 
members are light and efficient when using members with long spans [Porteous, 2013].  
==PAGE:23==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
11 
 
2.4.1. Glued-laminated timber 
Glued-laminated timber, also called glulam or GLT, is made by thin wood lamellas bonded together with 
adhesives and can be used for many types of structural members including beams, columns, and arches. All 
lamellas is orientated with the grain parallel to the longitudinal direction of the member (figure 2.8). The 
design options for glulam is very flexible, and the members can be curved in multiple directions and 
produced with sloped surfaces. The lamellas have normally a thickness of 12 to 33 mm for curved 
members, and a thickness of 33 to 50 mm for straight or slightly curved members. Long members is 
produced by using finger joints to elongate the lamellas along the longitudinal axis. The lamellas are dried 
to a moisture content of 12-18 %, before machined and assembled to avoid moisture related stresses 
[Porteous, 2013]. 
To ensure proper bonding of the lamella faces, mechanical or hydraulic jigs are used to press the lamellas 
together until the curing is complete (figure 2.7). Glulam members can either be produced with a 
homogeneous cross-section, with all lamellas of the same grading, or with a combined cross-section 
consisting of outer lamellas of a higher strength class compared to the lamellas in the center. By using 
stronger lamellas in the outer lamellas the strength properties is more efficiently used [Porteous, 2013]. In 
Denmark, the following strength classes for glulam members is mainly used: GL24h, GL24c, GL28h, GL28c, 
GL30h, and GL30c [DS/EN 14080, 2018]. GL stands for glulam, h and c stands for either homogeneous or 
combined cross-section, while the number describes the characteristic bending strength in MPa. In general 
glulam have better strength and stiffness properties compared to solid timber of the same dimensions 
[Norske Limtreprodusenters Forening, 2015]. 
 
Figure 2.7 - Mechanical jig; (1) Vertical plate; (2) Pressure equalization board; (3) Shim; (4) Pressure plate; (5) Tensioning bar; (6) 
Pressure equalization board; (7) Horizontal plate [Norske Limtreprodusenters Forening, 2015]. 
 
 
==PAGE:24==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
12 
 
2.4.2. Cross-laminated timber 
Cross-laminated timber, also called CLT or X-lam, is like glulam a product consisting af wood lamellas glued 
together with adhesives. Unlike glulam the lamellas are placed perpendicular, creating elements with 
orthogonally properties, making it suitable for solid panels and plates used for walls and ceilings (figure 
2.8). CLT elements consist of at least three board layers placed in right angles to one another and glued 
together over the full surface, while applied pressure [Wallner-Novak, 2014]. The outer layers are generally 
oriented in the direction with the highest capacity needs, e.g. main span for ceilings and vertical direction 
for walls. CLT is designed in this way to enable two-way spans, and increase the stability, while also reduce 
impacts from swelling and shrinkage. Generally symmetrical cross sections are used with three to nine 
layers, but up to 25 layers is produced [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. One layer may consist of up to three lamellas 
with fibers running parallel, but the total layer thickness is limited to 90 mm [Wallner-Novak, 2014]. 
Lamellas most common have thicknesses of 20-45 mm and widths of 80-200 mm, but thicknesses of 20-60 
mm and widths of 40 – 300 mm occurs. The element size of an CLT panel or plate is dependent of the 
manufacturer, but thicknesses of up to 500 mm, lengths up to 30 m, and widths up to 4.8 m are currently 
available. The lamellas are made of softwood in strength classes of C14 – C30, with C24 being the most 
commonly used. Normally the lamellas are dried to a moisture content of 8-15 %, before machined and 
assembled to avoid moisture related stresses. Like glulam, CLT elements can be produced with all lamellas 
of the same strength class, or with the outer layers of higher strength class than the central layers to make 
the material use more effective [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. 
 
Figure 2.8 - CLT and Glulam panel [Karacabeyli, 2013]. 
 
 
==PAGE:25==
Andreas Juul Larsen

Documentation report 
S144609

3. 
Project basis 
In this section the project basis for design and verification is given. This includes introduction of the building 
and the structural system, definition of the basis of design, description of the design limit states, 
description of the structural materials, and determination of the relevant loads and combinations. 
 
3.1. 
The building 
The project deals with a residential tower located on Else Alfeltsvej 10, 2300 København S, Denmark. The 
building is named Tower 2 in a project named Skovkvarteret II. The original building consist of prefabricated 
concrete wall elements and hollow core slabs. The scope is to redesign the building by using timber 
structures instead of reinforced concrete while limiting the necessary changes of the overall appearance. 
The building is illustrated in figure 3.1. The roof and eastern facade is removed in the figure to provide a 
look inside the building.  
 
Figure 3.1 - Building with roof and eastern facade removed.

3.2. 
Description of the building 
The building is designed as a squared tower of 17x17 meters with a total height of 28.3 meters from the 
terrain, and a total floor area of 2538 m2. The building contains 9 floors with a height of between 2.933 and 
3.000 meters. The building is used for residential use, comprising 31 apartments of 57 to 155 m2. All 
apartments are located in a corner of the building and have access to at least one private balcony. Except 
the location of windows and balconies the layout of floor 1 to 6 is identical and comprising four 
apartments. Floor 7 and 8 comprises four two-story high apartments, with access from the lower to the 
upper floor by private stairs. On the ground floor the number of apartments is reduced by one to make 
space for technical installations, bike parking, and waste handling. The floor level of this space is lowered 
0.8 meters to a total floor height of 3.733 meters. The apartments are accessed through a common access 
route containing one stair and one elevator. Access to the common access route is reached through the 
main entrance on the eastern facade or via the bike parking area at the western facade. On figure 3.2, 3.3 
and 3.4 floor plans for the ground floor, the 1st floor and the 8th floor is illustrated. These three floor plans 
covers more or less the layout of all floors in the building. On figure 3.5 a section of the building is 
illustrated. All architectural drawings used as basis for the project is found on drawing 100-116 in the 
associated drawing folder.

3.2.1. Structural system 
In the following subsections the structural system, both resisting vertical and horizontal impacts, is 
described. First the general structural system is described applicable for both the original and the 
redesigned project. This is followed by more specific descriptions of the two designs and the variations. 
 
3.2.1.1. General 
The structural system is made as a plate system with solid horizontal and vertical elements. The vertical 
loads acting on the roof and the floors is carried by assumed one-way spanning panels. The floor panels are 
supported by the exterior and interior walls, which distribute the loads to the foundations. Due to large 
openings in the floor structure between the lower and the upper floor of the two-story high apartments on 
the 7th floor, some of the floor panels are supported by either other floor panels or beams. 
The building is stabilized by six continuous interior walls, three in each of the two main directions. 
Horizontal forces acting on the exterior walls are distributed to the floor structures. The panels in the floor 
structures is designed with sufficiently stiff joints to ensure the assumption of an infinitely stiff diaphragm 
to be valid. The horizontal forces working on the horizontal floor diaphragms are then distributed to the 
stabilizing walls, according to their relative stiffness. The walls work as shear walls, and distribute the 
horizontal loads to the foundations, potentially with support from tension and shear anchoring if necessary. 
From the ground floor slab and down all structural elements are made by reinforced concrete, due to direct 
contact to soil with high moisture content. Investigation of the concrete elements is outside the scope of 
this project since the influence of the shift from concrete to timber elements having minor influence on 
these. 
 
3.2.1.2. Original project 
Information about the original project is provided in the associated structural documentation attached in 
appendix A.1. The documentation is produced by the Danish consulting engineering firm NIRAS A/S and 
provided in connection with this project. In the original project the floor structures are made by 220 mm 
hollow core slabs, while 180 mm hollow core slabs are used for the roof structure. The walls are made by 
prefabricated concrete walls, with thicknesses varying depending on the purpose. For the interior 
stabilizing walls a thickness of 200 mm is used, while a thickness of 180 or 150 mm is used for the exterior 
walls depending on whether they support the slabs or not. Balconies of light steel is anchored on the 
facades. In figure 3.6 the original building is illustrated while under construction, together with a floor plan 
illustrating the used span direction of the slabs.  
 
==PAGE:30==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
18 
 
 
 
Figure 3.6 – Left: Original building under construction [Google Maps], Right: Floor plan with span directions [Appendix A.1]. 
 
3.2.1.3. Redesigned project 
In the redesigned project, structural timber elements is used instead of concrete. Since a structural plate 
system is used, massive timber element capable of obtaining in-plane shear forces is beneficial. Cross-
laminated timber elements is used for both slabs and walls. The slabs are assumed one-way spanning, and 
the supports is distributed as evenly to the interior stabilizing walls as possible. For the roof, CLT panels 
with a thickness of 220 mm is used, while a thickness of 260 mm or 300 mm is used for the floor structures, 
depending on the load and the length of the span. For all walls, both interior and exterior, 200 mm thick 
CLT panels are used. The balconies are designed with a slab consisting af a CLT panel with a thickness of 140 
mm. The balconies are supported of cantilevered steel beams anchored to the exterior walls. 
The slabs are directly supported on top of the interior walls. From the ground floor to the 6th floor a 
toothed joint is used to avoid load transfer through the slabs and introduce a large risk of crushing the 
timber in the weak direction transverse to the fibers. At the exterior walls, the slabs is supported on the 
side of the wall by use of aluminium connectors. This choice is made to maintain as much height of the 
lintels above the wide openings in the facades as possible and obtain the required load bearing capacity. 
In the floor structure above the 7th floor, large openings are used to connect the two plans of the two-story 
high apartments. These openings introduce a need for elements spanning perpendicular to the common 
direction of the area. The CLT floor elements does not have the capacity to support the load from the 
perpendicular spanning elements, thus four steel beams is introduced. The beams then distribute the loads 
to the walls as the slabs else would. Figure 3.7 illustrates the beams and the slabs supported by them. The 
figure also illustrates the vertical load-bearing and stabilizing function of all walls. The function of the walls 
is applicable for all floors in the building.

3.2.2.2. Floor 
The same type of floor structure is used in two different thicknesses to comply with the structural needs of 
the building. For about ¾ of the floor area a CLT element with a thickness of 260 mm is used together with 
a concrete layer of 60 mm, while a CLT element with a thickness of 300 mm is used together with a 
concrete layer of 55 mm for the remaining ¼ of the floor area. The increase in thickness is due to long spans 
and unfavorable location of loads in these areas. 
The following two build-ups of cross-laminated timber is used in the floor structure: 
• 
CLT 260 L7s-2 (80l-2 - 30w - 40l - 30w - 80l-2) 
• 
CLT 300 L8s-2 (80l-2 - 30w - 80l-2 - 30w - 80l-2) 
Besides designing the floor structure to meet the requirements of load bearing capacity, sound insulation 
and fire safety, the structure must also preserve enough room height for the residents to feel comfortable. 
In residential housing this is often satisfied by preserving a free height of at least 2500 mm from floor to 
ceiling. With a minimum floor height of 2933 mm, the floor structure must not exceed a total thickness of 
433 mm. The thickest of the two versions of the floor structure is 430 mm thick, which meet the criterion of 
the maximum thickness. In table 3.2 the layers of the floor structure is listed, while the build-up is 
illustrated in figure 3.9.

3.3.4. Safety 
The safety of the structural aspect of the building is ensured on the basis of the following classes. 
 
3.3.4.1. Consequences class 
To evaluate the risk of human casualties and other consequences as a result of a structural collapse, a set of 
consequences classes is defined. According to table B.1 DK NA in [DS/EN 1990 NA DK, 2007], the concerned 
building is assigned to the high consequences class, CC3. This is due to an upper floor located more than 12 
meters above terrain in a building frequently used by residents [DS/INF 1990, 2018]. High consequences 
class indicates that the risk of casualties are either high or the economical, societal, or environmental 
consequences are large in case of a structural collapse. According to table A1.2(B+C) DK NA in [DS/EN 1990 
NA DK, 2007] a consequences class factor of KFI = 1.10, should be used when designing for the high 
consequence class.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:39==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
27 
 
3.3.4.2. Control class 
The control class is used to define the level of control trough the production of structural elements and 
execution on the construction site. All structural elements in the project are assigned to the normal control 
class, because no third party control is performed, and no eased conditions are assumed present. According 
to table F4 DK NA in [DS/EN 1990, 2007], a safety sub-coefficient of γ3 = 1.00 should be used for the normal 
control class.  
 
3.4. 
Design limit states 
The building and the structural elements are verified by use of the partial factor method described in 
[DS/EN 1990, 2007]. By use of the method it should be assured that none of the design limit states are 
exceeded in any of the relevant design situations. Design loads and capacities is calculated by use of 
characteristic values and partial safety coefficients specified in the concerned Eurocode. The relevant 
design limit states are described in the following subsections. 
 
3.4.1. Ultimate Limit States (ULS) 
In the Ultimate Limit States it is investigated whether the building and the structural elements can be 
verified in the extreme situations. The Ultimate Limit States include persistent, transient, seismic, and 
accidental design situations. The transient design situations is neglected and out of the scope of this 
project. [DS/EN 1990, 2007] contains rules for different types of persistent design situation, but only the 
design situation called STR concerning internal failure or excessive deformation of the structure or 
structural members is investigated. The other persistent design situations concerning loss of static 
equilibrium, failure or excessive deformations in soils, and fatigue failure of the structure, is assumed to be 
neglectable and out of the scope of this project. In the STR design situation the strength of the structural 
materials is the governing parameter.  
 
3.4.1.1. Persistent design situations 
When investigating if the structural element fails or develop excessive deformations in the persistent 
design situations it should be verified that equation 3.2 is complied with [DS/EN 1991, 2007]:

3.4.1.2. Seismic design situations 
In the seismic design situations it is investigated whether the building or the structural elements can be 
verified in the case of seismic impacts from earthquakes. In this design situation the stability of the 
structural system is investigated when exposed to horizontal forces from seismic activity.

3.4.1.3. Accidental design situation 
In the accidental design situations it is investigated whether the building or the structural elements can be 
verified in the case of accidents like collisions or fire exposure. In this project the accidental situation is 
investigated when designing for robustness and fire safety. 
 
3.4.1.3.1.

of equation 3.5 [DS/EN 1995-1-2, 2007]:

3.4.2. Serviceability Limit States (SLS) 
In the Serviceability Limit State it is investigated whether the building or the structural elements can be 
verified in the normal situation. The serviceability Limit States is used to ensure that deflections and 
vibrations from structural members does not exceed a level that could expose occupants to uncomfortable 
conditions or damage adjacent building parts [DS/EN 1990, 2007].  
 
3.4.2.1. Deflections 
A structural member should be designed with enough stiffness to limit the deflections to a certain level. 
The Eurocodes does not contain requirements for the limits, but the national annexes often contain a list of 
recommended limits that often give satisfactory results. In general the deflection limits is determined by 
the building owner on the basis of the future use. The deflection limits are often stated in relation to the 
length of the members and can be related to either the instantaneous or the final deflection. Furthermore 
the deflection can be developed from a single load type or a combination. The instantaneous and final 
deflection is illustrated in figure 3.14, while the link between them is defined in equation 3.4 [DS/EN 1995-

3.4.2.2. Vibrations 
The structural members should be designed in a way that ensures that vibrations is limited. The vibrations 
should be kept at a level that do not impair the function of the structure or cause unacceptable discomfort 
to the users. As with deflection the Eurocodes does not contain any requirements for the limits, but the 
national annexes often contain some recommendation. 
==PAGE:42==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
30 
 
In table A1.4 DK NA in [DS/EN 1990 DK NA, 2007] it is recommended that the first natural frequency of a 
floor structure in a residential building is kept below 8 Hz. If this can’t be complied with it should be 
ensured that the limit acceleration is kept below 0.2 % of the gravitational acceleration. If either of these 
recommended limits is complied with a satisfactory function should be ensured. In section 6.3.3 of 
[Wallner-Novak, 2014] a method for verification of vibration is given related to horizontal panels of solid 
wood like cross laminated timber. The method divides panels in three vibration classes according to the 
use. Panels are verified by fulfilling two criteria in terms of: 
• 
The first natural frequency. 
• 
The stiffness of the panel, defined as the deflection of a unit load of 1 kN. 
If the criteria in terms of the first natural frequency can’t be met, a criteria in terms of limit acceleration can 
be applied to ensure satisfactory function. The limits used for verification by the method is listed in table 
3.7 and comply with the recommended limits mentioned above. How to calculate the vibration values is 
reviewed in section 6.3.2.2. 
Table 3.7 - Vibration classes.

3.5. 
Structural materials 
The building is mostly built by structural elements made of timber, but also steel, aluminium and reinforced 
concrete are used. In the following subsections timber as a structural material is reviewed in depth. Steel is 
used for beams and connections and will be described briefly. Aluminium is only used for one type of 
connection and will be described when used. Reinforced concrete is not described, due to it only being 
used for structural parts in direct contact to the soil, which is out of the scope for this project. 
 
3.5.1. Timber 
Timber is a natural building material, with properties depending on moisture content and load exposure. 
Mainly the engineered timber product called cross laminated timber, CLT, or X-lam, is used because of its 
ability to resist in-plane shear forces, which is a dominant force type in plate systems. Due to CLT being a 
massive wood product, it also ads dead-load to the building. This is an advantage for the stability of the 
building, which light timber frame structures often lack. To join the individual CLT elements, timber boards 
and different types of fasteners is used. The following subsections will focus on cross laminated timber, but 
important properties and coefficients for solid timber, glulam and LVL is also described. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:43==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
31 
 
3.5.1.1. Manufacturer 
Cross-laminated timber is not yet incorporated in Eurocode 5, but the European Standard DS/EN 
16351:2015 deals with the design and performance requirements of the product. CLT is produced by many 
suppliers in Europe, mainly in Austria, Germany, and Sweden. For this project, a company called Stora Enso 
from Austria is used as supplier. The company produces CLT elements with a maximal length of 16 meters, 
a maximal width of 2.95 meters, and a maximum thickness of 400 mm. Lamellas is produces in timber of 
spruce with a thickness of 20, 30, or 40 mm [Stora Enso, 2015]. 
 
3.5.1.2. Service classes 
Strength and stiffness of timber depends on the moisture content. To take this into account all structural 
elements should be assigned to one of three service classes defined in [DS/EN 1995-1-1, 2007]. In this 
project all structural timber elements, except the one used for balconies, is assigned to service class 1. This 
class is used for heated indoor conditions, where the relative humidity seldom exceed 65 %, which 
correspond to a moisture content of about 12 % for most softwood species [Porteous, 2013]. The timber 
elements used for balconies is assigned to service class 2, used for covered structures, with a relative 
humidity seldom exceeding 85 %, corresponding to a moisture of about 20 % for most softwood species 
[Porteous, 2013]. Service class 3, which corresponding to unprotected outdoor timber structures, are not 
used in this project. Cross-laminated timber is approved to be used for service class 1 and 2. 
 
3.5.1.3. Load duration classes 
The strength parameters of timber decreases proportional with the duration of the loading. The longer the 
load duration the more the strength decreases [Porteous, 2013]. When verifying the strength and stiffness 
of structural timber the elements should be assigned to a load duration class, which is defined by the 
assumed duration of the load exposure. The load duration classes is defined in table 2.1 in [DS/EN 1995-1-
1, 2007], and examples of load exposures is given in table 2.2 DK NA in [DS/EN 1995-1-1 DK NA 2019]. The 
definitions and examples are summarized in table 3.8. If loads assigned to different load duration classes 
are combined, the shortest duration are the governing. 
Table 3.8 – Load duration classes 
Load duration class 
Symbol 
Duration 
Examples of load exposure 
Permanent 
P 
More than 10 years 
Self-weight 
Long term 
L 
6 months to 10 years 
Storage loading, Silo loading 
Medium term 
M 
1 week to 6 months

Short term 
S 
Less than one week 
Snow load, Temporary structures 
Instantaneous 
I 
Instantaneous 
Wind load, Accidental loading, Random point loads 
 
The long term load duration class is not used in this project and is therefore not further mentioned.

3.5.1.4. Characteristic material properties 
The characteristic material properties of wood materials used for this project is reviewed in the following 
subsections. 
 
3.5.1.4.1. 
Cross-laminated timber 
As stated in [DS/EN 16351, 2015] lamellas used for CLT elements should be graded according to the 
strength classes defined in [DS/EN 338, 2016]. Additional parameters used for timber in CLT elements are 
defined in [DS/EN 16351, 2015]. All parameters are valid for timber at moisture content consistent with a 
temperature of 20oC and a relative humidity of 65 %. For this project softwood timber of strength class C24  
is used. Characteristic material parameters for these strength classes are listed in table 3.9. Stora Enso is 
mainly using lamellas of C24 grade, but other grades are available on request. [Stora Enso, 2015].

3.5.1.5. Ultimate Limit States (ULS) 
To verify if a structural system or element got the required capacity or stability in the persistent design 
situation, design strength properties is used. To calculate the design strength properties a number of 
coefficients should be determined. 
 
3.5.1.5.1. 
Partial safety coefficients 
To consider uncertainties in the resistance model and effects from unfavorable deviations in material and 
product properties the characteristic values is divided by a partial safety coefficient γM [Porteous, 2013]. 
CLT is not yet incorporated in Eurocode 5, and thus no values for the partial safety coefficients has yet been 
assigned. CLT have many similarities to other engineered wooden products like glulam and LVL, and 
therefore the partial safety coefficients assigned to glulam, LVL and plate materials in table 2.3 DK NA in 
[DS/EN 1995-1-1 DK NA, 2019] is assumed appropriate to use. The partial safety coefficients is calculated as 
stated in equation 3.7.

0: 
Accounts for the type of failure. Except for glued connections the type of failure is given as 
warned without capacity reserve. Glued connections are not used in this project. 
0:

3.5.1.5.5. 
Member size effects 
The tension strength of timber increases with reduced member dimensions. The characteristic tension and 
flexural strengths in table 3.10, 3.11 and 3.12 is valid for solid timber with a height of 150 mm, glulam with 
a height of 600 mm and LVL with a height of 300 mm and a length of 3000 mm. If smaller dimensions is 
used the strength can be increased. Only the strength of LVL have to be decreased if larger dimensions is 
used compared to the reference sizes. The coefficients for the member size effects is calculated by use of 
equation 3.13, 3.14, 3.15 and 3.16 [DS/EN 1995-1-1, 2017].

3.5.1.5.6. 
System coefficient 
If a number of similar elements work together as a system, the element resistance can be increased with 
the system coefficient D. When gluing several boards of one layer up in parallel, the load distribution 
takes place via several structural elements simultaneously [Wallner-Novak, 2014]. The system coefficient 
for glued laminations valid for cross-laminated timber, can be found by use if the relation illustrated in 
figure 3.15. The system coefficient is not used in this project for simplicity. 
 
Figure 3.15 - System coefficient for glued lamination [Wallner-Novak, 2014]. 
 
3.5.1.6. Serviceability Limit States (SLS) 
When verifying a structural element in the Serviceability Limit States, mean values for the stiffness 
properties are used to make the most precise estimates [Munch-Andersen, 2016].  
 
3.5.1.6.1. 
Deformation factors 
When calculating the final deflection, including deformation from creep developing relative to the load 
exposure, a deformation coefficient kdef is used. The deformation coefficient considers the type of material 
and the moisture content, which is important parameters for the extent of the creep development 
[Porteous, 2013]. In table 3.16 the relevant deformation coefficients as listed. For solid timber, glued-
laminated timber and laminated veneer lumber the values are taken from table 3.2 in [DS/EN 1995-1-1, 
2007], while values for cross-laminated timber is taken from table 3.10 in [Wallner-Novak, 2014].

3.6. 
Loads 
The loads acting on the structure is specified according to the concerned parts of Eurocode 1. To make the 
most precise comparison to the original building in reinforced concrete, the loads are specified, if possible, 
to match the structural documentation received by NIRAS A/S, attached in appendix A.1.

Most of the light partition walls in the building is made of aerated concrete with a thickness of 100 mm or 
150 mm and is assumed to be permanent walls. According to [DS/EN 1991-1-1 DK NA, 2013], self-weight 
from permanent light partition walls can be included in the self-weight of the floor structure as an 
equivalent uniformly distributed load contribution, if the following two requirements are complied with: 
• 
The total self-weight of the partition wall per m2 of the wall surface must not exceed 1.5 N/%. 
• 
The total self-weight of the partition wall per m of the wall length must not exceed 4.0 N/%. 
With a self-weight of O = 6.5 N/%3, a maximal thickness of P = 0.15 %, and a height of ℎ= 2.6 %, the 
values are calculated as: 
• 
Self-weight per m2: O = 6.5 N/%3 ∗0.15 % = 0.98 N/%  ≤1.5 N/%  
OK 
• 
Self-weight per m: 
O = 6.5 N/%3 ∗0.15 % ∗2.6 % = 2.54 N/% ≤4.0 N/% 
OK 
As the requirements are complied with, the self-weight of the light partition walls can be applied to the 
self-weight of the floor structure as an equivalent uniformly distributed load contribution. The magnitude 
of the load contribution should be chosen as the largest of the following: 
• 
O = 0.5 N/%. 
• 
The self-weight of the partition wall per m2 of the wall surface, calculated as: O = 0.98 N/%. 
• 
Self-weight of all light partition walls in the concerned area divided with the floor area. The lower 
floor of apartment 3 (see architectural drawings) on the 7th floor is used for the calculation. The 
lower floor of the apartment got a floor area of 68 %, and include 3 meters of partition walls with 
a thickness of 150 mm and 16 meters of partition walls with a thickness of 100 mm: 
O = 6.5 N/%3 ∗2.6 % ∗R3 % ∗0.15 % + 16 % ∗0.10 %S
68 %
= 0.51 N/% 
The largest value of the equivalent self-weight is then determined as: O = 0.98 N/%. This value is 
rounded up to: O = 1.00 N/%, to match the value used in the documentation of the original project. 
The value is used for the upper bound value, which is used when the self-weight is unfavorable, O,T. For 
the lower bound value, used when the self-weight is favorable, like when verifying the stability, the value: 
O,	 = 0 N/% is used to account for future renovations.

Imposed load acting on balconies (category A5) is including potential concurrent contributions from snow 
load. Load from snow inclusive snow drift, can exceed the value in table 3.22, and imposed load should 
then be neglected. Vertical surface loads and vertical point loads should not be calculated as working 
simultaneously. A point load is normally assumed to act on a square with dimensions of 50 x 50 mm. 
 
3.6.3. Snow load 
The snow load acting on the roof and balconies is calculated according to [DS/EN 1991-1-3, 2007] and 
[DS/EN 1991-1-3 DK NA, 2015]. Snow load is classified as a variable bound vertical load. Both the roof and 
the balconies got a slope below 5 degrees and is therefore calculated as a flat monopitch roof. This means 
that the snow load shape coefficient μ1 is used (figure 3.16). According to table 5.2 in [DS/EN 1991-1-3, 
2007] the snow load shape coefficient of a flat monopitch roof is defined as μ1 = 0.8. The uniformly 
distributed characteristic snow load is then calculated by use of equation 3.17 [DS/EN 1991-1-2, 2007].

Snow drift is developed on the balconies because the building façade works as an obstacle. Snow drift is 
calculated as a triangular load, with the highest value at the façade. The snow load from drift is working 
simultaneously with the uniform distributed snow load. Snow drift is both developed when the concerned 
balcony is located in the wind side and in the lee side, with the location in the wind side resulting in the 
largest value of snow drift. To limit the calculation only the value of the snow drift in the most critical 
situation is investigated, namely the lowest located balcony on the wind side of the building. This value for 
the snow drift is then used for all balconies, which is assumed to be appropriate and a conservative choice. 
The snow drift is calculated according to [DS/EN 1991-3 DK NA, 2015]. 
First it should be defined whether the obstacle affects the snow distribution in a local or global manner. 
This is defined by the geometric parameter in equation 3.20.

3.6.4. Wind load 
The wind load acting on the building is calculated according to [DS/EN 1991-1-4, 2007] and [DS/EN 1991-1-
4 DK NA, 2015]. Wind load is classified as a variable bound load acting perpendicular to the surfaces. The 
magnitude of the wind load depends on a variety of parameters, like the geometry of the building, the 
location, the surroundings, and the wind direction. The wind load on surfaces is calculated by use of 
equation 3.21 and 3.22 [DS/EN 1991-1-4, 2007].

3.6.4.1. Terrain category 
The surroundings is described by a defined terrain category, determined by the geometry and frequency of 
obstacles around the building. The building is located on Western Amager in Copenhagen, Denmark, close 
to a relatively open area to the west. The terrain category is determined to match the choices made in the 
project documentation of the original building. The determined terrain categories are illustrated in figure 
3.18 and listed in table 3.25, together with the assigned values defined in [DS/EN 1991-1-4, 2007] and 
[DS/EN 1991-1-4 DK NA, 2015].

3.6.4.2. Fundamental basic wind velocity 
The fundamental basic wind velocity is defined as the 10 minute mean wind velocity at a height of 10 
meters above flat open terrain, corresponding to terrain category II. The fundamental basic wind velocity is 
independent of wind direction [DS/EN 1991-1-4, 2007]. Except for locations less than 25 km from the 
western coast of Jutland, the fundamental basic wind velocity is defined as wx,2 = 24 %/) everywhere in 
Denmark [DS/EN 1991-1-4 DK NA, 2015].

3.6.4.9. Interior wind pressure 
When a building is exposed to exterior wind pressure, either an overpressure or underpressure is 
developed inside. According to [DS/EN 1991-1-4, 2007] the most unfavorable of the two pressure types 
should be used together with the concerned external wind pressure when verifying the building or the 
structural elements. In table 3.31 the shape factors and the corresponding interior characteristic wind loads 
calculated by equation (3.22) is listed. When verifying the stability of the building the interior wind pressure 
is equalized and therefore neglected.

3.6.4.10. Wind on balconies 
Eurocode 1 does not define wind load shape factors for balconies. Instead it is assumed appropriate to 
assign the balconies for wind zone A, normally used for vertical wall. The wind is affecting the floor of the 
balcony vertically, either upwards or downwards, while the railing is affected horizontally. Since an open 
railing is used, the magnitude of the horizontal wind load is decreased by 50 percent. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:63==

3.6.5. Seismic load 
The building should be verified against seismic impact, even though these are relatively small in Denmark. 
Seismic load is used to ensure the strength and stability of the structure against seismic activity. Seismic 
load is classified as a horizontal bound load, defined as a certain percentage of the simultaneously working 
vertical load. Seismic load work in an arbitrary direction, with point of application in the center of gravity of 
the associated vertical load. The seismic load does not work simultaneously with the wind load. 
According to table A1.3 DK NA in [DS/EN 1990 DK NA, 2019] the magnitude of the seismic load is calculated 
according to [DS/EN 1998-1, 2005] and [DS/EN 1998-1 DK NA, 2019]. The latter is the first Danish annex 
related to Eurocode 8, and is only existing as a consulting edition. It is expected to be approved in 2020. 
Until then the consulting edition can be used temporarily. [DS/EN 1998-1 DK NA, 2019] introduces a 
simplified method for calculating the seismic load, compared to the rules in [DS/EN 1998-1, 2005]. To 
calculate the seismic load for this project the method in Annex D DK NA in [DS/EN 1998-1 DK NA, 2019] is 
used. 
The seismic load is calculated by use of equation 3.31 [DS/EN 1998-1 DK NA, 2019].

3.6.6. Geometric imperfections 
When verifying in the Ultimate Limit State for permanent design situations and accidental design situations, 
the unfavorable deviations in the geometry of the structure and location of loads should be accounted for. 
These deviations or misalignments is called geometric imperfections and should both be accounted for 
when verifying individual structural elements, and when verifying the whole structural system. The effect 
on the individual structural elements is introduced as load eccentricities, which is accounted for in section 
6.5.1.1. For the determination of the geometric imperfections when investigating a structural system made 
of cross-laminated timber, [Wallner-Novak, 2014] suggest that the method stated in section 5.4.4 of [DS/EN 
1995-1-1, 2007], intended for analysis of frames and arches, should be used. The geometric imperfections 
is defined as a horizontal force acting in the gravitational center of the associated vertical load. The 
geometric imperfections are acting simultaneously with either the horizontal wind load or the seismic load. 
The horizontal force from geometric imperfection is described by equation (3.34) [Wallner-Novak, 2014]. 
 = › ∗V
(3.34)
Where:

Geometric imperfection angle. 
•: 
The associated vertical force [kN].

equation 3.35 [DS/EN 1995-1-1, 2007].

4. 
Load distribution 
The building should both resist vertical and horizontal forces. These forces are distributed to the 
foundations by the load-bearing and the stabilizing system, respectively. The structural system is described 
in section 3.2.1, and the load distribution is based on this.  
 
4.1. 
Definition of structural elements 
To be able to distinguish between the individual structural elements, and more easily describe the load 
distribution a method for definition of elements have been created, which is described in this section. 
Horizontal surface elements – Slabs, Balconies and Stairs 
Definition code: Z - Type – Zone number – Subdivision 
Where: 
Z: 
 
Indicates a horizontal surface element zone. 
Type: 
 
FS = Floor slab (Slab above Ground floor to 7th floor). 
RS = Roof slab (Slab above 8th floor). 
BA = Balcony. 
ST = Stair. 
Zone number:  
Horizontal surface elements is collected in zones. 
Subdivision: 
Horizontal surface elements in the same zone is divided in subzones, 
depending on the support conditions. 
Vertical surface elements - Walls 
Definition code: W – Type – (Direction) – Number 
Where: 
W: 
Indicated a vertical surface element (Wall). 
Type: 
E = Exterior wall. 
 
I = Interior wall. 
Direction: 
The direction of the interior walls is indicated with either a X or an Y. 
 
X = Parallel with module line A, B, C and D. 
 
Y = Parallel with module line 1, 2, 3 and 4. 
Number: 
The vertical surface elements is assigned a number. 
Most of the vertical surface elements has varying loads along their length. Due 
to this each element is divided in up to five parts, defined as A to E.

Horizontal line elements – Beams 
Definition code: E – B – Number – Material 
Where: 
E: 
 
Indicated a horizontal line element. 
B: 
 
Indicated a beam. 
Number: 
 
The horizontal line elements is assigned a number. 
Material: 
 
S = Steel 
 
 
G = Glued-laminated timber 
Beams made of glued-laminated timber is only included in the project as an 
alternative solution to the steel beams.

4.2. 
Layout of slabs 
The layout of the slabs as illustrated on figure 4.1, 4.2, 4.3, 4.4 and 4.5 is based on the following criteria: 
• 
The standard production width of cross-laminated timber elements by the manufacturer Stora Enso 
is either 2450 mm, 2750 mm, or 2950 mm [Stora Enso, 2015]. It is attempted to design as many 
elements as possible with a width matching the standard sizes. When not possible, elements with a 
width of maximum 1475 mm, equivalent to the half width of the widest standard width, is sought. 
This makes it possible to produce two individual elements from one standard width, and thus 
reduce the material consumption. The building is originally designed for concrete elements; thus 
the width of the slab zones often does not match the standard production width.  
 
• 
The span direction of the slabs is selected as the shortest length for the slab zones as a whole. A 
layout which avoids the use of beams is selected for a number of reasons. The use of beams 
increases the number of required elements, which result in an increased usage of time on the 
construction site. The possible height of the beams would have been very limited due to the 
desired ceiling height, and therefore timber beams would not have been an ideal solution. By 
avoiding beams, the stabilizing force distributed to the stabilizing walls is optimized, which is 
preferable. 
 
• 
As illustrated on figure 4.1 and 4.2, a toothed joint is used for support of the slabs on the interior 
walls in the slabs above ground floor to 5th floor. The teeth is designed with a width of 250 mm. The 
edges of the slabs is located in a way, that ensures that the slab teeth is not cut. If this were not 
avoided it would decrease the possible locations of joints in the horizontal diaphragms. 
 
4.3. 
Vertical load distribution 
Besides the self-weight of the structure, the building should also be able to resist forces from variable loads 
acting on slabs, balconies, and stairs. In table 4.1 the load definitions included in the project is listed. In the 
table all vertical loads are listed. The load definitions is attached as appendix D. 
The following choices have been made to simplify the vertical load distribution: 
• 
For the wind load on the roof the most critical wind zone and wind direction is used for the whole 
roof. This means that zone I with wind from west is used as the critical compressive force, and 
zone F with wind from west is used as the critical suction force. Internal underpressure is added to 
the compressive force, and internal overpressure is added to the suction force.  
  
• 
For snow load on the balconies the average surface load is used. This is a conservative 
approximation since the assumed uniform distribution is more unfavorable than the actual 
triangular distribution.
