4.4. 
Horizontal load distribution 
The stabilizing system of the building should be verified to resist horizontal forces in all directions. The 
horizontal forces include wind load, seismic load, and geometric imperfections. The wind load acts as a 
combined surface load from compression on the wind side and suction on the lee side. Seismic load and 
geometric imperfections acts as a line load in the center of gravity of the associated vertical load. Wind load 
and seismic load is two different load cases, and only the most critical should be investigated. A 
contribution from the geometric imperfections should be added to the most critical horizontal load case, as 
a defined percentage of the associated vertical load. The wind load acts in the concerned direction of the 
wind, while the seismic load acts in an arbitrary direction. The contribution from the geometric 
imperfections is calculated to work in the same direction as either the wind load or the seismic load, 
depending on which is the critical. As stated in section 3.6.4 the critical wind direction is wind load from 
west, while the wind load from the other three main directions is of the same order of magnitude. To verify 
the stability of the building in both main directions, the horizontal forces from west and north is 
investigated. Stability is achieved by ensuring sufficient resistance of the horizontal slab diaphragms and 
vertical shear walls. It should be verified that the stabilizing members and connections have enough 
capacity to distribute the horizontal loads via diaphragms and shear walls to the foundations. On figure 4.9 
the building is illustrated with attached wind directions. 
 
Figure 4.9 - Building with wind directions. 
==PAGE:81==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
69 
 
4.4.1. Wind load 
The horizontal load from wind acts as a combined surface load from compression on the wind side and 
suction on the lee side. Only the wind on the exterior surfaces is considered, as the wind pressure on the 
interior surfaces is equalizing. The wind load on the facades are distributed to the horizontal slab 
diaphragms as line loads. Each diaphragm receive the horizontal load from half the height of the façade on 
the floor over and under its location. All horizontal wind load acting on the parapet is distributed to the 
roof diaphragm. The horizontal load acting on the two-story high façade on 7th to 8th floor is distributed 
equally to the diaphragm above the 6th  and the 8th floor. The distribution of horizontal wind load to the 
diaphragms is illustrated on figure 4.10 and 4.11 for wind from west. 
 
Figure 4.10 - Distribution of horizontal wind load on diaphragms. Wind from west.

4.4.2. Seismic load 
The seismic load is distributed to the horizontal diaphragms by the location of the center of gravity of the 
associated vertical load. Vertical load acting on the slabs is calculated as acting on the diaphragm the 
concerned slab is a part of. Vertical load from the walls is assumed to have center of gravity at half the 
height of the walls, thus the load is calculated as acting equally on the diaphragm above and below the 
wall. In general the seismic load is distributed in a similar manner as the wind load. The magnitude of the 
seismic load is calculated as a specific part of the associated vertical load. The magnitude of the part is 
calculated in section 3.6.5 and rewritten in equation 4.2. The magnitude of the seismic load is equal in all 
direction. 
&
/O = 0.023
(4.2)
 
Load combination SEI-1 and SEI-2 as defined in table 3.34 is used to calculate the seismic load. In table 4.3 
the seismic load acting on each diaphragm is listed. In the table the diaphragm referred to as “Roof” is the 
diaphragm located above the 8th floor. Same approach is used for the other diaphragms. The calculation 
and distribution of the seismic load on the diaphragms is calculated in appendix K.

4.4.3. Geometric imperfections 
When verifying the stability of a structure potential geometric imperfections in geometry and location of 
loads should be considered. This is considered by adding a specific part of the associated vertical load, 
called the geometric imperfection angle, to the critical horizontal load. The magnitude of the geometric 
imperfection angle is calculated in section 3.6.6 and rewritten in equation 4.3. 
› = 0.0021
(4.3)
 
The associated vertical load used for determination of the contribution from the geometric imperfections 
consist of all load acting on and above the diaphragm. In contrast to the seismic load, all vertical loads from 
walls and similar elements located on the floor above the concerned diaphragm contribute to the 
horizontal load from geometric imperfections. The contribution from geometric imperfections is added to 
the critical of the wind or seismic load, and therefore the magnitude is calculated for all four relevant load 
combination: STR-8, STR-9, SEI-1, and SEI-2. The load combinations is defined in table 3.34. In table 4.4 the 
contribution from geometric imperfections acting on each diaphragm is listed. The calculation and 
distribution of the geometric imperfections on the diaphragms is calculated in appendix L.

4.4.4. Critical horizontal load 
The critical horizontal design load acting on the horizontal diaphragms is either wind load with a 
contribution from geometrical imperfections, or seismic load with a contribution from geometrical 
imperfections. The critical horizontal load is not necessarily the same in both main direction since the 
vertical wind affected façade area or the magnitude of the wind surface load could be different. In this case 
the wind affected area is similar in all both direction, but the magnitude of the wind load is larger from 
west compared to the other directions. The magnitude of the seismic load and the contribution from 
geometrical imperfections is equal in all direction. 
The critical horizontal load is investigated both when the vertical load is at its maximal value and at its 
minimal value. In the case of maximal vertical load, the load combinations STR-8 and SEI-1 is compared, 
while the load combination STR-9 and SEI-1 is compared in the case of minimal vertical load. When 
comparing the horizontal forces from wind load and seismic load, the stabilizing vertical load is important 
to consider when deciding the most critical. For the wind load (STR-8 and STR-9) the stabilizing vertical load 
is equal to 0.9*Gk, while it is equal to 1.0*Gk for the seismic load. This means that the horizontal force from 
the wind load is the critical if it is larger than 90 percent of the horizontal force from seismic load. If this is 
not the case, the horizontal load from seismic load is the most critical and should be used when verifying 
the stability of the structure. Before comparing the wind load and seismic load, the contribution from 
geometric imperfections should be added. In table 4.5, 4.6, 4.7 and 4.8 the horizontal loads on the 
diaphragms is compared and the critical load cases is determined.

As stated in table 4.5, 4.6, 4.7 and 4.8 the critical horizontal load is wind load (STR-8 or STR-9) with a 
contribution from geometrical imperfections for all cases except for the 8th floor diaphragm (Diaphragm 
above 7th floor) with wind from north. This is caused by a decreased wind affected facade area for this 
diaphragm due to two-story high façade walls redistributing the forces. Since the wind force from south 
and east is equal to the wind force from north, the same would be the case for these directions. 
To simplify the calculations this deviation in the critical load case is neglected, and wind load is used as the 
dimensioning horizontal load for the whole building (marked with bold text in the tables). This assumption 
is of a limited significance since the 8th floor diaphragm is the one that receive the horizontal loads with the 
smallest magnitude. The investigation of the critical load cases is attached in appendix M.

4.4.5. Distribution of horizontal load to the stabilizing walls 
Different methods can be used when distributing the load from the horizontal diaphragms to the vertical 
shear walls. To assure a stable building at least three shear walls must be present. At least one of these 
walls must not be parallel to the other walls and must neither have a common intersection. If precisely 
three shear walls is included in the bracing system the system is categorized as statically undetermined, 
and the forces can be distributed by use of the equilibrium equations. If more than three shear walls is 
present the system is categorized as statically undetermined. By arranging the shear walls with a 
summarized center of gravity close to the center of the diaphragm the most suitable bracing system is 
designed. This limits the eccentricity of the horizontal force resultant, thus decreasing the unfavorable 
twisting forces. The arrangement of the stabilizing shear walls included in the bracing system is illustrated 
on figure 4.12. A total of six shear walls is included in the bracing system, thus the system is categorized as 
statically undetermined, and the loads can be distributed in multiple ways. 
 
Figure 4.12 - Stabilizing shear wall included in the bracing system. 
==PAGE:88==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
76 
 
Only interior walls that are continuous from terrain to roof is included in the stabilizing system. 
Furthermore the parts of the interior walls that include openings is omitted. Wall WIY1 include a small 
opening for a waste shaft on each floor. These openings is neglected in the calculation since it is relatively 
small and located in in the center of the wall where the significance is very limited. On the 7th and 8th floor 
wall WIY1 additionally includes a door opening. These openings could have influence on the capacity of the 
shear wall. This influence have not been investigated as part as this problem since it is assumed to have 
miner importance. However it is assumed that the pressure zone of the wall does not include the openings. 
For further work, the investigation of the influence of the openings on the capacity should be included. 
Two common methods for distribution of horizontal forces to the stabilizing shear walls is by using the 
elasticity theory and either assuming an infinite stiff horizontal diaphragm or infinite stiff shear walls. By 
assuming an infinite stiff horizontal diaphragm the horizontal forces is distributed to the shear walls 
according to their respective stiffness. By assuming infinite stiff shear walls, the horizontal forces are 
distributed solely by the stiffness of the horizontal diaphragm. The second method often mean that the 
stiffest walls with the highest capacity won’t receive the largest share of the forces, since the horizontal 
diaphragm can’t distribute the forces efficiently. To be able to distribute the horizontal forces effectively to 
the stiffest shear walls it is instead assumed that the horizontal diaphragms is infinite stiff. 
In [Wallner-Novak, 2014] it is recommended to calculate the stiffness of cross-laminated timber walls 
anchored with tie rods and shear brackets as stated in equation 4.4. The stiffness of the walls perpendicular 
to its length is assumed to be 0, thus the walls can only resist forces parallel to their length.

To simplify the calculations, Irregular incident wind flows have not been investigated. 
 
According to [Wallner-Novak, 2014] the effect from irregular wind flows can be considered 
by adding an eccentricity of ² = ±0.1 ∗- to the resulting horizontal force acting on the 
horizontal diaphragm. By adding an eccentricity to the force the twisting moment increase, 
thus the resulting forces on the shear walls either increase or decrease depending on the 
location of the wall compared to the direction of the moment. Further work could include 
investigating the effect on the distributed forces when adding the eccentricity.

listed in table 4.10 and 4.11 supports this conclusion. Three of the four walls that receive the largest forces 
is located parallel to the x-direction. The stability of the stabilizing shear walls is investigated in section 5.

4.4.6. Internal forces in horizontal diaphragms 
To ensure that the forces investigated in section 4.4.5 can be distributed to the stabilizing shear walls, it 
need to be verified that the assumption of an infinite stiff horizontal diaphragm is valid. This can be 
achieved by calculating the internal forces in the diaphragm, and then ensure that the connections and slab 
elements have sufficient capacity to resist these. According to [Wallner-Novak, 2014] the bending of the 
horizontal girder elements in the diaphragms is normally not relevant for calculation, due to small forces 
compared to the capacity of the element. Due to this the capacity of the horizontal girders is not 
investigated. The capacity of the connections in the horizontal diaphragms is investigated in section 7. 
The staircase in the common access route centrally located in the building introduces a large continuous 
opening in the horizontal diaphragms on all floors except the roof. To consider the influence of this 
opening, the diaphragms is divided into four horizontal beams, where of two is activated for each main 
direction of horizontal forces. The diaphragm beams is illustrated on figure 4.13 and 4.14. The diaphragm 
above the 6th floor is used as an example since it is affected by the largest loads. The influence from the 
small openings for ducts and similar installations is assumed insignificant. 
To simplify the calculation the walls perpendicular to the force direction is neglected, and the walls parallel 
to the force direction is assumed located in the same line. The assumed location of the reactions from the 
walls is illustrated on figure 4.13 and 4.14. To define the percentage of the horizontal load, which is resisted 
by each of the two diaphragm beams, the width of the beams and the location of the stabilizing shear walls 
is used. The percentage of exterior load is equal to the assumed percentage of the reactions that is taken 
by the supports for the concerned beam. For wall WIX1, WIX3, WIY1 and WIY3 the percentage of the 
reaction taken by each beam is decided by the percentage of the diaphragm depth used as height for each 
beam. The area of the diaphragm used for this estimation is the area located between the concerned wall 
and the façade, e.g. the area in figure 4.13 bordered by module line 1, 2, D and E for wall WIX3. This way of 
dividing the reaction for these four walls is chosen since they all are in contact with both diaphragm beams. 
Wall WIX2 and WIY2 is only located in one of the diaphragm beams, thus the full reaction is assumed taken 
by that beams, e.g. beam A for wall WIX2 on figure 4.13 and beam B for wall WIY2 on figure 4.14. When the 
distribution of reactions is determined the percentage of the external horizontal force can be calculated. In 
table 4.12 the distribution illustrated on figure 4.13 and 4.14 is summarized.

Since the shear walls perpendicular to the load direction is neglected, equilibrium in term of the moment

5. 
Stability 
It should be verified that the stabilizing system of the structure have the sufficient capacity to resist the 
horizontal forces acting on the building. In this chapter it is outlined how the forces determined in chapter 
6 are distributed through the structure to the ground. As described in section 3.2.1 the structural system is 
made as a plate system consisting of horizontal floor and roof diaphragms, as well as vertical stabilizing 
shear walls. Both horizontal diaphragms and vertical shear walls is made by solid cross-laminated timber 
elements and is the main parts of the stabilizing system. The internal forces in the horizontal diaphragms is 
investigated in section 4.4.6, and these forces are used to design the diaphragm connections in chapter 7. 
Besides the connections, no further investigations of the horizontal diaphragms will be done. The 
verification of the stabilizing vertical shear walls is performed in section 5.1. 
For the analysis of the stabilizing system vertical upward wind load in zone G is used for the whole surface 
of the roof. This is assumed to be suitable, since the more critical zone F is located in the roof corners only, 
and thus distribute most of the forces to the exterior non-stabilizing walls. See figure 3.19 for illustration of 
the wind load zones on the roof.    
 
5.1. 
Stabilizing walls 
The location of the stabilizing shear walls is illustrated on figure 4.12, and the horizontal forces is 
distributed to the walls as described in section 4.4.5. Wind load with a contribution from geometric 
imperfections is determined as the critical horizontal load case as defined in section 4.4.4. 
When the horizontal load is distributed by the horizontal diaphragms to the shear walls, the force 
accumulate floor-wise through the building, and can be considered in an isolated fashion for each floor. 
When using continuous stabilizing walls the force increase progressively down the floors, with the walls on 
the lowest floor resisting the largest forces. The stabilizing walls are both affected by destabilizing 
horizontal forces and stabilizing vertical forces. Like the horizontal forces the vertical forces also 
accumulate through the building. In the following sections the shear walls are investigated for overturning, 
compression, sliding and deformation. The vertical force can both be favorable and unfavorable depending 
on the property investigated. To account for this the stabilizing walls are verified for both load combination 
STR-8 and STR-9 in the ultimate limit state according to table 3.34. When verifying for deformations in the 
serviceability limit state, load combination SLS-3 according to table 3.35 is used. Wall WIX1, WIX2 and WIX3 
is investigated for horizontal load from west since this is the dominant wind direction. Wall WIY1, WIY2 and 
WIY3 is investigated for horizontal load from either north (WIY1, WIY3) or south (WIY2), depending on the 
most unfavorable distribution of the vertical loads. The calculation of the stability of the shear walls is 
attached in appendix Q.1-6. The load accumulation through a continuous stabilizing wall is illustrated in 
figure 5.1. 
==PAGE:98==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
86 
 
 
Figure 5.1 - Accumulated forces in stabilizing shear wall. 
 
 
==PAGE:99==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
87 
 
5.1.1. Overturning 
When the stabilizing shear walls is exposed to horizontal forces an overturning moment is created at the 
bottom edge of the wall on each floor. The moment is accumulated along the height, resulting in the 
largest overturning moment at the bottom edge of the wall on the ground floor. For most of the stabilizing 
walls the stabilizing vertical force is not uniform distributed along the length, due to variations in slab spans 
and presence of bathroom cabins. The non-uniform distribution introduces an additional moment, due to 
an eccentricity of the resultant of the vertical force compared to the geometric center of the stabilizing 
wall. By considering the possible non-uniform distribution of the vertical load, the overturning moment can 
be calculated by use of equation 5.1. 
.´ = . + / ∗Ra −®/2S
(5.1)
Where: 
.´:  
Overturning moment [kNm]. 
.:  
Accumulated moment from the horizontal force [kNm]. 
/:  
Vertical force [kN]. 
a:  
Location of the resultant of the vertical force measured from the tension edge [m]. 
®: 
The length of the stabilizing wall [m]. See table 4.9. 
By use of static equivalence it can be investigated whether the vertical load is enough to stabilize the wall 
against overturning. This is done with use of equation 5.2 by ensuring that the eccentricity of the vertical 
load, due to the force from the overturning moment, does not exceed ®/2. If this limit is exceeded the 
vertical load fall outside the wall, which result in an unstable element. The principle is illustrated in figure 
5.2. 
²1 = .´// ≤®/2
(5.2)
 
 
Figure 5.2 - Principle of static equivalence. 
Even though stability is ensured by static equivalence, tension anchoring may still be applied to increase the 
compression zone and avoid compressive failure. This topic is investigated in section 5.1.2. In table 5.1 it is 
listed for which stabilizing walls that tension anchoring is applied, and of what reason.

The letters in table 5.1 indicates the reason of why tension anchoring is applied: 
A: 
The vertical load is not enough to stabilize the wall on the concerned floor, and therefore the 
limit specified in equation 5.2 is exceeded. 
B: 
The vertical load is enough to stabilize the wall on the concerned floor, but at least one floor 
segment located above require tension anchoring. This is due to upward wind suction on the 
roof. The tension anchoring must be continuous to allow the distribution of the tension force 
to the foundations. 
C: 
The vertical load is enough to stabilize the wall on the concerned floor, but tension 
anchoring is applied to increase the length of the compression zone, and thus avoid 
compressive failure. 
D: 
The vertical load is enough to stabilize the wall on the concerned floor and tension anchoring 
is not required.

strength is calculated by use of equation 5.3. The principle for the calculation is illustrated in figure 5.3.

If tension anchoring is not applied equation 5.3 with a´ = 0 % and ® =  ® −2 ∗²1 can be used to 
calculate the reaction at the edge of the wall, where the horizontal force is acting. In that case the reaction 
´ would be a compressive force. 
For stabilizing walls where tension anchoring is necessary, the required tensile force is increasing towards 
the ground. A pair of tension rods is used as tension anchoring. The tension rods is placed in both ends of 
the walls, to stabilize for moment forces in both directions. Tension rods of dimensions 2xØ10, 2xØ12, 
2xØ14, 2xØ16, 2xØ20, 2xØ25, 2xØ28, 2xØ32, 2xØ40 are used depending on the required tensile capacity. 
Reduction couplers for tension bars by the manufacturer peikko, is used to reduce the dimensions between 
floors if possible, to reduce the use of steel. It is only possible to reduce the tension rods by one size for 
each set of reduction couplers, and it is assumed feasible to use a maximal of one set of reduction couplers 
per floor, if the forces permits it. 
When verifying the capacity of the tension rods the following criteria should be met: –- ≥ –, where –- 
is the design tensile capacity of the tension rods and – = ´ is the design tensile force the tension rods 
should be able to resist. The tension rods are further investigated in chapter 7. In table 5.2 the design 
tensile force in the tension anchoring for load combination STR-9 is listed. STR-9 is the critical load 
combination when verifying for overturning since the lowest stabilizing vertical force is present in that 
combination. The chosen dimensions of tension rods is listed in table 5.3, and the location of these is 
illustrated in figure 5.4. See appendix Q.1-6 for calculations.

As it can be seen in table 5.2 and 5.3 the structure is obvious most sensitive to horizontal forces in the x-
direction. This is because the total length of the shear walls, and therefore the total stiffness of the shear 
wall system in the x-direction is relatively small, while the dominant wind direction from west mainly 
affects these walls. For the walls in the y-direction mainly affected by horizontal forces from north and 
south, only the shortest wall WIY1 require tension anchoring on the lowest four floors. The length of the 
shear walls is an important property for the stabilizing capacity of the shear walls, which is why the shortest 
walls is the most critical in the case of overturning. By using another method for calculating the relative 
stiffness of the shear walls, a larger part of the horizontal force could be distributed to the longer walls. 
Due to limitations of the project period, other methods for calculation of the relative stiffnesses haven’t 
been assessed.

5.1.2. Compression 
Due to horizontal forces the resulting compression force under the wall is moved to the opposite edge of 
where the forces is acting. This means that the compression zone is decreased, and the compression stress 
increases. The compressive reaction  is calculated by use of equation 5.4. For description of the symbols 
and the selected values of the different lengths, see equation 5.3 in section 5.1.2. The principle of the 
calculation is illustrated in figure 5.3. 
 = .
² + / ∗a −a´
²
 
(5.4) 
 
The most common way of transferring load from a wall on one floor to the wall located on the floor below 
is by load transfer through the slabs in the horizontal diaphragms. This means that the load is transferred as 
compression at an angle of 90 degrees relative to the fibers in the slabs. The compression strength 
perpendicular to the fibers is significantly lower than the strength parallel to the fibers, as listed in table 3.9 
in section 3.5.1.4.1. This difference in the strength makes timber elements very sensitive to compression 
perpendicular to the fibers. The compression capacity perpendicular to the fibers in the slabs is calculated 
by use of equation 5.5.

As it can be seen in table 5.7 compressive failure happens under many of the shear walls on the lower 
floors. The failure occurs even through the length of the compression zone of many of the concerned walls 
is already been increased. To solve the problem another method for transfer of load from floor to floor is 
used for the 1st floor to the 6th floor. A toothed joint is instead used, where the walls is supported directly 
on the walls below for approximately half the length. The remaining length is used for support of the slabs 
on the walls. This means that the compressive force is transferred from wall to wall by compression parallel 
to the fibers in the vertical layers of the walls, while compression perpendicular to the fibers in the slabs is 
completely avoided. The joint is made by teeth with a width of 250 mm. To ensure that at least half the 
length of the compression zone consist of direct contact between the walls, the utmost tooth in both ends 
is a wall tooth. The geometry of the joint is illustrated in figure 5.8. 
 
Figure 5.8 - Geometry of toothed joint. 
The compression strength parallel to the fibers is significantly larger than the compression strength 
perpendicular to the fibers. By using a toothed joint a more optimal use of the strength properties is 
achieved, even though only the front face of the vertical layers inside the teeth can be used for the contact 
area. The compression capacity parallel to the fibers in the walls is calculated by use of equation 5.8. 
N,2,- = ,2,,| ∗£2,

1st to the 6th floor, since the utilization rates does not exceed 0.50. The utilization rate for the ground floor 
exceeds 0.50, but since the wall on this floor as supported on concrete in the full length, failure is avoided 
as long as the utilization rate does not exceed 1.00. All calculations can be found in appendix Q.1-6. 
 
5.1.3. Sliding 
It should be ensured that sliding in the horizontal joints between the shear wall and the horizontal 
diaphragms is avoided. This is ensured by meeting the criteria given in equation 5.11.

According to [Wallner-Novak, 2014] the recommended value for the friction coefficients for sliding of 
timber-timber is ^ = 0.4. The friction coefficient for sliding of timber-concrete is likewise ^ = 0.4. However 
a considerable uncertainty is associated with the calculation of the friction resistance, especially when 
sound insulating mats or films is used to prevent flank sounds from distributing through the joints. 
Conservatively a friction coefficient of ^ = 0.0 is used for both timber-timber and timber-concrete joints. 
This means that the total shear force should be resisted by another method than friction.  
Steel brackets is used to resist the shear force at both the top and bottom edge of the shear walls. The steel 
brackets is designed in chapter 7. From equation 5.11 it is obvious that the most steel brackets are required 
at the bottom walls, since the force accumulated through the structure. In table 5.10 the design shear 
force • acting on each wall for load combination STR-8 are listed. STR-8 is the critical load combination, 
when investigating the sliding force, due to the larger contribution from geometrical imperfection. In table

calculation of the sliding forces can be found in appendix Q.1-6.

5.1.4. Deformation 
The deformation of the stabilizing shear walls is assessed in the serviceability limit state by use of load 
combination SLS-3. The horizontal deformation consists of contributions from deformation of the cross-
laminated timber plate and contributions from displacements of the fasteners. The deformation of the 
plate depends on the stresses from shear and bending, while the displacements of the fasteners depends 
on sliding of the steel brackets and expansion of the tension rods. Since the cross-laminated timber plates 
got a relatively high stiffness, the dominant portion of the deformations is due to the fasteners [Wallner-
Novak, 2014]. 
The equations specified in section 10.5.3 of [Wallner-Novak, 2014] is used to determine the deformation of 
the stabilizing shear walls.

6. 
Design of structural elements 
In this chapter the structural elements are designed according to the ultimate limit states (ULS) and the 
serviceability limit states (SLS) described in section 3.4. In the ultimate limit states both the persistent 
design situation and the fire scenarios in the accidental design situation is assessed. In the serviceability 
limit states both deformations and vibrations is considered. All calculations are according to the relevant 
Eurocodes listed in section 3.3.1. The structure is mainly constructed by use of cross-laminated timber 
elements, why these will be the main focus of this chapter. CLT is not yet incorporated in Eurocode 5, and 
therefore the theory included in the design guidelines listed in section 3.3.2 is used for the design. The 
theory in these design guidelines are very similar with only few differences. If nothing else is specified the 
theory origins from [Wallner-Novak, 2014]. 
 
6.1. 
Basis for calculation 
Cross-laminated timber elements are able to resist and distribute forces in all three main directions. These 
are referred to according to the global axes and the direction of the board layers in the following matter: 
x-axis 
 Global axis parallel to the direction of the fibers in the top layers, also referred 
to as the longitudinal layers. Often the main direction of the load bearing 
capacity. 
y-axis 
 Global axis perpendicular to the direction of the fibers in the top layers. This 
corresponds to the direction of the transverse layers. Often the ancillary 
direction of the load bearing capacity. 
z-axis 
 Global axis perpendicular to the xy-plane. The axis follows the thickness of the 
element. 
Global 0o-direction 
Direction parallel with the fibers of the top layers, also referred to as the 
longitudinal layers. This global 0o-direction equals to the global x-axis. 
[Wallner-Novak, 2014] utilizes this naming scheme. 
Global 90o-direction 
Direction perpendicular to the direction of the fibers in the top layers, also 
referred to as the transverse. This global 90o-direction equals to the global y-
axis. [Wallner-Novak, 2014] utilizes this naming scheme. 
Local 90o-direction 
Local axes parallel to the direction of the fibers in the individual layers.  
Local 90o-direction 
Local axes perpendicular to the direction of the fibers in the individual layers.  
The definitions of the global x-, y-, and z-axes and the associated forces is illustrated in figure 6.1, while the 
cross-sectional dimensions is illustrated in figure 6.2. The naming is chosen to match the naming scheme 
used in [Wallner-Novak, 2014].  
 
Figure 6.1 - Definition of the x-, y-, and z-axes and the associated forces.

Only cross-laminated timber elements consisting of five board layers arranged with right angles to one 
another is used for the project. If one board layer have a thickness of more than 40 mm, it is made of two 
layers of lamellas arranged parallel to one another. Since the two lamellas is arranged parallel to one 
another they are still calculated as one board layer. This means that all cross-laminated timber elements 
used for the project consist of three longitudinal layers and two transverse layers. As illustrated in figure 
6.2 the longitudinal layers are named 1, 2 and 3, while the transverse layers are named 1,2 and 2,3. 
For all elements included in the project a dominating direction of load distribution is present, which means 
that the elements can be threaten as panel strips, normally with a width of one meter. Then the elements 
can be treated as uniaxial stressed beams. In that case the transverse layers are not assigned any 
longitudinal stresses, and the modulus of elasticity perpendicular to the span direction is assumed ¶2 = 0 
[Wallner-Novak, 2014]. 
 
6.2. 
Cross-sectional values for cross-laminated timber 
For verification in the ultimate limit states, net cross-sectional values without considering shear flexibility 
may be used. The net cross-sectional values are described in section 6.2.1. For analysis of the serviceability 
limit states the slip between the board layers should be considered. Effective cross-sectional values 
considering the shear flexibility should then be used. The gamma method included in annex B of [DS/EN 
1995-1-1, 2007] is applied. The effective cross-sectional values are described in section 6.2.2. Both the net-
cross-sectional values and the effective cross-sectional values consider layers with different modulus of 
elasticity by including the ratio / in the equations, where  is the modulus of elasticity of the 
individual layer, and  is the chosen reference modulus of elasticity. Since only timber layers of strength 
class C24 is used for the cross-laminated timber elements this ratio is /  = 1 and therefore ignored. 
==PAGE:115==
Andreas Juul Larsen

27-06-2020

6.2.1. Net cross-sectional values 
Since a dominant direction of load distribution is present for all cross-laminated timber elements, net cross-
sectional values for the longitudinal layers is presented. However the cross-sectional values for the 
transverse layers are determined analogously. When considering the transverse layers the outer 
longitudinal layers is not considered, which means that an element with five board layers can be considered 
as an element with three board layers. When considering the stresses in the direction of the longitudinal 
layers, the transverse layers act as spacers only subjected to shear [Wallner-Novak, 2014]. The distribution 
of normal and shear stresses along the thickness of a five layered element stressed in the longitudinal 
direction is illustrated in figure 6.2. As illustrated the transverse layers is only subjected to shear stresses 
constant along the thickness of the layer. 
 
Figure 6.3 - Distribution of normal and shear stresses along the thickness of a five layered element stresses in the longitudinal 
direction [Wallner-Novak, 2014].

6.2.2. Effective cross-sectional values 
When analyzing in the serviceability limit states the shear deformations of the transverse layers must be 
considered as a part of the overall deformation. A simple way of doing this is by applying the dowelled 
beam model. In the model the longitudinal layers are considered as parts of the beams cross-section, while 
the transverse layers are considered as laminar doweling. As illustrated in figure 6.4 the overall 
deformation then consist of a bending portion from tension of the cross-section and a shear portion from 
deformation of the transverse layers. The portion from the shear deformations typically lies below 30 
percent of the bending deformations, depending on the cross-section build-up, the load pattern, and the 
slenderness of the element. 
 
Figure 6.4 - Deformation portions of a cross-laminated timber beam from bending and shear [Wallner-Novak, 2014]. 
Varies methods intended for analysis of flexible connected flexural members exist. One of these is the 
Gamma method defined in annex B of [DS/EN 1995-1-1, 2017], which will be used for this project. The 
method is included in the general design standards, and is also described in [Wallner-Novak, 2014] and 
[Svenskt Trä, 2015]. The method is simple and built on an engineering-based approach. One advantage of 
the Gamma method is that the deformations can be calculated through pure bending, and no separate 
terms for the shear deformation have to be determined. One disadvantage is that method depends on the 
span, making it system-dependent. The shear flexibility of the transverse layers is considered by reducing 
the longitudinal layers by a factor 0 [

']. An effective moment of inertia €
 can then be calculated and used 
for verification in the serviceability limit state. This means that the shear flexibility is considered trough a 
reduced flexural stiffness [Wallner-Novak, 2014]. 
The Gamma method is developed to be used for cross-sections with three of five board layers, 
corresponding to two or the longitudinal layers. Cross-sections with seven or nine board layers can be 
analyzed by the modified Gamma method, which is a more complicated and comprehensive version. For 
both three and five layered elements the second layer from the top is theoretically retained, as illustrated 
on figure 6.5 with a thicker edge. The adjacent layers are flexible linked to the retained layer, and their 
respective Stainer portion is reduced by a Gamma value depending on the span of the element [Wallner-
Novak, 2014]. For a five layered element stressed parallel to the longitudinal layers the Gamma values are 
calculated by use of equation 6.9, 6.10 and 6.11. The Gamma values are named according to the respective 
longitudinal layer, e.g. the Gamma value belonging to the longitudinal layer named 1 in figure 6.2 is named 
0.

6.3. 
Verification of structural elements of timber 
In this section the theory behind verification of structural elements of timber is described. Since major 
parts of the structural elements is made by cross-laminated timber elements this will be the focus. 
However when relevant it is briefly described how the theory is used for timber elements of solid timber, 
glued-laminated timber, and laminated veneer lumber.  
The elements are designed according to the design limit states described in section 3.4. When designing 
according to the cold conditions, which includes all design situation excluding the fire situation the original 
or initial cross-section is used. When designing for the ultimate limit states of the accidental design 
situation in the event of fire, also described as the hot condition, a residual cross-section is used. The 
residual cross-section is formed due to charring of the material exposed to hot temperatures. Different 
design methods based on different fire curves can be used to determine the residual cross-section. In this 
project the timber elements is verified against two fire situations. The first is the standard fire curve 
defined in section 3.2.1 of [DS/EN 1991-1-2, 2007], and the second is the parametric fire curve defined in 
annex A of [DS/EN 1991-1-2 DK NA, 2014]. For the standard fire curve the reduced cross-section design 
method is used, while the reduced properties design method is used for the parametric fire curve. The 
major difference of the two design methods is how they handle the non-charred but weakened layer of 
material located at the charred edge of the element. The methods is illustrated on figure 6.6 and further 
described in chapter 11, where the design for the fire situation is addressed. 
 
Figure 6.6 - Design methods for the fire situation [Wallner-Novak, 2014].

respectively.

6.3.1.3. Tension transverse to the element plane 
The tension strength perpendicular to the fibers is very low, and connections with tensile forces transverse 
to the element plane should be carefully observed. In case of small forces fully threaded screws, which are 
screwed into the entire thickness of the element can be used. For larger forces, a connection method 
where the load is applied as compression on the opposite site of the tensile force is more suitable [Wallner-
Novak, 2014].

Timber shows an elastic-plastic performance when subjected to compression stresses perpendicular to the 
direction of the fibers. When stressed above the elastic limit the cells of the timber is compressed and 
plastically deformed. From compression tests with a cuboid specimen with unidirectional layup, ,¶2 
coefficients for rod-shaped elements have been derived, while an increase of the contact length on each 
side of 30 mm have determined [Wallner-Novak, 2018]. Due to the interlocking effect of the cross layers in 
cross-laminated timber the transverse strain in the timber is impeded by a higher extensional stiffness, as 
illustrated on figure 6.8. This indicates that the deformations and tensile stresses perpendicular to the 
direction of the fibers is reduced. This have a favorable effect on the compressive strength perpendicular to 
the direction of the fibers in cross-laminated timber. It allows the characteristic compressive strength 
perpendicular to the direction of the fibers to be increased to the 1.2-fold value of the strength used for 
glued-laminated timber. For cross-laminated timber of the C24 strength class it means that the 
characteristic strength gets the value: ,¶2, = 1.2 ∗2.5 ./& = 3.0 ./& [Wallner-Novak, 2018]. This 
value is used for the project and listed in table 3.9. 
 
Figure 6.9 - Interlocking effect. Left: Cross layers. Right: Unidirectional layup. [Wallner-Novak, 2018]

6.3.1.9. Shear upon stressing as a plate 
Upon stressed as a plate both the capacity of the entire element and the board layers should be 
considered. The capacity depends on whether boards are glued at the edges and whether cracks is present. 
The theory in [Wallner-Novak, 2014] and [Svenskt Trä, 2017] is based on a simplified method, that are used 
in the current product approvals. The simplified method is adequate for elements with board layers 
approximately the same thickness. Other methods are developed that considers stresses more precisely for 
elements with very different layer thicknesses [Wallner-Novak, 2014]. In this product the focus of plate 
stresses is mainly on the shear walls that consist of board layers with equal thickness, and therefore the 
simplified method is assumed sufficient, and will be used for design. A total of three failure mechanism is 
investigated.  
 
6.3.1.9.1. 
Mechanism 1: Shearing off failure of the boards along a joint 
Mechanism 1 considers the shear failure of the boards if these are not edge glued. The boards of the layers 
in the direction perpendicular to the boards of the concerned layers works as reinforcement, which is why 
the strength value ,,“, is used instead of the normal shear strength of the individual boards ,,. The same 
applies for elements stressed as an upright girder in section 6.3.1.8. The failure occur perpendicular to the 
fibers in either the longitudinal layers or the transverse layers. The criteria in equation 6.42 must be 
complied with. See figure 6.13 for illustration.

Failure may occur due to mechanism 1 or 2 if the boards are edge glued or cracks are present, while failure 
may occur due to mechanism 3 if the boards are not edge glued and no cracks are present. The designer 
should be cautious before ignoring mechanism 1 and 2 since it is difficult to ensure that cracks won’t 
develop. Mechanism 3 can be ignored if edge gluing are not used, since it often gives the highest strength 
values.

6.3.1.10. Buckling 
For members like walls and columns subjected to axial compression the risk of instability due to buckling 
from the element plane should be considered. Buckling is considered by the linear buckling theory 
described in [DS/EN 1995-1-1, 2007]. The second order effects is considered by introduction of a buckling 
coefficient , which is a function of the quality of manufacturing, the relative slenderness, and the shape 
of the cross-section. Buckling will occur about the axis with the highest slenderness, which in case of walls 
supported over their entire length, is transverse to the element plane around the y axis. In this project all 
walls is designed with the longitudinal layers in the vertical direction, and therefore the theory is described 
from that view. The buckling coefficient  for members subjected to axial compression is calculated by use 
of equation 6.45, 6.46, 6.47, 6.48 and 6.49.

6.3.1.11. Lateral torsional buckling 
High and slender beams may fail by a combination of twisting and horizontal deformation, even though 
they are only subjected to vertical load. This is called lateral torsional buckling, and it can be defined as free 
and fixed. If the compression edge of the beam can deflect freely it is defined as free lateral torsional 
buckling, and if the compression edge is fixed by e.g. a roof structure it is defined as fixed lateral torsional 
buckling. The fixed type is in practice not a problem [Munch-Andersen, 2016]. In this section the theory of 
designing for free lateral torsional buckling is assessed according to [DS/EN 1995-1-1, 2007]. No cross-
laminated timber beams is exposed to free lateral torsional buckling in this project, thus this section is 
addressed for unidirectional timber elements like solid timber, glued-laminated timber and LVL. The effect 
from lateral torsional buckling is assessed by applying a lateral torsional buckling coefficient , which is 
calculated by use of equation 6.51, 6.52, 6.53 and 6.54.

6.3.1.12. Combined bending and compression 
For members subjected to a combination of bending and compression is should be ensured that the 
capacity of the member is sufficient to obtain the combined forces. If there is not risk of buckling the 
criteria in equation 6.57 should be complied with, while equation 6.58 should be complied with if risk of 
buckling is present. Both equation is directed for stress in the direction of the longitudinal fibers, but the 
criteria for the transverse layers is similar. In figure 6.17 the case of members subjected to a combination of 
bending and compression with the risk of buckling is illustrated.

6.3.2. Serviceability limit state (SLS) 
In this section the topics listed in table 6.2 will be described. The verification method, strength properties 
and cross-sectional values are according to section 3.4.1, section 3.5.1, and section 6.2, respectively.

For the theory of horizontal deformations of shear walls reference is made to section 5.1.4. Deformations 
of cross-laminated timber panel is calculated by equation 6.59 according to the Euler-Bernoulli theory and 
the Gamma method described in section 6.2.2 [Svenskt Trä, 2017].

6.3.2.2. Vibrations 
As described in section 3.4.2.2 the vibration properties of panels are verified by fulfilling two criteria in 
terms of: 
• 
The first natural frequency. 
• 
The stiffness of the panel, defined as the deflection of a unit load of 1 kN. 
If the criteria in terms of the first natural frequency can’t be met, a criteria in terms of limit acceleration can 
be applied to ensure satisfactory function. The limit values of the criteria is depending on the associated 
vibrations class as listed in table 3.7. 
 
6.3.2.2.1. 
Criteria in terms of the first natural frequency 
The first natural frequency of cross-laminated timber panel is calculated by use of equation 6.63 [Wallner-
Novak, 2014].

that the stiffness of the transverse layers could be considered when verifying the vibration properties. For 
this to be possible the connections between the individual slab elements parallel to the span direction 
should be designed to resist the forces introduced by the transverse distribution. These forces have been 
considered in chapter 7. The coefficient for considering the influence of the transverse load-bearing effect 
is calculated by use of equation 6.64 [Wallner-Novak, 2014].

According to [Wallner-Novak, 2018] the flexural stiffness of screeds may be considered in the calculation, 
generally without statement of the compound effect. The statement is given as an extract from the 
Austrian national annex to Eurocode 5 ÖNORM B 1995-1-1:2015 and apply if the properties and 
requirements listed in the applicable standards is respected. Based on this the effect from including the 
stiffness of screeds in the flexural stiffness of the cross-laminated timber panels is investigated, without 
considering the interaction between the two materials. For further work, not limited by the project period, 
the interaction between the materials should be investigated, together with an in depth analysis of the 
transverse load-bearing effect. The flexural stiffness in the longitudinal and the transverse direction with 
contribution from the screed is calculated by use of equation 6.65 and 6.66, respectively.

6.4. 
Slabs 
In this section the slab elements is designed and verified according to load-bearing capacity and stiffness. 
Cross-laminated timber is used for the load-bearing part of all floor, roof, and balcony structures. All slabs 
are single-span elements, simple supported at both ends. In the section both panels used for floor slabs, 
roof slabs, and balcony slabs is designed. 
6.4.1. Critical slab elements 
The most critical slabs is the ones with the longest spans and/or the one with the largest load impacts. In 
case of the floor slabs the location of the heavy prefabricated bathroom is an important factor for the 
required element size. The critical slabs that have been calculated is listed in table 6.3. Regarding the floor 
slabs five different zones have been verified, since  the location of pre-fabricated bathroom cabin varies, 
and therefore the span length, can’t be used as the only factor for determining the critical slabs. This also 
gives the possibility to use different elements sizes or strength classes, depending on the load impact and 
section forces. Regarding the roof and balcony slabs only the one slab zone with the longest span is 
calculated since the load is uniformly distributed, and therefore the length of the span is the only relevant 
factor when determining the critical slabs. In figure 6.20 the location of the critical slabs is indicated with a 
grey color, possibly surrounded with a red border. The figure also shows the span length and slab zone 
widths of the critical slabs. Only the location and sizes of the critical floor and balcony slabs is illustrated, 
but the critical roof slab zone ZRS1a is equal to the floor slab zone ZFS1a regarding the length of the span 
and the slab zone width. 
Table 6.3 - Critical slabs.

As illustrated on figure 6.20 the slabs include openings for ducts and installations. [Wallner-Novak, 2018] 
include theory of how to investigate the load-bearing capacity when considering the openings, by using 
imaginary beams both in the longitudinal and transverse direction around the openings. Due to limitations 
of the project period this have not been investigated. For further work this should be included. Instead it is 
assumed that the cross-laminated timber panels have the sufficient load-bearing capacity and stiffness in 
the transverse direction to redistribute the forces around the openings. Since the maximal width of the 
openings is only 800 mm, the size is assumed to be relatively small. 
As previously described the floor and roof slabs is fully supported by aluminium connectors on the inside of 
the load-bearing external walls. At the load-bearing walls the floor and roof slabs supported by toothed 
joints on top of the walls. As described in section 5.1.2, this means that the slabs is only supported in 
approximately half their width. This reduces the load-bearing capacity regarding shear and compression 
perpendicular to the direction of the fibers at the supports to 50 percent. 
==PAGE:140==

If openings in the slabs is located at the supports the support teeth is redistributed to ensure that the 
support width is preserved. An example of the geometry of a slab with an opening at a toothed support is 
illustrated in figure 6.21. Above doors the full width is used for support of the slabs since no vertical load 
from the wall is present here. The space beside the doors is then used for direct load distribution from wall 
to wall.

6.4.2. Load impact 
The slabs is subjected to loads from different sources. These sources are listed an illustrated in the 
following: 
Slab structure – Load definition nr. H1/H2/H3/H4: Global uniformly distributed line load including self-
weight from the structure and vertical imposed load, snow load and wind load acting on the structure 
depending on the type of slab.  
 
 
Figure 6.22 – Global uniformly distributed line load.

unfavorable point of the slab, which is assumed as the center of the span.

Stair in dwellings – Load definition nr. H6: Point load from light timber stairs including self-weight and 
imposed load. The point load is conservatively assumed located at the center of the span of the slabs. The 
stair in dwellings are only located on the floor slabs. The location of these is illustrated on figure 4.8 in 
section 4.1.  
 
Figure 6.24 – Point load from light timber stair in dwelling.

The slabs are calculated as strips with a width of one meter. The magnitude of the point loads should be 
referred to the one-meter strip. The stairs in the dwellings are approximately one meter wide, thus the 
magnitude of the point load by referring to the one-meter strip is unchanged. Regarding the imposed point 
load acting on the slabs it is normally assumed to have a shape of a square with a width of 50 mm [DS/EN 
1991-1-1; 2007. A simple method to refer the imposed point load to the one-meter strip is by assuming a 
load propagation angle from the slab surface to the center of gravity of between 35 and 45 degrees to the 
vertical, as illustrated on figure 6.22 [Wallner-Novak, 2014]. The magnitude of the equivalent point load 
referring to the one-meter strip is then calculated by use of equation 6.70 and 6.71. The method means 
that the equivalent value is increasing with a decreasing thickness of the slab element. [Wallner-Novak, 
2018] includes a more advanced and precise method of distributing the load from point loads. Due to 
limitations of the project period this advanced method have not been analyses. The method could be 
included for further work.

Unlike when verifying structural elements of other materials like steel or concrete, the load combination 
with the largest load is not necessarily the most critical. This is due to that the strength of timber is 
dependent of the duration of the load. Therefore it is often necessary to verify the load bearing capacity for 
several load combinations of the persistent design situations in the ultimate limit states. However is the 
load value of a load combination being lower than that for a load combination with a longer load duration, 
that combination won’t be critical. An example of this is the STR-7 load combination for both the roof slabs 
and the balcony slabs. 
 
6.4.3. Structural design 
In this section the critical slab elements is designed according to the theory described in section 6.3. In the 
ultimate limit states the design of the slab elements include verification of the load-bearing capacity of the 
cold cross-section in the persistent design situation and verification of the residual cross-section in the 
accidental design situation in case of fire. In the serviceability limit state the design of the slab elements 
include verification of vertical deformations and vibrations including analysis of the first natural frequency, 
the stiffness, and the acceleration.  
The floor slabs is assigned to vibration class I (High requirements), the balcony slabs is assigned to vibration 
class II (Medium requirements), and the roof slabs is assigned to vibration class III (No requirements), 
according to table 3.7 in section 3.4.2.2. 
Regarding vibrations in the floor slabs the following three cases are analyzed: 
Case 1: Support on two sides – Without transverse distribution effect, and without contribution from the 
stiffness of screed; y	,

 = 1.00, €

 = 0. This is the solution used for the final design. 
Case 2: Support on four sides – With transverse distribution effect, but without contribution from the 
stiffness of screed; €

 = 0. 
Case 3: Support on four sides – With transverse distribution effect, and with contribution from the stiffness 
of screed. 
In table 6.6 and 6.7 the maximal utilization rates for each analyzed limit state is listed for the critical slabs. 
The calculation of the critical slabs can be found in appendix R.1-7 is indicated in table 6.6 and 6.7.

From the results in table 6.6 and 6.7 it is obvious that the serviceability limit states is the designing limit 
states. The deformation limits is complied with for all investigated slab zones. The limit for the final 
deformations in the quasi-permanent load combination is the most critical for floor slabs except floor slab 
zone ZFS1a, where the final deformation in the characteristic load combination is the critical limit. This 
difference is because there is no prefabricated bathroom cabin located in this zone, which means that the 
influence of  the imposed load increases. The magnitude of the imposed load is reduced more in the quasi-
permanent load combination compared to the characteristic load combination, which is why this difference 
occur. The same is the case for the balcony slab, where all the variable loads is reduced more in the quasi-
permanent load combination compared to the characteristic load combination. In the case of the roof slab 
the critical deformation limit is the characteristic self-weight since the impact from snow load is reduced in 
the load combination. Even though a larger cross-section is applied for the floor slab zone ZFS4a it is still 
one of the floor slab elements with the highest utilization. This is because of long span combined with a 
very unfavorable location of the prefabricated bathroom cabin, which means that a large part of the load is 
located far away from the support. The bathroom cabins got a relatively high self-weight, which makes 
==PAGE:148==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
136 
 
their presents and location a very critical factor for the design. This is also the case when verifying the load-
bearing capacity in the ultimate limit state, thus the presents of bathroom cabins affects which load 
combination is the most critical. For floor slab zones where bathroom cabins are located, the critical load 
combination is STR-1 with dominating self-weight, while load combination STR-2 with dominating imposed 
load is the most critical for floor slab zones without bathroom cabins.  
In the ultimate limit states of the ultimate limit states the only high utilization rates is calculated for the 
rolling shear in the persistent design situation at floor slab zones where bathroom cabins are located close 
to the support. The high utilization rates is due to the toothed supports at the interior load-bearing walls, 
which decreases both the rolling shear capacity and the transverse compression strength with 50 percent. 
The compressive forces at the supports is low, thus the transverse compressive stress is no problem. For all 
other forces in the ultimate limit states the utilization rates is relatively low and is therefore not a designing 
factor. In general the utilization rates for the residual cross-section in the hot condition in case of fire is low 
due to sufficient fire protective insulation and large cross sections. The fire situation and the insulation is 
described in chapter 11. 
While the vibrations is no problem for the roof and balcony slabs due to lower requirements, the vibrations 
are a very critical factor for design of the floor slabs. The stiffness criteria is complied with for all 
investigated floor slabs, which is due to the co-effective width of slab zones, that apply due to the two-
dimensional stiffness of the cross-laminated timber elements. 
Only the floor slab zone ZFS1a with the shortest span length of the investigated floor slabs comply with the 
criteria considering the first natural frequency. The reason for this is a high distributed mass from both the 
floor structure and the bathroom cabins. An increasing distributed mass decreases the first natural 
frequency, which indicated that the screed layer, with the purpose of increasing the sound insulating 
properties of the floor structure, in terms of vibrations is unfavorable. By utilizing the transverse 
distribution properties by supporting the slab zones at all four edges (Case 2) the first natural frequency can 
be increased, but only by a little. By utilizing the stiffness of the screed layer for the total stiffness (Case 3) it 
can be increased further. The magnitude of the increase is however still very small and may be neglected. 
In all of the three cases for vibrations investigated the criteria for the first natural criteria can’t be complied 
with for all of the four longest slab zones, which means that the criteria that consider the acceleration must 
be complied with. Unless floor slab zone ZFS1a and ZFS2a with utilization rates of 1.03 and 1.02 (Case 1), 
which is assumed acceptable, all the floor slabs comply with the criteria. Like the first natural frequency, 
the utilization of the transverse distribution properties (Case 2) and the including of the stiffness from the 
screed (Case 3) improves the acceleration with some few percent. If a little improvement could turn an 
unsatisfying solution to a satisfying solution, either case 2 or case 3 could be a solution. This, however, 
requires that the slab structure and the walls around the slab zone is designed for the two-way load 
distribution, and that the composite effect between the screed and the cross-laminated timber is 
investigated and documented. Case 1 with uniaxially load distribution and no considered stiffness 
contribution from the screed is used for the final design of the floor slabs. 
Cross-sections with a thickness of 260 or 300 mm requires a lot of wood material. Since the length of the 
span is an important designing factor for the required cross-section, the maximal possible span length of a 
cross-laminated timber panel with a thickness of 200 mm, i.e. CLT 200 L5s (40l – 40w – 40l – 40w – 40l), 
have been determined. 
 
==PAGE:149==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
137 
 
The maximal possible span length have been determined for four cases equivalent to cases used for design 
in this project. The cases includes a floor slab without a bathroom cabin (ZFS1a), floor slab with a bathroom 
cabin located favorable (ZFS1b/ZFS2a/ZFS3a ), a floor slab with a bathroom cabin located unfavorable 
(ZFS4a), and a roof slab (ZRS1a). The load from the stairs in the dwellings have been ignored for this 
investigation. The maximal span length is listed in table 6.8. The calculation of the maximal span length is 
found in appendix R.8-11, as indicated in table 6.8. 
Table 6.8 - Maximal length of span with a cross-section of CLT 200 L5s.

None of the four cases in table 6.8 do not comply with the criteria of the first natural frequency. Except for 
the floor slab without bathroom cabin, all the cases comply with the criteria considering the acceleration. 
The floor slab without the bathroom cabin have a utilization rate of 1.18 regarding the acceleration criteria, 
that in this case is assumed acceptable. 
From table 6.8 it is obvious that the effect from the bathroom cabins on the possible span length is very 
critical, since an unfavorable located bathroom cabin reduces the possible span length with more than two 
meters. If just focusing on the slabs without bathroom cabins it can be argued slab spans in residential 
buildings that aims to utilize cross-laminated timber panels efficiently should be limited to a maximal of 6 
meters for floor slabs and 7 meters for roof slabs. The maximal possible span lengths of course depends on 
the specific design of the floor and roof structures used in the concerned building. 
The results in table 6.8 also indicated that some of the roof slab zones for the concerned building could be 
designed with a thickness of 200 mm. However, it is decided to design all roof slabs with a thickness of 200 
mm for simplicity.   
 
6.5. 
Walls 
In this section the load-bearing and stabilizing cross-laminated timber walls are analyzed and designed 
according to the theory in section 6.3. All walls, both interior and exterior, have a thickness of 200 mm and 
a build-up of: CLT 200 C5s (40l – 40w – 40l – 40w – 40l). All continuous walls that are included in the 
building are load-bearing since they carry the loads from the walls on the floors above. About half of these 
are also supporting the slabs in the roof and floor structures. Only the interior walls are utilized as 
stabilizing since the exterior walls includes a large number of openings for windows and doors, which 
greatly decreases the possible stabilizing capacity. In figure 6.27 the location of load-bearing and stabilizing 
walls are illustrated for the ground floor. Except wall WIX4 and WIX5 all walls are continuous throughout 
the building height. The load-bearing walls are separated in slab supporting and not slab supporting walls. 
Due to the openings for the stair in the apartments on the top floors, small areas of the floor structure 
above the 7th floor is rotated 90 degrees regarding the span direction of the cross-laminated timber panels. 
This means that some of the walls, that otherwise do not support slabs, do support small slab areas on this 
floor. This is relatively small loads and is not changing the overall definition of the walls as not slab 
supporting. All interior walls around the staircase is either supporting the concrete stair or the landing.  
==PAGE:150==
Andreas Juul Larsen

Figure 6.27 - Overview of load-bearing and stabilizing walls.

6.5.1. 
Vertical load-bearing walls 
The capacity of the vertical load-bearing walls are investigated for three different cases, due to different 
challenges regarding load impact and geometry: 
Interior walls at bathroom cabins: The interior walls that support floor slabs where the prefabricated 
bathroom cabins are located is subjected to the largest loads of all vertical load-bearing walls in the 
building. The walls are designed by calculating the capacity of a one meter wide strip of the walls subjected 
to the maximal vertical load acting on the concerned wall element. Since the largest accumulated vertical 
loads is subjected to the walls on the ground floor, this is the focus of the design. See the location of the 
investigated wall strips in figure 6.28.  
Interior walls at door openings: The parts of the interior walls located just beside the door openings is 
subjected to large concentrated vertical forces from the lintels above the doors. The walls is designed by 
calculating the load-bearing capacity of a wall strip with a width of 300 mm, which is the width beside the 
doors where direct wall to wall transfer of the vertical loads is ensured. This ensures that the concentrated 
loads is not transferred through the slabs as compression perpendicular to the direction of the fibers. Since 
the largest accumulated vertical load is subjected to the walls on the ground floor, this is the focus of the 
design. See the location of the investigated wall strips in figure 6.28. 
Exterior walls: The vertical load-bearing walls is filled with openings, that need to be considered. The walls 
are designed by calculating the capacity of a one meter wide strip of the walls subjected to the average 
vertical load acting along the length of the concerned wall element. The openings are considered by 
distributing the forces to the residual cross section. 
 
Figure 6.28 – Investigated strips of vertical load-bearing interior walls. 
==PAGE:152==
Andreas Juul Larsen

Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020

6.5.1.1. Eccentricities 
As mentioned in section 3.6.6 the geometric imperfections for the individual elements should be 
considered by introducing load eccentricities. Apart from the eccentricity of the wall’s deviation from 
planarity ²4, which is applicable for walls of all types of materials, Eurocode 5 does not include any 
information of how to define the load eccentricities. Instead the guidelines for concrete elements described 
in [Jensen, 2014] is used to determine the values for the eccentricities. The total eccentricity is calculated 
by use of equation 6.72 and illustrated in figure 6.29.

6.5.1.2. Interior walls at bathroom cabins 
The wall strips are subjected to both vertical and horizontal forces. The vertical forces consist of load from 
the wall above, the supported slabs and the bathroom cabins. The vertical load from the bathroom cabins is 
distributed to a bigger part of the wall than the actual width of the cabins due to load propagation. This is 
included in the calculation by introducing an effective load width referring to the one-meter strip. The load 
width is calculated by equation 6.73 and illustrated in figure 6.30.

Figure 6.30 – Propagation of load from the bathroom cabins into the wall. 
The vertical load is assumed to be constant along the height of the wall, even though the height at the top 
will be slightly lower than in the bottom due to the self-weight of the wall. The vertical load in the bottom is 
used for the verification. 
 
==PAGE:154==
Andreas Juul Larsen

S144609

The horizontal load consist of a uniform surface load from the difference of the internal wind pressure in 
the rooms on each side of the wall, and a horizontal imposed line load, which in this case is conservatively 
assumed as a uniformly distributed load. Together with the eccentricity on the vertical load the horizontal 
load introduces a moment force on the wall. The total moment force is conservatively assumed located at 
the half height of the wall. The total moment force is calculated by use of equation 6.74. 
. = 1
8 ∗Ý ∗ℎ + ² ∗N 
(6.74)
Where: 
.: 
Moment force [kNm].

Horizontal uniformly distributed line load [kN/m]. 
ℎ: 
Height of wall [m]. 
²: 
Eccentricity of vertical load [mm]. 
N: 
Vertical load [kN]. 
The wall strips is thus subjected to a vertical compression force acting the direction of the fibers in the 
longitudinal layers, combined with a moment around the y-axis. This equals the situation illustrated on 
figure 6.17 in section 6.3.1.12. The wall strips is investigated regarding the load bearing capacity in both the 
persistent design situation and the accidental design situation of the ultimate limit states. In the persistent 
design situation the cold cross-section is verified, while the residual cross-section in case of fire is verified in 
the accidental design situation. For both the standard fire and the parametric fire the wall is investigated 
for both one-sided fire exposure and two-sided exposure. The two-sided exposure is only theoretical, thus 
the fire safety design of the building is assumed to be capable of preventing it from occurring. 
By investigating all relevant load combination, the critical combinations have been determined as STR-1 
with dominating self-weight for the persistent design situation, and ACC-1 with dominating imposed load 
for the accidental design situation. Generally the self-weight is found as the critical load type, due to the 
height of the building. This indicates that the horizontal load have a very little influence on the results. In 
table 6.9 the loads and forces acting on the four investigated interior wall strips is listed for the critical load

equation 6.67 it is seen that the load propagation width of the lintels is close  approximately 300 mm on 
both sides of the opening when using the height of the opening (2.1 %) as the wall height: d“´ = ℎ/4 ∗

6.5.1.4. Exterior walls 
The exterior load-bearing walls are like the interior load-bearing walls subjected to both a vertical uniformly 
distributed line load and a horizontal uniformly distributed surface load. The horizontal load acting on the 
exterior walls is however larger since it mainly consist of the compression or suction force from the exterior 
wind load. Additionally the exterior walls are exposed to moment forces from the balconies. The balconies 
are supported by cantilevered beams mounted on the façade. The moment is conservatively assumed 
acting in the center of the wall, at the same location as the maximal moment from the horizontal load. The 
moment from the balcony is generated as a result of horizontal load on the balcony floor and vertical load 
on the railing. Equation 6.68, which is valid for the interior walls, is then substituted with equation 6.75. 
Conservatively all exterior load-bearing walls is verified for the moment from the cantilevered beams 
supporting the center of the large ZBA1 balconies on the top floors. 
. = 1

The exterior walls contains a lot of openings for doors and windows. The wall openings result in larger 
forces in the remaining cross-section. According to [Wallner-Novak, 2014] approximately uniformly 
distributed forces can in general be assumed. In relation to the one-meter strip the effective vertical load 
acting on the remaining cross-section is calculated by use of equation 6.76.

The exterior walls in all four facades has been investigated. In each façade three parts of the façade is 
investigated: 
• 
Part C-D-E on the ground floor. This is the part of the façade that resist the largest vertical loads.  
• 
Part A-B on the ground floor. To investigated if a smaller cross section could be a possibility for this 
part of the façade, which resist vertical load of lower magnitudes. 
• 
Part E on the 7th – 8th floor. This part of the façade has a height of two floors since the openings for 
the stairs in the apartments on the top floors is located here. This result in a longer buckling length 
for the wall, which could be critical regarding instability.

6.5.1.5. Results 
All the load-bearing walls is calculated as wall strips that are simple supported in both the top and bottom. 
The buckling length is equal to the height of the wall. In table 6.12 the maximum utilization rates for the 
most critical wall strips described in section 6.5.1.2, 6.5.1.3 and 6.5.1.4 is listed. The wall strips is 
investigated for compression parallel with the fibers of the longitudinal layers, bending out of the plane, 
and buckling as a consequence of instability when subjected to a combination of compression and bending. 
Due to small horizontal forces the verification of the shear stresses is omitted. The wall strips is verified in 
both the persistent design situation for the cold cross-section, and in the accidental design situation for the 
hot cross-section. The two design situations is illustrated in figure 6.34. Due to charring the moment in the 
accidental state increases because of a change in the center of gravity. The increased moment is calculated 
by use of equation 6.77. The moments listed in table 6.9, 6.10, and 6.11 for the accidental design situation 
is exclusive the extra moment from the change of the location of the center of gravity since it depends on 
the charring depth that are varies from the standard fire to the parametric fire.
