149 
 
From the results in table 6.12 it can be concluded that all vertical load-bearing walls got the sufficient 
capacity to resist the forces in both the persistent design situation and the accidental design situation when 
exposed to fire from one side. From the investigation of two-sided fire exposure on the interior walls, the 
results clearly state that the situation should be avoided. By exposing the walls from two sides the cross-
section is charring from both sides, which result in residual cross-section with a too high slenderness to be 
able to resist both bending and buckling. The situation should be avoided by including a proper fire 
sectioning in the fire design strategy. 
Relatively high utilization rates are calculated for buckling of the critical interior walls in both the persistent 
and the accidental design situation. This indicated that the cross-section of the interior walls and the 
thickness of the fire insulation is efficiently designed and should not be either increased or decreased. The 
same applies for the external walls. All three investigated wall parts got high utilizations rates, but the 
reason varies from part to part. Part C-D-E on the ground floor is critical due to large vertical loads from the 
structure above, part A-B on the ground floor is critical due to many opening and a high effective width 
coefficient, and part E on the 7th – 8th floor is critical due to the increased buckling length. 
Based on the assumption of relatively large moments from the balconies acting on all the walls it is not 
possible to decrease the cross-section for any of the investigated exterior wall parts. A more detailed and 
comprehensive calculation with more consideration of the location of the balconies, could possibly give the 
possibility of decreasing the cross-section of the exterior walls with a few millimeters, when only 
considering the vertical load-bearing capacity. Another important criteria for the determination of the 
exterior wall thickness have been the required thickness to ensure sufficient load-bearing capacity of the 
lintels above the openings for doors and windows in the facades. This lintels are investigated in section 6.7. 
 
6.5.2. 
Shear walls 
The shear walls is illustrated on figure 6.27 and investigated according to the theory in section 6.3.1.7 and 
6.3.1.9. As illustrated in figure 6.35 the shear walls are subjected to a horizontal point load acting at the top 
of the wall parallel to the transverse layers. The horizontal point load is the accumulated horizontal force 
from wind load acting on the facades, inclusive a contribution from the geometric imperfections, which is 
distributed to the shear walls according to their stiffness in section 4.4.5.    
 
Figure 6.35 - Shear wall. 
==PAGE:162==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
150 
 
The shear walls is only investigated in the persistent design situations since the wind load is reduced with 
the coefficient  = 0.2 in the accidental design situation, and therefore is assumed reasonable to be 
omitted. The critical load combination is STR-8 where both the wind load and the contribution from the 
geometrical imperfections is at its maximal values. In appendix Q.1-6 all the six shear walls are investigated 
on each floor. Since the horizontal force accumulates through the building the shear walls on the ground 
floor should resist the largest forces. In this section the results for the calculation of the shear walls on the 
ground floor is reviewed. The floor height of the ground floor is used as the height of the shear walls: ℎ=
2.993 %. In table 6.13 the length of the shear walls together with the accumulated horizontal forces and 
moments acting on the walls in load combination STR-8 is listed.

From the utilization rates in table 6.8 it can be concluded that the shear walls have the sufficient capacity to 
resist the horizontal forces. It is obvious that the shear walls parallel to the x-direction have the highest 
utilization rates, which is due to large horizontal forces from the wind. The utilization rates for the walls 
parallel with the y-direction is relatively low, and if the capacity to resist horizontal forces was the only 
design criteria the cross-section for these walls could be decreased. The bending strength is only of interest 
for short walls since the length of the wall is in third potency in the equation for the section modulus. In 
general shear mechanism 2, considering the shearing failure of the glued surfaces in the intersection points, 
is the designing criteria. In [Wallner-Novak, 2014] the recommended value for the board width to be used 
in the calculation of the torsional shear strength of the glued surfaces is & = 80 %%, if the exact value is 
unknown. Using this value would give failure in the shear walls parallel to the x-direction. Instead a board 
width of & = 160 %% is used to achieve the required resistance. A board width of & = 160 %% is the 
same width, as is used for the slab panels to validate the use of a characteristic rolling shear strength of 
,,“, = 1.1 ./&. With a maximal board thickness of ¼By' = 40 %% the criteria is: & ≥¼By' ∗4 =
160 %%.

6.6. 
Load transfer through slabs 
In this section two different solutions of transferring loads from walls on one floor to the walls on the floor 
below. The first solutions is load transfer through the slabs as compression perpendicular to the direction 
of the fibers. The second solution is direct transfer from wall to wall through a toothed joint. This solution 
avoid compression perpendicular to the direction of the fibers in the slabs, which timber is very sensitive to 
both when considering load bearing capacity and deformations. The strength and stiffness of timber 
perpendicular to the direction of the fibers is significantly lower than in parallel to the direction of the fiber, 
thus perpendicular compression should be attempted to avoid if possible. The toothed joint is made of 
teeth with a width of 250 mm, that consist of 50 percent slab teeth and 50 percent wall teeth. This means 
that the load can be transferred along 100 percent of the wall length in the first solution and along 50 % of 
the wall length in the second solution. The two solutions are illustrated on figure 6.36. 
 
 
Figure 6.36 - Load transfer solutions. Left: Load transfer through slabs. Right: Direct transfer from wall to wall through toothed joint. 
The two solutions are both investigated regarding the load-bearing capacity in the persistent design 
situation in the ultimate limit states and regarding the compressive deformation in the serviceability limit 
states. The heaviest loaded wall strip in the building, which is wall WIY2 – Part A, is used to investigate the 
load transfer solutions. The wall strip is investigated in section 6.5 and the location is illustrated on figure 
6.28. In the persistent design situation the compression perpendicular to the direction of the fibers in the 
slabs is investigated by use of the theory in section 6.3.1.5 with £
 = d ∗RC + 30 %%S and  = 1.40, 
while the compression parallel to the direction of the fibers of the longitudinal layers in the walls is 
investigated by use of the theory in section 6.3.1.2 with £2,	
 = d/2 ∗¼2,

section identified as: CLT 200 C5s (40l – 40w – 40l – 40w – 40l). When referring to a wall strip with a width

Test has shown that the final deformation from compression perpendicular to the direction of the fibers 
depends more on the moisture content than with other stresses. According to [Wallner-Novak, 2018] this 
can be considered by using the deformation coefficient corresponding to the respectively higher utilization 
class. This is an assumption in an approximate fashion [Wallner-Novak, 2018]. Since the slab is assigned to 
utilization class 1, the deformation class corresponding to utilization class 2 should be used, i.e. 
 = 1.0 
according to table 3.16 in section 3.5.1.6.1. 
The deformation from compression parallel to the fibers in the longitudinal layers of the walls is not 
calculated since they is normally neglectable due to a high stiffness. Instead the compressive deformation 
of the two load transfer solution is compared solely on whether the deformations of the slabs occurs or 
not. 
In the persistent design situation the load-bearing capacities is investigated with load combination STR-1, 
STR-2, and STR-6, while the compressive deformations is investigated with load combination SLS-1 and SLS-
2. These are assumed to the most critical load combinations since the largest parts of the vertical loads 
comes from self-weight, imposed load, and snow load. The loads are assumed constant along the thickness 
of the slabs and are listed in table 6.15. Propagation of the load from the bathroom cabins into the walls is 
conservatively not considered for the analysis, thus the magnitude of the loads can’t be directly compared 
to the load used to verify the concerned wall strips in section 6.5. No recommended limits for the 
compressive deformations of slabs is given in the cross-laminated timber guides. Instead a limit of 	 ≤
B' = ℎ/500 where ℎ is the floor height and a limit of 	 ≤B' = ℎ/1000 where ℎ is the full building 
height is assumed to be appropriate to use. In table 6.16 the results for the analysis of both the 
compressive load-bearing capacity and the compressive deformations is listed. The calculations of the load 
transfer can be found in appendix U.

From the results in table 6.16 it can be concluded that the solution where the load is transferred through 
the slabs lead to compressive failure in the slabs above the three lowest floors. By using the solution with 
direct load transfer from wall to wall by applying toothed joints failure is avoided, even though the effective 
contact area is decreased with approximately 70 percent. This is due to a seven times larger compressive 
strength parallel to the direction of the fibers compared the strength perpendicular to the direction of the 
fibers.  
 
==PAGE:166==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
154 
 
To avoid compressive failure the building is designed with toothed joints in the slabs above the ground 
floor to above the 5th floor. From above the 6th floor and up to the roof, load transfer through the slabs is 
chosen as the solution, due to relatively small vertical forces, and high horizontal forces in the diaphragm 
above the 6th floor, which requires the full connection length to ensure the required capacity of the 
connections between the slab elements.    
Regarding the deformations, load transfer through the slabs lead to a total final deformations of almost 4 
cm, which is evaluated as too much to ensure that the adjacent building parts is not damaged. Especially 
the compressive deformations and elastic strains in the slab above the two lowest floors is assumed too 
large and the deformation does not comply with the assumed limit. By using toothed joints in the slabs 
above the lowest six floors the total final compressive deformation of the building is limited to only 8 mm, 
which corresponds to under 1/3300 of the total building height. However, to obtain the total realistic 
vertical displacement of the building, the settlements of the foundations should be added, but this is out of 
the scope for this project and have not been investigated. 
 
6.7. 
Lintels 
In this section the lintels above openings in the walls is analyzed. The interior walls contain openings for 
doors that all have a width of 1000 mm. The doors in the interior walls are fixed in the same location on all 
floor, except for a couple of doors on the ground floor and the two top floors, which, however, has no 
significant impact on the design. The location of the three most critical lintels in the interior walls are 
illustrated on figure 6.28 in section 6.5.1. All three lintels are integrated in the cross-laminated timber wall 
elements and denoted as continuous lintels. The lintels in the interior walls got a height of ℎ= 468 %%. As 
described in section 6.3.1.7 a maximal beam height of ℎB	 = ®/3 = 333 %% is used for the verification, to 
ensure that the beam theory assuming linear stress distribution is valid, and avoid a overestimation of the 
load-bearing capacity. 
In the exterior walls the size and location of the openings for walls and doors changes from floor to floor. 
Therefore a lot of different load distributions paths is observed. Four different lintels is analyzed to 
investigate the two situations that a assumed the most interesting for the complete design of the building. 
The first situation consist of a lintel located in the western façade (Wall WE4) with a span of 2412 mm and 
supporting the heaviest floor structure in the building including a prefabricated bathroom cabin. In the 
analysis the span is conservatively rounded up to 2450 mm. The lintel is integrated in the CLT wall element 
as a continuous lintel. The lintel is illustrated with a blue color and the number 1 on figure 6.37. The lintel is 
illustrated in the eastern façade (Wall WE2), to make is possible to illustrate the two situations together. 
The lintel that is illustrated on the figure have the same geometry as the analyzed lintel on the opposite 
façade.  
The second situation are illustrated on figure 6.37 and is about how to solve the challenge when an 
otherwise continuous load-bearing wall is interrupted by an opening on the ground floor in the eastern 
façade (Wall WE2). The lintels on the floors above, illustrated with a red color on the figure, does not have 
the capacity to distribute 100 percent of the load away from the opening. (The two lintels identified with 
number 2 and 3 are analyzed in this section). This means that the lintel above the opening on the ground 
floor, illustrated with a light brown color and the number 4 on the figure, have to resist a part of the load 
accumulated from the floor above. 
==PAGE:167==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
155 
 
The lintels illustrated with red is continuous CLT lintels, while the lintel illustrated with a light brown color is 
made of three parallel placed LVL beams of the type Kerto-S from Metsa Wood with a width of 69 mm 
each. The LVL beams are screwed together to ensure that they work as one. The total thickness of the LVL 
beams is 207 mm, which exceed the width of the wall element with 7 mm, this however can be placed 
against the insulation on the outside and will not result in any challenges. LVL is used instead of cross-
laminated timber, glued-laminated timber, and solid timber, since it got significantly higher strength 
properties as listed in table 3.11 in section 3.5.1.4.3, due to the build-up consisting of multiple thin veneers 
located parallel to the span direction. However, the LVL lintel can’t resist 100 percent of the accumulated 
load from the floors above, and therefore the load is distributed between the LVL lintel and the CLT lintels 
on the floors above by use of the plasticity theory. The CLT lintels resist 50 percent of the load on the 
concerned floor, while the LVL lintel resist the last 40 percent together with 100 percent of the load from 
the 1st floor slab supported directly by the LVL lintel. the lintels in the exterior walls got a height of ℎ=
614 %%, which is rounded down to 600 mm for the analysis. The slabs are mounted on the inside of the 
walls to achieve the largest possible height for the lintels, which otherwise would not have the required 
capacity to carry the subjected load. The cross section of both the CLT lintel in the first situation, the CLT 
lintels in the second situation, and the LVL lintel is illustrated on figure 6.38. 
 
 
Figure 6.37 – Analyzed lintels in the exterior walls illustrated in the eastern façade (Wall WE2). Left: Load-bearing timber elements. 
Right: Building with envelope and balconies.

exterior wall. 
The loads are predominantly loaded by self-weight and imposed load, thus STR-1 and STR-2 are the critical 
load combinations in the persistent design situation and ACC-1 is the critical load combination in the 
accidental design situation. Both the lintels in the interior and exterior walls are subjected to a uniformly 
distributed line load from the slabs and walls, while the lintels in the exterior walls is additional subjected 
to an assumed centrally located point load from the balconies. The loads acting on the lintels is listed in 
table 6.17 for the relevant load combinations. All lintels is calculated as single-span simple supported 
beams, as illustrated on the static model in figure 6.39.

In the persistent design situation all lintels is investigated for sufficient bending and shear capacity. 
Additionally the compressive stresses between the LVL lintel and the CLT wall is assessed. Since the LVL 
lintel is an engaged lintel, the bending capacity have been reduced due to lateral torsional buckling as 
described in section 6.3.1.11. In the cold condition the value for the lateral torsional buckling coefficient is 
calculated to:  = 0.37. The width of one LVL beam, d = 69 %%, have been conservatively used to 
calculate the coefficient, since the joint between the LVL beams have not been investigated.   
In the accidental design situation the same properties has been investigated as I the persistent design 
situation. The lintels are verified for a two-sided fire expose: from the bottom and the side. The lateral 
buckling coefficient for the LVL lintel in both the design situation of a standard fire and a parametric fire has 
been calculated to: , = 0.43.

been used to calculate the instant deformations for both the CLT and LVL lintels. In the case of CLT beams 
with the transverse layers parallel to the direction of the span the equation is formulated as stated in 
equation 6.80, with £ = £•5 = ¼ ∗ℎ.

From the results in table 6.18 it is seen that the shear strength is the designing factor for short lintels, while 
the bending strength and the stiffness is the designing factor, which is to be expected when applying the 
beam theory assuming linear stress distribution. The capacity in the fire situation is generally not a problem 
due to sufficient fire insulation. Regarding the LVL lintel the compressive strength perpendicular to the 
direction of the fibers is also a designing factor, like with all other timber products. It is concluded that the 
analyzed lintels got the sufficient capacity both regarding strength and stiffness. However the most critical 
lintels got relatively high utilization rates, which tells that geometry of the facades with changing positions 
of openings is a challenge when using cross-laminated timber as load-bearing members, since it generates a 
lack of continuous load-bearing walls.     
 
6.8. 
Steel beams 
Steel beams are used when timber beams is unsuitable due to lack of strength or stiffness. It concerns 
beams for support of horizontal cross-laminated timber slab panels in the floor structure above the 7th floor 
due to large openings, and cantilevered beams for support of the balconies. HEB cross-sections are used for 
all steel beams, in sizes depending on the span and the load impact. Four beams are used to support the 
floor slabs above the 7th floor: two HE180B beams (EB1S, EB2S), and two HE200B beams (EB3S, EB4S). 
HE140B beams are used to support all balconies. The cross section of the beams is illustrated in figure 6.40. 
Material properties is according to section 3.5.2, while cross-section properties is from [Jensen, 2019] and 
listed in table 6.19. The location of the steel beams are illustrated on a floor plan of the 8th floor in figure 
6.41. In section 6.9 an alternative solution of using glulam beams for support of the slabs instead of steel 
beams. The section highlights the reason timber beams is not suitable for the task.

6.8.1. Beams for support of floor slabs 
The steel beams for support of floor slabs is calculated as simple supported single-span beams. The beams 
are subjected to a uniformly distributed line load along the full length from self-weight of the beam and 
self-weight and imposed load from the floor structure located directly on top of it. In case of beam EB1S the 
line load also include load from floor slab zone ZFS1c. The beams are also subjected to a uniformly 
distributed line load from the floor slabs supported by the beams. The extend of the line load depends on 
the concerned beam. Additionally the beams are subjected to a point load from the upper support of the 
light apartment stairs. In figure 6.42 the general static system of the steel beams is illustrated. The strength 
of steel does not depend on the duration of the load in the persistent design situation as is the case for 
timber structures. The steel beams are only subjected to self-weight and imposed loads, thus STR-2, ACC-1 
and SLS-1 is the critical load combination in each of the analyzed design situations. The geometry and the 
design loads for all four beams is listed in table 6.20. 
 
Figure 6.42 - Static system of steel beams for support of floor slabs.

In the ultimate limit states the steel beams are analyzed for both the persistent design situation and the 
accidental design situation. In both design situations the beams are investigated regarding bending, shear, 
and lateral torsional buckling. Since the focus of this project is timber structure, reference is made to 
Eurocode 3 and the associated Danish national annexes regarding the theory of designing and verifying 
steel members. The beams are analyzed for two-sided exposure, i.e. from bottom and one side, in the case 
of a parametric fire in the accidental design situation. In the serviceability limit states the deformations are 
analyzed. Both deformation limits aimed at steel beams and timber slabs is assessed, since deformations in 
the steel beams also affect the timber slabs that they support. The maximal utilization rates are listed in 
table 6.21 for all relevant design situations. The calculations of the steel beams can be found in appendix

The designing factor for the steel beams is the deformations from the loads in the characteristic load 
combination. The limit is valid because it should be ensured that the damages that occur due to extensive 
deformations is avoided. Regarding the load bearing capacity in the ultimate limit states bending and 
lateral torsional buckling is the most critical properties, which is expected when analyzing beams with 
relatively long spans. As described more deeply in section 6.9 timber structure got a significantly lower 
bending stiffness than steel, thus the utilization of long timber beams is often challenging when 
requirements are set regarding the maximal allowed deformations.

6.8.2. Beams for support of balconies 
This section will focus on the center beam of the large balconies (ZBA1) on the 7th and 8th floor, since this is 
the beams with the largest catchment area. The balconies got a width of - = 6.1 % and a depth of á =
1.475 %, and thus a catchment area of £ = -/2 ∗á = 4.5 %. The balconies are subjected to a vertical 
uniformly distributed surface load including self-weight, imposed load, snow load and wind load acting on 
the balcony slab. As described in section 3.6.2 the imposed load are including potentially concurrent 
contributions from snow load. This means that the imposed load and the snow load should not be 
combined, and only the most critical in the concerned case should be included. Increased load from snow 
drift should be included in the snow load. In reality the snow drift results in a triangular distribution of the 
snow load with the maximum value at the façade. Conservatively an average value is used for the snow 
load resulting a uniform distributed surface load. The wind load is either acting as a combined force from 
pressure on the top surface and suction on the bottom surface or the opposite, depending on which 
combination is the most critical. Additionally the balconies are subjected to horizontal forces acting on the 
railing. The horizontal forces consist of a uniformly distributed surface load from wind load, and a 
horizontal imposed line load acting on the railing. Both horizontal forces are acting either inwards or 
outwards depending on which case is the most unfavorable. 
The most critical combination regarding the cantilevered beams is when the combined vertical forces are 
acting downwards, while the combined horizontal forces are acting outwards. The situation is illustrated in 
figure 6.43. This combination results in the largest possible moment at the fixed support on the wall. The 
characteristic loads acting on the balconies are listed in table 6.22. 
 
Figure 6.43 - Load impacts on balconies.

6.9. 
Glulam beams – Alternative solution 
Glulam beams are designed with the purpose to investigate which minimum dimensions would be required 
to use if timber beams should be used instead of steel beams for the support of slabs in the floor structure 
above the 7th floor. The static system and the loads, except a minor decrease in the self-weight due to 
change of material, is still as described in section 6.8.1 where the steel beams were assessed. To limit the 
required dimensions of the glulam beams the strongest standard strength class used in Denmark, GL30h, is 
used for the design. Straight glulam beams is normally manufactured with a width up to 210 mm and height 
that increases with 33 mm between each cross-section size. The height increase is due to lamellas with a 
thickness of 33 mm is normally utilized. The limit for the maximum allowable height of the glulam beams is 
equal to the thickness of cross-laminated timber floor panels that the beams should support. This 
requirement is set to avoid the beams from being visible from below the slab and deface the architectural 
expression. The glued-laminated timber beams have been named according to section 4.1 and the 
corresponding steel beams. The calculation of the beams can be found in appendix Z.1-4, where both the 
ultimate limit states and the serviceability limit states have been analyzed. In table 6.24 the results from 
the investigation is listed. The maximum width of 210 mm is used for all beams.

The designing factor for all the analyzed glued-laminated timber beams is the deformation, mainly 
dependent on the flexural stiffness. Glulam in strength class GL30h got a mean modulus of elasticity 
parallel to the direction of the fibers of 2,'
y	 = 13600 ./&. Compared to steel with a modulus of 
elasticity of  = 210 Š/&, glulam beams need a moment of inertia that are /2,'
y	 = 15.44 times 
bigger than the corresponding steel beams to achieve the same flexural stiffness. In general the stiffness of 
timber is one of the biggest challenges when designing beams, especially if long spans are utilized. For the 
two shortest beams, that are exposed to the largest loads, the bending strength also becomes a designing 
factor. This is because the ratio between the total load and the length of the span is lower than what is the 
case for the longer beams exposed to a smaller total load. None of the four analyzed glulam beams comply 
with the limit height decided from the thickness of the CLT floor panels. Due to this the steel beams 
designed in section 6.8.1 is used for the final design.

7. 
Connections 
In this chapter the most important connections are addressed and designed. The connections are divided in 
three groups depending on the purpose of the connections. The first group is the stability connections, 
which include the connection that takes part in transferring the horizontal loads from the horizontal 
diaphragms and the shear walls to the foundations. The second group is the connections with the purpose 
of supporting the slabs and transferring the vertical loads to the beams and walls. The third groups is the 
connections that are securing the facades and the roof against the suction from the wind. 
The aim when designing the connections have been to find the best solution statically while also 
considering other topics like fire-safety, soundproofing and airtightening. The design of the connections 
highly depends on the design choices made when designing the structural system and the individual 
structural elements. Due to this as well as the highrise nature of the building, the design of all connections 
are not straight forward, and special solution have been necessary in some cases. However, in all cases it 
have been attempted to find efficient and simple solutions, which won’t prolong the construction period. 
   
7.1. 
Stability connections 
This section address the most important connections included in the stabilizing system of the building. 
 
7.1.1. Shear walls 
If found necessary shear walls should be secured against tension and shear forces. The design of the shear 
walls have been addressed in chapter 5, where also the need for tension and shear anchoring have been 
determined. 
   
7.1.1.1. Tension anchoring 
The need for tension anchoring to avoid overturning of the shear walls was addressed in section 5.1.1, and 
the required tension force to be anchored was listed in table 5.2. It was found that all three walls in the x-
direction required tension anchoring on all floors, while only wall WIY1 in the y-direction required tension 
anchoring on the lowest four floors. A common way to anchor shear walls of cross-laminated timber 
against tension forces is to apply angle brackets designed for tensile stresses, as illustrated in figure 7.1. 
The angle brackets work by transferring the tension forces through the slabs by a threaded rod. An angle 
bracket are then fastened to the shear wall on each floor with a large amount of screws. The tensile 
capacity of these are however limited and is not an ideal solution for highrise buildings. The large number 
of screws also make the installation comprehensive and time consuming, in particular if more than one pair 
of brackets are needed to transfer the tensile forces due to high loads. 
 
 
==PAGE:180==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
168 
 
 
Figure 7.1 - Angle brackets for tensile stress [Rothoblaas, 2017a]. 
 
Instead of using angle brackets, continuous tension rods can be used to transfer the tensile forces. The 
method is briefly described in section 5.1.1 but will be further described in this section. The solution with 
tension rods work by transferring the tension forces to the foundations, which then have to be designed for 
these forces. The foundation are made of in-situ concrete and are receiving the tensile forces via 
embedded tension rods connected to the continuous tension rods by reinforcement couplers. The tension 
rods receive the forces from the shear walls via 200x200 mm anchor plates of steel placed in recesses on 
the upper corner of the walls on each floor. The forces from the shear walls are transferred to the anchor 
plates as compression, while steel nuts are used to transfer the forces from the anchor plates to the 
tension rods. 
The continuous tension rods consist of rods, with a length corresponding to the floor height of the 
concerned floors, which are jointed with reinforcement couplers. This creates the possibility of reducing the 
diameter of the tension rods from floor to floor if the tension forces allow it. In general the tension force 
decreases along the height of the building, with the largest forces at the lower floors and the lowest forces 
at the upper floors. If the diameter of the tension rods can be reduced a reduction coupler is used instead 
of a standard reinforcement coupler. Couplers from Peikko is used, and information about these can be 
found in appendix AA.10. The tension rods are designed in pairs, with one rod located on each side of the 
centerline of shear wall. For each tension bar a continuous hole with the dimensions of 45x45 mm is carved 
to make enough place for the tension bars with the largest diameters. The holes are located centrally in the 
transverse layers of the shear walls to maintain as much vertical load-bearing capacity as possible. In the 
lowest 500 mm of the shear walls the dimensions of the holes are increased to 80x80 to make room for the 
steel nuts and reinforcement couplers. This solution requires that the walls be custom-made, which put 
bigger demands on the manufacturer, and increase the expenses of the production. The solution is 
however preferred since it enables fast installation at the construction site and increases the robustness of 
the structure due to the continuous anchoring of the most critical shear walls. The extra expenses from the 
custom-made elements may be equalized by the avoided use of screws in the method of using angle 
brackets.     
==PAGE:181==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
169

on figure 5.4 in section 5.1.1. The required diameter of the tension rods applied for each shear wall is listed 
in table 5.3 in section 5.1.1. When verifying the design the following have been investigated:

7.1.1.2. Shear anchoring 
The need for shear anchoring to avoid sliding and large deformations of the shear walls was addressed in 
section 5.1.3 and 5.1.4, respectively. The purpose of the shear anchoring is partly to transfer the shear 
force from the shear wall on one floor to the corresponding shear wall on the floor below, and partly to 
transfer the shear force from the horizontal diaphragms to the shear walls supporting it. As mentioned in 
section 5.1.4, steel brackets are used to anchor the shear walls against the shear forces. Three different 
steel brackets are used, depending on the conditions of the connection. The three types are listed below 
and illustrated in figure 7.3. 
• 
Shear joint between wall of CLT and horizontal diaphragm of CLT. A steel bracket identified as 
TITAN N – TTN240 with 2x36 LBA nails Ø4.0 x 60 from Rothoblaas is used. To avoid flank 
transmissions of sound through the steel bracket a soundproofing product called ALADIN STRIPE 
SOFT-ALADIN95 from Rothoblaas is used. Information about the steel bracket, the soundproofing 
profile, and the interaction, denoted TITAN SILENT, can be found in [Rothoblaas, 2019a, p. 186], 
[Rothoblaas, 2018, p. 104], and [Rothoblaas, 2019a, p. 234], respectively. With the soundproofing 
product applied both the shear strength and the stiffness of the steel bracket is reduced. The 
design shear capacity of the steel bracket is reduced with approximately 24 percent to a value of: 
‡-, = 23.55 N, while the stiffness is reduced with approximately 13 percent to a value of: q¡ =
7.144 N/%%. [Rothoblaas, 2019a, p. 234] does not contain information about the stiffness of the 
steel bracket, thus the value valid for the steel bracket identified as TITAN F – TTF200 from 
Rothoblaas is used instead. TTF200 is similar but smaller than TTN240, thus it is assumed to be 
conservatively to use the value. Information about TTF200 can be found in [Rothoblaas, 2019a, p. 
218]. The connection is illustrated in figure 7.4, which is based on detail drawing 503 in the 
associated drawing folder. 
==PAGE:183==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
171 
 
• 
Shear joint between wall of CLT and floor of concrete. A steel bracket identified as TITAN S – 
TCS240 with 14x HBS Plate screws Ø8.0 x 80 and 2x M16 x 160 VIN-FIX PRO 5.8 chemical concrete 
anchors from Rothoblaas is used. Information about the steel bracket can be found in [Rothoblaas, 
2019a, p. 204]. The design shear capacity of the steel bracket is: ‡-, = 55.00 N, while the 
stiffness is: q¡ = 8.200 N/%%.  The connection is illustrated in figure 7.5 (Left), which is based on 
detail drawing 507 in the associated drawing folder 
• 
Shear joint between wall of CLT and wall of concrete. A steel bracket identified as TITAN Plate C – 
TCP300 with 21x LBA nails Ø4.0 x 60 and 2x M16 x 190 VIN-FIX PRO 5.8 chemical concrete anchors 
from Rothoblaas is used. Information about the steel bracket can be found in [Rothoblaas, 2019a, 
p. 254]. The design shear capacity of the steel bracket is: ‡-, = 34.40 N, while the stiffness is: 
q¡ = 8.200 N/%%. [Rothoblaas, 2019a, p. 254] does not contain information about the stiffness 
of the steel bracket, thus the value valid for the steel bracket TITAN S – TCC240 from Rothoblaas is 
used instead. Both steel brackets are used for timber to concrete connections, thus it is assumed 
that the same value can be used.  The connection is illustrated in figure 7.5 (Right), which is based 
on detail drawing 508 in the associated drawing folder 
 
    
    
 
Figure 7.3 - Steel brackets. Left: TITAN N - TTN240. Middle: TITAN S - TCS240. Right: TITAN Plate C - TCP 300 [Rothoblaas, 2017]. 
 
Where each type is applied and how many are required to achieve the sufficient shear capacity and 
stiffness is listed in table 5.12 in section 5.1.4. Calculations of the shear anchoring, including utilization rates 
and distance between the steel brackets can be found in appendix AA.2. Since a friction coefficient of ^ =
0.0 was conservatively assumed in section 5.1.3, the sliding force is the designing factor for the shear 
anchoring. As expected the largest amount of steel brackets are used on the lower floors where the shear 
force is largest.

7.1.2. Connections in horizontal diaphragms 
To ensure that the horizontal diaphragms work as intended and are capable of transferring the horizontal 
forces to the stabilizing shear walls, the horizontal connections between the individual slab elements have 
to be proper designed. The connections include both the joints supported by walls and the joint not 
supported by walls. The connections should be able to transfer the horizontal shear, moment, and normal 
forces calculated in section 4.4.6, as well as, if not supported by walls, a vertical shear force from a possible 
varying vertical displacement of the slabs due to different boundary conditions and different load 
magnitudes. In general the most critical joints is the ones with the shortest length or the largest loads. By 
referring to the results from section 4.4.6 and appendix P.1-4, while using the terminology from section 
4.4.6 the most critical joints have been determined to be located at line C (Module line B/C) and line D 
(Module line C) in beam A, when horizontal loads are acting from west (x-direction). The joint in line C is the 
most critical joint that are supported by a wall (Wall WIX2), while the most critical joints that are not 
supported by a wall is located around line D. The location of the critical joints is illustrated with a gray color 
in figure 7.6. Around line D two joints are located parallel to each other. One of them is critical due to the 
short length, while the other is critical due to the varying vertical displacement due to the prefabricated 
bathroom cabins. To limit the amount of calculations only the joints in these two lines are designed, since 
all other joints conservatively can be performed in a similar way. The designed fasteners used to ensure the 
sufficient capacity of the joint in line C is used for all joints supported by a wall. The designed fasteners 
used to ensure the sufficient capacity of most critical of the long and the short joint at line D is used for all 
joints not supported by a wall. If the design conditions changes between the floors, e.g. due to different 
slab thickness or joint design, different designs are included to incorporate the change in the design. 
 
Figure 7.6 - Location of critical joints in horizontal diaphragms. 
==PAGE:186==
Andreas Juul Larsen

7.1.2.1.  Forces 
As mentioned earlier the horizontal joints in the horizontal diaphragms may be exposed to three different 
type of forces: horizontal shear force, horizontal normal force, and vertical shear force. How the forces are 
determined and distributed along the joints is described in the following subsections. 
7.1.2.1.1. 
 Horizontal shear force 
The horizontal shear force acting on the joints is assumed to be evenly distributed along the length of the 
joint. If the fasteners is distributed evenly along the length of the joint, all fasteners are exposed to the 
same shear force. The shear force acting on each fastener is calculated with use of equation 7.1 [Wallner-
Novak, 2018]. 
‡• = •

®: 
Length of joint [m]. 
²•: 
Distance between the fasteners parallel to the direction of the joint [m]. 
 
7.1.2.1.2. 
Horizontal normal force 
The horizontal normal force consist of a moment force from wind on the facades perpendicular to the 
concerned joint, and a tension force from suction on the facades parallel to the concerned joint. The 
tension force is assumed to be evenly distributed along the length of the joint. The moment force is acting 
on the joint as a tension force and a compression force. The compression force is generally not critical and 
will not be investigated. The tension force can either be taken as a single tension force at the end of the 
joint, by some sort of tension board, or be taken of the fasteners along the length of the joint. If the first 
solution is applied the tension force along the joint due to suction on the parallel facades is calculated by 
equation 7.2, while the tension force at the end of the joint due to the moment force is calculated by 
equation 7.3. If the second solution is applied, and the compression force are assumed to be located at the 
end of the joint, the tension force along the joint is calculated by equation 7.4 [Wallner-Novak, 2018].

7.1.2.1.3. 
Vertical shear force 
If the joint is not supported by a wall, a vertical shear force due to varying displacements of the slabs 
should be considered. The difference in the vertical displacement can be caused by many different reasons 
including uneven distribution of variable loads, different support conditions, and different magnitude or 
location of self-weight. If the difference in displacement is due to uneven distribution of variable loads and 
different support conditions, e.g. difference between a three sided supported slab and a two sided 
supported slab, the vertical shear force is calculated by use of equation 7.5. If the difference are due to 
different magnitude or location of self-weight the vertical shear load is calculated by equation 7.6. If the 
situations considered in equation 7.6 and 7.6 is combined equation 7.7 is used [Wallner-Novak, 2018].

7.1.2.2. Connections not supported by walls 
Connections not supported by walls can be designed in many different ways. In this chapter three different 
designs are investigated and the most optimal is selected for the final design. The three different designs 
are illustrated in figure 7.7 and investigated in the following subsections. 
 
Figure 7.7 - Connection designs. Left: Crossed screws. Middle: Joint cover strip. Right: Rabbet edge. [Wallner-Novak, 2014]. 
Due to the large difference in thickness between the CLT panels used for the floor structure and the roof 
structure, the connections are design for both types. The connections in the horizontal floor diaphragms 
are designed to be used for both the CLT panels with a thickness of 260 mm and a thickness of 300 mm.  
The loads used to design the connection in the horizontal floor diaphragms are the ones acting on the 
diaphragm above the 6th floor, which is the most critical loaded diaphragm in the building. In table 7.3 the 
length of the joints and the characteristic loads acting on them is listed. The calculation of the loads can be 
found in appendix P.1 and AA.3-6. For all investigated joints, all types of forces are assumed distributed 
evenly along the joints.

7.1.2.2.1. 
Crossed screws 
In this solution pairs of fully threaded screws are placed with an angle of 45 degrees, as illustrated to the 
left in figure 7.7. The tension force acting on the joint from the horizontal normal force, the horizontal 
moment force and the vertical shear force is transferred as axial tension in the screws, while the horizontal 
shear force along the joint is transferred as shear in the screws. When screws are attached in the front face 
of CLT, the characteristic axial load bearing capacity is calculated by use of equation 7.9 [Wallner-Novak, 
2014]. Since the screws are rotated 45 degrees compared to the direction of both the horizontal and 
vertical loads, the axial load bearing capacity is multiplied with √2.

7.1.2.2.3. 
Rabbet edge 
In this solution the CLT panels are produced with rabbet edges, which is connected with fully threaded 
screws as illustrated to the right in figure 7.7. Rabbet edges with a depth of 160 mm is used. The tension 
force acting on the joint from the horizontal forces is transferred as shear in the screws, while the vertical 
shear force is transferred as axial tension in the screws. When screws are attached in the element surface 
of CLT, the characteristic axial load bearing capacity is calculated by use of equation 7.12 in section 
7.1.2.2.3 [Wallner-Novak, 2014]. As with the other two solution the characteristic shear capacity of the 
screws is calculated according to the Johansen theory and the equations in section 8.2 in Eurocode 5 
[DS/EN 1995-1-1, 2007]. The characteristic embedded strength of the timber in case of screws attached in 
the element surface of CLT is calculated by use of equation 7.13 in section 7.1.2.2.3 [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. 
When using a rabbet edge connection the load-bearing capacity of both the screws and the notches should 
be verified. The capacity of the screws should be verified by use of equation 7.16. Additionally the capacity 
when exposed to individual forces should be verified.

7.1.2.2.4. 
Final design 
From the results in the previous three subsections it is obvious that the shortest of the two joints is the 
critical, even though the long joint in the horizontal floor diaphragm is exposed to a much larger vertical 
shear force. Due to this all joints in the horizontal diaphragms, which are not supported by walls, are 
conservatively designed in the same way as the shortest of the two joints. If all joint were addressed and 
calculated the number of required fasteners could be decreased, but due to limitations of the project 
period this have not been performed.  
In table 7.7 the determined required internal distances of the fasteners from all three design suggestions 
regarding the shortest of the two joints is summarized. When focusing on the horizontal floor diaphragms 
the required amount of screws per meter for each of the solutions is approximately 11 screws in solution 1, 
29 screws in solution 2, and 9 in solution 3. The same situation is present in the case of the horizontal roof 
diaphragm. Generally the tensile load-bearing capacity of fully threaded screws is many time larger than 
the shear capacity, thus the optimal arrangement is in the direction of the load. However, the 50 percent 
decrease of the axial load-bearing capacity when attached to the front face of the CLT elements, decreases 
the difference. By only looking on the required amount of screws solution 3 is the best, in particular 
because the screws are shorter. However the rabbeted edges increases the demands on the manufacturer 
and the planning of the installation on the construction site. Additionally the use of rabbet edges decreases 
the usable width of the cross-laminated timber panels. Due to this the solutions with crossed screws are 
selected for the final design. Furthermore, this choice unify with the solution used for the joint in the 
horizontal diaphragm located at a wall, as described in section 7.1.2.3. In figure 7.8 the design of the 
connection in the horizontal floor diaphragm is illustrated, which is based on detail drawing 503 in the 
associated drawing folder. The connection in the roof diaphragm can be found on detail drawing 504 in the 
associated drawing folder.

7.1.2.3. Connections supported by walls 
The connections in the horizontal diaphragms that are supported by walls, is divided in two groups: the 
ones were a toothed joint is used, and the one where the full length of the joint can be used to make the 
connection. As mentioned earlier the short joint located in module line D, which is illustrated and denoted 
line C in figure 7.6 is the most critical joint and is used to design the connections. Due to an opening in the 
diaphragm the joint is only 3500 mm long in the floor diaphragms. From the diaphragm above the ground 
floor to the diaphragm above the 5th floor a toothed joint is used, while the rest of the diaphragms got a full 
joint. Due to the support by a wall, these connections are only exposed to horizontal loads. The design of 
the joints is addressed in the following subsection. 
 
7.1.2.3.1. 
Full joint 
The diaphragms with a full joint is divided in the roof diaphragm and the floor diaphragms above the 6th 
and 7th floor, which is designed individual due to different thicknesses of the slabs. For the joints in the 
floor diaphragms the loads acting on the diaphragm above the 6th floor is used for the verification, since 
these are the most critical. Due to the full joint the loads are assumed to be evenly distributed along the 
length. In table 7.8 the characteristic loads acting on the joints is listed.

7.1.2.3.2. 
Toothed joint 
Where the horizontal diaphragms cross the walls above the ground floor to above the 5th floor toothed 
joints are used to avoid large compression stresses perpendicular to the direction of the fibers in the slab 
elements. The toothed joints are described and addressed in section 5.1.2 and 6.6. As mentioned earlier 
the length of the considered joint in line C on figure 7.6 is decreased from 4.21 meters to 3.5 meters, due to 
an opening in the diaphragms. Since the tooth in the end of the joint should be a wall tooth to allow the 
intensified compression reaction in the case of horizontal forces to be transferred, the length of the joint 
only make room for 7 slab teeth with a width of 250 mm. The horizontal loads in the diaphragms can only 
be transferred past through the joint via these 7 teeth. Due to this the required capacity can’t be achieved 
by solely using crossed screws, since the teethed geometry limits the dimensions and internal distance of 
screws that can be used. 
The problem is solved by resisting the shear force with five pairs of crossed screws in the five teeth located 
in the center and resisting the tensile force from the moment and normal force with a tension joint in the 
two teeth located closest to the ends of the joint. The tension joint consist af a solid timber board fastened 
to each side of the joint with screws. A solid timber board with the dimensions 600 x 250 x 50 is used as the 
tension board. In each end of the board 16 HBS screws Ø8.0 x 140 are used to transfer the tension force 
between the board and the CLT panels. For the crossed screws five pairs of VGZ screws Ø9.0 x 340 are used. 
Information about the screws can be found in [Rothoblaas, 2019b, p. 24] and [Rothoblaas, 2019b, p. 134]. 
The layout of the toothed joint with both crossed screws and tension joint is illustrated in figure 7.10, which 
is based on detail drawing 509 in the associated drawing folder. In the figure it is illustrated how the forces 
are distributed in the joint. 
The theory and equations used to design and verify both the crossed screws and the tension joint is 
reviewed in section 7.1.2.2 and will not be described further in this section. In general the tension joint 
work as a joint cover strip only exposed to a tension force perpendicular to the length of the joint, i.e. ‡D, 
while the crossed screws are only exposed to a shear force along the joint, i.e. ‡•. 
In table 7.10 the characteristic loads acting on the joint is listed, while the results from the design of the 
connection is listed in table 7.11. STR-8 with dominating wind load and instantaneous load duration, 
'5,| = 1.1, is the critical load combination. See appendix AA.9 for the calculations. In figure 7.11 the 
design of the tension joint is illustrated, which is based on detail drawing 511 in the associated drawing 
folder. The shear connection with the crossed screws can be found on detail drawing 510 in the associated 
drawing folder.  
Table 7.10 – Characteristic loads acting on the toothed joints.

7.2. 
Vertical load-bearing connections 
In this section the most important vertical load bearing connections are addressed and designed. Generally 
these connections focus on the support of the slabs. Compression stresses from connections where the 
slabs are resting on top af the walls is addressed in section 6.4 and will not be further described in this 
section. 
 
7.2.1. Support of slabs at exterior facade 
To achieve the maximal height for the lintels above the openings in the exterior walls, the cross-laminated 
timber slabs are mounted on the interior side of the facade walls. A common way of doing this is by using 
angle profiles of steel mounted horizontally along the inside of the walls. However this solution exposes the 
horizontal edge of the angle profile below the slabs. In case of fire the applied fire insulation consisting of 2 
x 15 mm fire-proof gypsum boards is not enough to prevent the temperature of the steel to reach 
temperatures where the supported timber may initiate charring, which could lead to structural failure of 
the CLT panels. Charring of timber due to high temperatures in timber to steel joint is further addressed on 
chapter 9. 
==PAGE:199==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
187 
 
Due to fire concerns a solution with concealed connectors is favorable. For the final design, a concealed 
connector in aluminium from Rothoblaas is used. The connector is named LOCK T FLOOR [Rothoblaas, 
2019a, p. 60], and is a hook connector that according to Rothoblaas is a quick and easy solution. The 
connector and the concealed solution is illustrated in figure 7.12. As illustrated on the figure a 22 mm deep 
recess is carved into the wall to make room for the connector. This is not a problem since the outer vertical 
layers of the wall is 40 mm thick, and only the horizontal layers are used for the load-bearing capacity of 
the lintels above the openings in the exterior walls. Neither is it a problem when considering the 
compressive strength of the vertical layers since the utilization rate is relatively low as addressed in section 
6.5.1.5. The connector consist of modules with a length of 300 mm, allowing the connector to fit with most 
geometries.  Each module consist of two parts, where one is mounted on the wall and the other is mounted 
on the front face of the slab.  Each module part is mounted on the wall or slab with 8x LBS screws Ø7.0 x 
80. The connection is installed by hooking the slab fitting from the top to the bottom. The installation 
method is illustrated in figure 7.13. Aluminium is an expensive material, and the price of the connector 
have not been investigated. However, by using a concealed connector, cost for extra fire-protective 
insulation is avoided. 
 
Figure 7.12 - Concealed connection [Rothoblaas, 2019a]. 
 
 
Figure 7.13 - Installation method of concealed connector [Rothoblaas, 2019a]. 
As illustrated on figure 7.14 the connectors are used along the full length of the exterior walls in the 
facades. The reason why the connectors are also used parallel to the primary load bearing direction of the 
slabs, is to limit the differentiated deflection at the walls, as well as to increase the stiffness of the slab 
structures, even though the contribution from the transverse stiffness of the CLT panels is not considered in 
the verification of the slabs in section 6.4.

appendix G.1. The characteristic reactions in the secondary load-bearing direction, also designated as the

in section 7.1.2.1.3.

7.2.2. Support of slabs on steel beams 
In the floor structure above the 7th floor, some of the CLT panels are supported by steel beams, as 
illustrated on figure 6.41 in section 6.8. The CLT panels are rebated at both top and bottom and installed on 
the lower flange of the beams. Screws are used to fix the CLT panels to the lower flange, thus ensuring that 
the CLT panels does not dislodge. The connection between the CLT panels and the steel beams is illustrated 
on figure 7.16, which is based on detail drawing 513 in the associated drawing folder. The most critical 
connection is slab zone ZFS1b resting on the lower flange of steel beam EB1S, which is located in module 
line B. The connection is the most critical because the CLT panel got the longest span of all panels 
supported by beams. The residual cross section of the rebated CLT panel depends of the geometry of the 
steel beam and the associated tolerances. By referring to table 9.2 in [Wallner-Novak, 2014] the limit for 
the maximal residual height of the 260 mm thick CLT panel is calculated by use of equation 7.21. The 
support length of the CLT panel on the lower flange also depends on the geometry and the tolerances. By 
likewise referring to table 9.2 in [Wallner-Novak, 2014] the limit for the maximal support length is 
calculated by use of equation 7.22.

7.3. 
Securing against wind suction 
The structural elements in the envelope of the building should be secured against suction forces from the 
wind. If proper anchoring is not ensured the elements will be torn of the building. In this section the 
anchoring of the facades and the roof is addressed. 
 
7.3.1. Facade anchoring 
The largest suction forces from the wind acting on the facades occur on the exterior walls parallel to the 
wind direction when the wind is coming from west, more specifically wind zone A illustrated on figure 4.13 
in section 4.4.6.  When combining the exterior wind pressure with the internal overpressure a total 
characteristic suction force of ,T5	 = −1.55 N/% is calculated. With a maximum floor height of 
ℎ= 3 %, the design line load in load combination STR-8 is calculated to ‡,T5	 =  ¡| ∗1.5 ∗,T5	 ∗
ℎ= −7.66 N/%.  
Since the CLT panels in the floor structure are mounted on the side of the exterior walls, the anchoring of 
the facade need to have the sufficient capacity to function as bracing for the walls. The aluminium 
connectors addressed in section 7.2.1, can’t resist horizontal or tension forces, thus these connectors can’t 
function as the bracing for the exterior walls. The bracing force at each floor is calculated according to 
section 9.2.5.2 in [DS/EN 1995-1-1, 2007]. By assuming that conditions applicable for glued-laminated 
timber and laminated veneer lumber is also applicable for cross-laminated timber, the design bracing force 
is calculated by use of equation 7.25 [DS/EN 1995-1-1, 2007].  
‡,xy	• = N

value of ‡ = 10.14 N/%. 
Two connections are required to secure the facade against the total horizontal force: a connection that 
anchors the exterior walls to the slabs, and a connection that joints the exterior walls on the floor below 
and above the slabs. For the anchoring of the exterior walls to the slabs, TITAN N – TTN240 angle brackets 
from Rothoblass is used [Rothoblaas, 2019a, p. 186]. The angle brackets in steel got a design tensile 
strength of ‡,- = 6.03 N in the instantaneous load duration. By placing the brackets with an internal 
distance of ² = 1000 %% on both sides of the slabs a utilization rate of õ = 0.84 is achieved, which is 
acceptable. For the joint between the floors, VGZ 9.0 x 260 screws from Rothoblass angled with 45o is used 
[Rothoblaas, 2019b, p. 134]. The screws resist the tension force by the axial load bearing capacity, which, 
with an angle of 45o to the load, is calculated to a design value of ‡y•,- = 2.81 N in the instantaneous 
load duration. By using an internal distance of ² = 250 %%, a utilization ratio of õ = 0.90 is achieved, 
which is acceptable. The calculations of the facade anchoring can be found in appendix AC.1. Both the angle 
brackets and the angled screws is illustrated in figure 7.15 in section 7.2.1.

7.3.2. Roof anchoring 
Like the facade the roof must be anchored to ensure that the suction force from the wind is not causing a 
lift-of. The largest upward force from the wind is applicable for wind zone F combined with internal 
overpressure, which got a characteristic value of  = −1.77 N/%. The stabilizing and favorable

8. 
Robustness 
The robustness of a construction must be treated to limit the risk and consequences of a progressive 
collapse due to failure of one or more structural elements. Annex E in [DS/EN 1990 DK NA, 2019] sets up 
the guidelines of how to verify the robustness of a structure located in Denmark. The extent of the 
requirements regarding the verification of the robustness of a construction depends on the assigned 
consequences class, and only in case of the high consequences class, CC3, which is applicable for the 
concerned building, a documentation of the robustness is a requirement [DS/EN 1990 DK NA, 2019]. 
A construction can be classified as robust: 
• 
If the critical parts of the structure is only slightly sensitive to accidental impact and defects, or 
• 
If no comprehensive failure of the structure occurs when a limited part of structure fails. 
Accidental impacts and defects include unpredicted load impacts, accidental deviations of the actual static 
behavior and the applied design models, unpredicted deviations of the executed project and the project 
material, unforeseen geometrical imperfections, etc. [DS/EN 1990 DK NA, 2019]. 
A robust structure is achieved by appropriate choice of materials, overall static principle, and structural 
system. If the structure include any key-elements of significant importance for the overall robustness of the 
construction, these should be proper designed. A key-element is a limited part of the structure, which got a 
central role in the structural system. In general the aim should be to verify the robustness of a structure 
without utilization of key-elements, since these could introduce critical points in the system [DS/EN 1990 
DK NA, 2019]. 
The robustness of a construction can be documented by either one of the following three criteria: 
• 
Demonstrating that the critical parts of the structure, i.e. the key-elements, are only slightly 
sensitive to accidental load impacts and defects. 
• 
Demonstrating that no comprehensive failure of the structure occurs if a limited part of the 
structure fails. This method is also referred to as loss of element. 
• 
Demonstrating that the key-elements got sufficient safety to achieve the same safety as the 
corresponding structure verified by the loss of element criteria. 
Since key-elements should be avoided, the concerned building is designed without these, thus the second 
criteria by demonstrating that no comprehensive failure occurs by loss of element is used to verify the 
robustness. When verifying by loss of element, the extent of expected collapse, due to failure of a limited 
part of the structure, must not exceed a limit defined in [DS/EN 1990 DK NA, 2019]. The maximal accepted 
extend of collapse is defined as 15 percent of the floor area on each of two stories located directly above 
each other. The collapse area must however not exceed 240 m2 on each story, and 360 m2 in total. 
Additional it should be demonstrated that the structure can redistribute the load from the failing building 
part to the surrounding structures. The load-bearing capacity of the surrounding structures should be 
verified in the accidental design situation by use of load combination ACC-4 ('5 = 1.1) as defined in 
table 3.34 in section 3.7.1. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:208==
Andreas Juul Larsen

8.1. 
Loss of element 
Robustness can be demonstrated by the loss of element criteria if the limits for the extend of the collapse 
defined earlier is complied with when either an arbitrary column or an arbitrary 3 meter long wall piece 
collapses. Since the structural system of the building does not include any columns, the worst case scenario 
is a situation where a 3 meter long wall piece is removed. Two walls is investigated: interior wall WIY2 
located between module line 2 and 3, and exterior wall WE2 located in module line 4. The interior wall is 
critical because it supports one of the slabs with the longest spans, while resisting the larges vertical forces 
of all walls in the building. The exterior wall is critical because it includes openings which combined with the 
removed 3 meter wall piece creates a very long opening, while also supporting slabs with a relatively long 
span. The lintels above the initial openings in the exterior wall fails due to the removed 3 meter wall piece, 
thus a part of the supported slabs with a total width of 6.012 meters collapses. The location of the removed 
wall pieces and the corresponding collapsed slab areas is illustrated in figure 8.1 and 8.2.

8.1.1. Interior wall

designed to function as an individual upright girder with a span of at least 3 meters. The upright girders are 
designed to have the sufficient load-bearing capacity to redistribute the vertical load to the residual walls.

8.1.2. Exterior wall 
The situation when removing a 3 meter wall piece from the exterior wall WE2 is very similar to the situation 
described in section 8.1.1, where a 3 meter wall piece was removed from the interior wall WIY2. Like the 
interior walls, the exterior walls on each floor are also designed to individually span across the accidental 
opening and redistribute the loads to the residual load-bearing elements. The difference is, however, that 
only the height equivalent to the height of the lintels can be used do redistribute the loads due to the 
openings in the exterior walls. The openings also causes the accidental opening to be far larger than the 3 
meter wide wall piece which in the worst case is removed. The situation is illustrated in figure 8.4, where 
the lintel that should redistribute the forces on each floor is indicated with a blue color.

found in appendix AD.2. The related connections are designed in section 8.1.3. 
The result indicated that lintels in all the exterior walls can span at least 6 meters. Due to this the lintels on 
the floors above the LVL lintel, which carries load from multiple floors and is designed in section 6.7, can 
support the full load on the concerned floors in the accidental design situation. This means that a collapse 
of the LVL lintel does not cause a progressive collapse, and therefore the LVL lintel does not need to be 
designed as a key-element.

wall joint.  Since the reaction from the loss of element in the exterior wall is the largest of the two cases 
investigated in section 8.1.1 and 8.1.2, ‡,, = 60.48 N versus  ‡,, = 50.62 N, see appendix AD.1-2, this

used [Rothoblaas, 2019b, p 134]. The theory and the calculation method is equivalent to how the shear 
capacity was calculated for the crossed screws in section 7.1.2.2.1. 
For the t-joint 4 pieces of TITAN S – TTS240 steel angle brackets from Rothoblaas is used [Rothoblaas, 
2019a, p. 2014]. A vibration resilient profile are added to limit sound transmissions through the brackets. 
For the vertical wall joint a joint cover strip of LVL 27x160 fastened with HBS screws Ø8.0 x 100 from

9. 
Fire 
In this chapter the fire related topics are described. The focus of the chapter is primarily the structural fire 
safety design, but initially the more general fire safety related topics are treated. The fire safety design is 
analyzed according to chapter 5 in the Danish Building Regulations 2018, BR18. Paragraph 82 of [BR18, 
2018] most importantly states that buildings should be designed in a way that in the case of fire ensures a 
safe evacuation of occupants, reasonable working conditions for the rescue services, sufficient load-bearing 
capacity of structural elements, and use of suitable materials for surfaces and building parts. 
 
9.1. 
Classifications 
Paragraph 84 in [BR18, 2018] states that buildings should be divided in one or more buildings sections, 
which afterwards should be assigned to a utilization class and a risk class. The two classes are used to 
determine the minimum requirements for the fire safety design. The utilization class is defined by: if the 
building section is designed for overnight stays, if occupants have knowledge about the escape routes, if 
occupants can bring themselves to safety, and if the building section is designed for more than 50 people. 
For the concerned building designed for residential use, all building sections are assigned to utilization class 
4 [BR18, 2018]. 
The risk class is defined by the use of the building and the complexity of evacuation of occupants, with 
regards to the design and the fire load. For the concerned building in utilization class 4 and the level of the 
floor on the top story located between 9.6 and 45 meters above the terrain, all building sections are 
assigned to risk class 3 [BR18, 2018]. The fire safety design of a building can either be designed by use of 
prescriptive regulations, based on experiences from past fires, or by use of a performance-based approach. 
For the concerned building, the fire safety is analyzed by use of prescriptive regulations valid for residential 
housing, which is described in [Præ-accepterede løsninger, 2018]. The prescriptive regulations are based on 
the standard fire curve, which is described in section 9.2.1. 
The fire reaction properties of building products and materials are classified according to the European 
Classification System in EN 13501-1 [DS/INF 147, 2003]. The system consists of a main class describing the 
flammability of the product, one sub-class denoted s1, s2, or s3, describing the smoke potential of the 
product, and one sub-class denoted d0, d1, or d3, describing the potential for burning droplets dropping 
from the material. The main classes are named A1, A2, B, C, D, E and F, with A1 indicating a non-
combustible material, E indicating a highly flammable product, and F indicating a product that have not 
been tested. The residual four classes indicates products with increasing flammability from class A1 to class 
E. Regarding the sub-classes a lower number indicates better fire properties [DS/INF 147, 2003]. 
The fire resistance of load-bearing element, fire-protective elements, and fire separative elements are 
classified according to the European Classification System in EN 13501-2 [DS/INF 147, 2003]. The relevant 
classifications are: 
• 
R – Load-bearing capacity: The ability of a structural element to maintain its load-bearing capacity. 
• 
E – Integrity: The ability of an element to avoid the fire from spreading from the exposed side to the 
unexposed side due to smoke and flames. 
• 
I – Insulation: The ability of an element to avoid the fire from spreading from the exposed side to the 
unexposed side due to heat transfer. 
==PAGE:214==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
202 
 
The classifications are given a number that indicated the period in minutes where the element should 
retain the ability. In Denmark, the classification periods of 30, 60 and 120 minutes are used. 
Buildings should be separated in fire cells and fire sections to limit the risk of fire and smoke spreading, as 
well as increase the available evacuation time. Building elements used to separate fire cells should be 
classified as at least EI 60, while building elements used to separate fire sections should be classified as at 
least EI 60 A2-s1,d0 [Præ-accepterede løsninger, 2018]. Both classification indicate that the building 
elements should maintain a fire separating function in at least 60 minutes. The difference of the two 
classification is that it is allowed to use flammable materials in building elements used to separate fire cells 
but not in building elements used to separate fire sections. Timber is a flammable materials, which means 
that it can’t be used in fire section separating elements if the prescriptive regulations are used. This is 
however ignored in this project. For further work a performance based analysis of the building should be 
made to validate the use of timber in the fire section separating elements. In figure 9.1 it is illustrated how 
a normal floor is separated in fire cells and fire sections, according to the provided architectural material. 
 
Figure 9.1 - Fire sectioning. 
The classification period of the load-bearing capacity of structural elements depends on the level of the 
floor structure on the highest located floor above the terrain. For buildings with the floor structure of the 
top floor located between 12 and 45 meters above terrain the following periods are applicable [Præ-
accepterede løsninger, 2018]. 
• 
Load-bearing elements on the top floor 
R 60 
• 
Load-bearing elements on the other floors 
R 120 A2-s1,d0 
• 
Balconies 
 
 
R 60 
The classification for the load-bearing elements on all other floors than the top floor is only valid if non-
combustible materials is used. Due to this requirement, a performance-based approach using a parametric 
fire is required to validate the design. In this project the capacity of the load-bearing elements is both 
investigated in the case of a standard fire and in the case of a parametric fire. The two types of design fires 
and the associated design methods are described in the following section.  
==PAGE:215==

9.2. 
Structural fire safety 
The structural fire safety of the building is ensured by verifying the load-bearing capacity of the structural 
elements when exposed to a post flashover fire. This is done by applying a relevant design fire, calculating 
the temperature development, analyzing the temperature related effects on the material properties, and at 
last verifying the structural elements for the load acting in the design fire situation. 
 
9.2.1. Design fires 
In this project the structural elements have been analyzed for two types of design fires: the standard fire 
and the parametric fire. For both types of fires it is assumed that fire only develops in one fire cell at a time, 
see figure 9.1, and the separating building elements maintain the separating ability trough the full duration 
of the fire. This means that fire separating slab elements are only exposed to the fire from the bottom, 
while fire separating vertical members are only exposed to fire from one side. In case of other elements the 
fire exposure depends on the geometry and location.     
 
9.2.1.1. Standard fire 
The most common and least complicated of the design fires is the ISO834 standard fire. The prescriptive 
regulations described in section 9.1 relates to the standard fire, which are used to declare and categorize 
fire properties of building products and materials. The purpose of the standard fire is only to ensure that 
the structure maintains the sufficient load-bearing capacity as long as the evacuation of occupants and the 
actions from the rescue services is ongoing. The fire is assumed to increase towards infinity and does not 
include a cooling phase. By using the standard fire it is accepted what the structural elements might fail 
after the required period where the sufficient load-bearing capacity should be maintained. The applicable 
required periods, also called resistance classes, are listed in section 9.1. The standard fire does not consider 
the geometry of the fire compartment, nor the presence and amount of combustible materials. This 
indicates that the use of the standard fire design could both give conservative and unconservative result 
depending on the concerned situation. Modern buildings are often constructed tighter and with more 
thermal insulation compared to when the resistance classes was determined, thus a design based on these 
classes could prove to be unsuitable in some cases. The temperature development of the gas is nominal 
and described by equation 9.1 [DS/EN 1991-1-2, 2007]. In figure 9.2 the temperature development of a 
standard fire is illustrated. On the figure the temperature after 60 and 120 minutes of fire development is 
indicated. This is the two resistance classes which are of relevance for this project.

9.2.1.2. Parametric fire 
A parametric fire design is applied to consider the actual conditions of the fire compartment, including 
geometry, openings, fire load and thermal inertia of surfaces. The development of fires is controlled by the 
energy balance, which is defined by the amount of energy that enters the fire from combustible materials, 
and the amount of energy that disappears through openings and surfaces. The parametric fire is a fully 
developed fire curve that includes both the heating and the cooling phase. This makes it suitable for 
designing the structural elements to maintain the sufficient load-bearing capacity through a full fire 
development, ensuring that the structures won’t fail at any time due to fire exposure. The parametric fire 
curve defined in annex A in [DS/EN 1991-1-2 DK NA, 2014] is applied for this project, which is applicable for 
fire compartments with a floor area up to 500 m2 without openings in the roof and with a maximal height 
of four meters [DS/EN 1991-1-2, 2007]. The fire development is defined by equation 9.2 [DS/EN 1991-1-2 
DK NA, 2014].
