The fire load density considers the presence and amount of combustible material in a fire compartment 
compared to the total surface area. An increase of the fire load density results in a fire curve with a higher 
maximum temperature that peaks later and have a longer cooling phase. For dwellings, the fire load density 
can be determined as p = 200 .ù/% according to annex E in [DS/EN 1991-1-2 DK NA, 2014]. 
In case of structural members of timber, concrete and insulated steel a fire with high temperatures, a slow 
development and a long cooling phase is the most critical, because it gives the best conditions for the heat 
to be transmitted through the insulating layers. The most critical fire is developed in the case of a low 
opening factor, a low thermal inertia, and a high fire load density. This means that a fire compartment with 
a large surface area, a low number of openings, a well-insulated envelope, and a high amount of 
combustible material, is often resulting in the most critical conditions regarding the fire situation. 
In the case of the concerned building all fire cells illustrated in figure 9.1 is considered a fire compartment. 
All the fire compartments are more or less identical regarding the fire properties, and therefore the fire 
compartment illustrated in figure 9.3 are used for the analyze of the parametric fire situation. In table 9.1 
the calculated fire properties are listed, while the parametric fire curve is illustrated in figure 9.4. The fire 
compartment only got a few openings, which is why the minimum value for the opening factor is utilized. 
Due to well insulated surrounding surfaces the fire compartment got a relatively low thermal inertia. By 
combining the calculated fire properties a slow developing fire with a high maximal temperature is 
developed, which as described earlier, results in a critical situation in case of fire. The parametric fire curve 
and the maximal temperatures are calculated in appendix AE.7-9 and AE.11.

9.2.2. Timber 
Even though timber is a combustible material, that will ignite when exposed to a fire, timber structures 
have good and predictable fire properties. Timber structures of a reasonable size burns slowly with a steady 
progress of charring, and often preserves its stiffness and load bearing capacity even after an advanced 
time of fire exposure. The ignition temperature of plane timber surfaces is often stated to be 280 oC, which 
corresponds to the temperature where structural timber by general experience will ignite after 5 minutes 
of fire exposure [Hertz, 2017]. Timber burns by means of an endothermic process of pyrolysis, which 
develops gas that penetrates the material to the surface and flows to the ambient air. The gas then reacts 
exothermic with the oxygen in the air, and a part of the developed heat interns the material again to 
support a new pyrolysis process. At the surface of the timber specimen the fire exposed timber are 
transformed to charcoal which have no strength and got an insulating effect on the material behind. Behind 
the charred layer the pyrolysis zone is located, where temperatures are between 250 and 350 oC [Svenskt 
Trä, 2017]. The material in the pyrolysis zone got reduced strength and stiffness properties. Behind the 
pyrolysis zone the unburned material are unaffected. At wide cracks and outer corners the charring depth 
will be larger, compared to the uniform surface [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. Also the use of steel fittings and 
fasteners increase the charring depth locally, since unprotected steel can heat rapidly to temperatures 
above the ignition point for timber [Hertz, 2017]. To protect the timber from charring, fire-proof insulation 
can be applied. In this project all structural timber elements are protected by 2 x 15 mm fire-proof gypsum 
boards of type F, both to protect the elements, but also to reduce the fire load in the initial phase of a fire. 
==PAGE:220==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
208 
 
[DS/EN 1995-1-2, 2007] includes two methods for calculating the load-bearing capacity of structural timber 
elements in the case of fire: The reduced cross-section method, and the reduced properties method. In 
both methods the residual load-bearing capacity is calculated based on the residual cross-section, which is 
determined by subtracting the charring depth from the initial cross-section on all fire exposed sides. The 
methods differs in the way the residual strength of the pyrolysis zone is considered. In the reduced cross-
section method the area of the pyrolysis zone is subtracted from the unaffected residual cross-section, 
while the reduced strength of the pyrolysis zone is considered by reducing the properties of the non-
charred cross-section in the reduced properties method. The reduced cross-section method is applied in 
the case of the ISO834 standard fire and will be described further in section 9.2.2.1. In case of the 
parametric design fire, only a further developed version of the reduced properties can be applied, as 
further described in section 9.2.2.2. 
When utilizing engineered wood products consisting of lamellas glued together the properties of the 
adhesives when exposed to high temperatures should be considered. If non heat-proof adhesives like 
polyurethane are utilized detachment of partial areas of the charred layers may occur, and delaminating 
must be considered, which result in an increased and varying charring rate [Wallner-Novak, 2014]. If heat-
proof adhesives like melanin-urea-formaldehyde are utilized delamination is assumed not to happen, and a 
steady charring rate is applicable [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. 
          
9.2.2.1. Standard fire 
As mentioned earlier the reduced cross-section method are used to calculate the residual load-bearing 
capacity in the case of the standard fire. The effective unaffected cross-section is determined by 
subtracting the effective charring depth from all fire exposed sides. The effective charring depth is 
calculated by use of equation 9.7 and is illustrated for a three-sided fire exposed beam in figure 9.5 [DS/EN 
1995-1-2, 2007].

As stated earlier all timber elements is protected by 2 x 15 mm fire-proof gypsum boards of type F. In the 
case of timber elements protected with this type of fire-insulation the nominal charring depth is calculated 
by use of equation 9.9. The timeline for the development of charring for protected timber elements can be 
divided in four periods. The first period runs from the time of ignition until the temperature behind the 
insulation reach a level where charring of the timber initiates, denoted P8. The second period runs from 
the time the charring initiates until the time when the insulation fails, denoted P. Conservatively the time 
when the insulation fails is set to P = P8, thus the second period is eliminated. The third period runs from 
the insulation fails until the charring depth reaches the minimum of 25 mm and the depth of the equivalent 
unprotected charring, denoted Py. In this period the charring rate is doubled due to uncharred timber being 
exposed to hot gasses. The final period runs from the time when the increased charring ends until the 
considered period of fire exposure are reached. In figure 9.7 the variation of charring depth over time when

In [DS/EN 1995-1-2, 2007] the depth of the pyrolysis zone for timber structures exposed to fire in more 
than 20 minutes is determined as: ¼2 = 7 %%. Regarding cross-laminated timber, tests and simulations 
have shown that this is not always a reasonable value to apply. For CLT members the depth of the pyrolysis 
zone depends on the number of layers that the element consist of, the function of the element, the type of 
stress that are applied to the fire exposed surface, and if the element is protected or not [Svenskt Trä, 
2017]. In table 9.2 all relevant values for the depth of the pyrolysis zone to be applied for the CLT elements 
is listed. Since all CLT elements in the final design is protected, only these values are applied. The values are 
calculated by use table 7.4 in [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. The calculations of the depth of the pyrolysis zone can be 
found in appendix AE.1-6. The project contains cross-laminated timber elements of 5, 7 and 8 layers, but to 
simplify the calculation, the values of the pyrolysis depth valid for elements with 5 layers is conservatively 
applied for all elements. Only values valid for the tension side of horizontal elements, e.g. slabs or beams, 
and the compression side of vertical elements, e.g. walls, are relevant. This is because the horizontal 
members are only exposed at the bottom edge where tension stresses are present, while one-sided 
exposed walls always will bend away from the fire, causing compression stresses at the fire exposed side.

From the results in table 9.3 it can be concluded that the decrease of the nominal charring depth is minimal 
when only applying one layer of insulation. This is due to the increased charring rate when the insulation 
fails. By applying an extra layer the charring depth is decreased significantly. Due to this 2 x 15 mm fire-
proof gypsum boards are applied for all timber elements. The charring depth is further decreased by 
applying more insulations layer, but this increases the material use and space consumption, which is 
undesirable. 
Delamination have a large effect on the charring rate as soon as the charring depth exceeds the thickness 
of the outer lamella. The increase is most significant with thin lamellas, non or little fire insulation, and long 
durations of fire exposure. The more lamellas that are included in the charring layer, the bigger are the 
impact from the delamination compared to when delamination is avoided. From the results it can be 
concluded that by ensuring that the adhesives are heat-proof are more robust design is achieved in case of 
fires. The calculations of the charring depths can be found in appendix AE.1-6 for cross-laminated timber 
and appendix AE.10 for glued-laminated timber and laminated veneer lumber.  
As mentioned earlier the strength of the effective cross section is unaffected, thus equation 3.11 and 3.12 
in section 3.5.1.5.4 with '5, = 1.00 and 01, = 1.0 is used to determine the design strengths and 
stiffnesses, when designing by use of the standard fire and the reduced cross-section method.

9.2.2.2. Parametric fire 
As mentioned earlier a further developed version of the strength reduction method is used to calculate the 
residual load-bearing capacity of structural timber elements when applying a parametric fire. In the 
strength reduction method only the depth of the charred layer is removed, while the strength of the 
residual cross section is reduced. In this method monodimensional charring is applied instead of nominal 
charring, while rounded corners should be subtracted from the residual cross-section. For guidance of how 
to calculate the geometry of the rounded corners, reference is made to [Hertz, 2017]. In the case of the 
parametric fire the charring rate is calculated by use of equation 9.13 [Hertz, 2017].

The maximum duration of burning in the concerned case is calculated to: P = 175 %Æ‚. As equation 9.14 
indicated, the development of the charring depth with the time when considering the parametric fire is not 
linear as was the case with the standard fire. Instead the charring depth grows rapidly in the initial phase 
and later flattens out. [DS/EN 1995-1-2, 2007] does not contain information about how to incorporate fire 
insulation in the calculations, thus methods described elsewhere must be used. 
The topic is dealt with in the report “Engineering methods for structural fire design of wood buildings – 
structural integrity during a full natural fire” based on a project by RISE Research Institutes of Sweden and 
presented by Brandforsk in 2018. The main topic of the report is how to avoid a progressive structural 
collapse of tall timber buildings. Structural collapse as a result of fire is rare, but if it happens, it often leads 
to high property loss, especially in case of tall buildings. If the consequences of a structural collapse is large, 
it is often desired to analyze and prove that the concerned building can withstand a fully developed fire. 
Methods and guidelines on the topic is available for structures of steel and concrete, but are lacking for tall 
timber buildings [Brandon, 2018]. 
In contrast to steel and concrete, structural elements of timber is combustible and can contribute to the 
fire load. If the building is not proper designed, there may be a risk, that the fully developed phase of the 
fire continuous until the timber building is burned down, and no more combustible material is present. The 
report presented by [Brandon, 2018] includes a new analytical design approach for tall timber buildings in 
relation to a fully developed parametric fire, with the purpose to ensure a robust structure with limited risk 
of large structural consequences [Brandon, 2018]. 
To avoid a progressive collapse in case of fire the following aspects must be ensured [Brandon, 2018]: 
• 
Robust separations between fire compartments. 
• 
Conditions that lead to a decay phase and self-extinguishing of the fire. 
• 
A structure that can withstand a fully developed parametric fire until self-extinguishing.  
The first aspect is assumed to be complied with though the applied fire sectioning of the building. The 
second aspect can be complied with by ensuring that the amount of combustible material is limited, and do 
not increase during the fire exposure. Parametric design fires include a fully developed phase and a decay 
phase, but does not consider a possible second flashover, that could happen if new combustible material 
are added to the fire. When considering timber elements, this can either happen because of delamination 
due to non-heat-proof adhesives, or because the fire protection of initially protected elements fails. Both 
these cases are causing cold timber surfaces to become exposed to the hot temperatures. This could in 
many cases result in a second flashover, due to an increase in the combustible material available for the fire 
[Brandon, 2018]. The third aspect is generally applicable when a parametric fire is addressed and is 
complied with by using proper design methods. 
==PAGE:228==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
216 
 
In most cases encapsulating of the timber elements with fire insulating materials are required to ensure a 
proper structure in regard to structural fire safety. A few uninsulated timber surfaces are possible to 
include in the analysis, but then modifications of the parametric curves is required, which make the process 
far more comprehensive. In general, all timber must be covered with insulation material to assume a usual 
parametric course of the fire. The report states that the insulation material must be designed with enough 
thickness to ensure that the timber structures are protected and do not start charring during the full fire 
[Brandon, 2018]. However, this demand have been found to be too restrictive when applying the 
parametric fire as it is stated in [DS/EN 1995-1-2 DK NA, 2019], due to a prolonged cooling phase compared 
to the one applied in [DS/EN 1995-1-2, 2007]. The demand is omitted in this project, but for further work 
the topic should be further investigated. Instead the analytical method presented in the report is used to 
calculate when charring of initially protected timber elements begins. 
Studies have found that fire-proof gypsum boards of type F in general fails then the temperature on the 
unexposed side of the boards reaches a temperature between 300oC and 500oC. Conservatively it is 
assumed that the gypsum boards fall of then the temperature behind the board reaches a temperature of 
300oC [Brandon, 2018]. The temperature development through the gypsum boards are determined by a 
model that utilizes one-dimensional numerical calculations. Both the gypsum layers and the timber element 
is divided in lamellas of equal thickness. Constant heat distribution across the individual lamella is assumed. 
When the temperature on the unexposed side of a gypsum board reaches 300oC, all lamellas representing 
the board is removed from the model before the calculations are continued. The charring of the timber 
element begins immediately after the base layer of the insulation falls of, except if the temperature of the 
gas have decreased to below 300oC when the fall-of occurs. 
The heat is transferred from the gas to the exposed surface by convection and radiation. The convective 
and radiative heat transfer is calculated with use of equation 9.17 [Brandon, 2018]. After a layer of 
insulation falls of, the exposed surface is moved to the next insulation layer before the calculation is 
continued. 
p	 = pû + pû " = ℎ ∗Ò–• −–Ó + • ∗ì ∗R–•4 −–4S

The most precise results is obtained by use of very thin lamellas, as well as including the full thickness of 
the timber element. This allows the model to consider the heat transfer from the timber to the ambient air 
on the unexposed side. To simplify the model and limit the amount of required calculations, a lamella 
thickness of 5 mm is used, thus a gypsum board with a thickness of 15 mm is divided in three lamellas. Only 
a few lamellas of the timber located closest to the exposed side is included in the calculations, thus the 
heat transfer to the ambient air in the unexposed side is omitted. In figure 9.8 the optimal analytical model 
is illustrated as it is applied in [Brandon, 2018]. The figure is included in this report to give an overview of 
the method, even though other values are applied to simplify the calculations. The figure is a schematic 
drawing of the model when two layers of fire-proof gypsum boards are applied.

Material properties for gypsum boards is determined for a design temperature of 500oC, while a design

average temperature of the material through the analysis. Values for the material properties are taken

calculations are stabile through the full numerical analysis. The time when charring initiates is calculates for

From the results listed in table 9.6 it can be seen that the effect from adding only one layer of insulations is 
relatively large, while the effect from adding extra layers is relatively small. This is because the layers 
located behind the initially exposed layer, is already heated up due to heat transfer through the layers in 
front. The fall-off time of the outer layers of gypsum boards decreases when extra layers are added. This is 
because the gypsum boards got a higher thermal resistance than the timber, thus decreasing the amount of 
heat that is transferred from the outer gypsum board layers to the inner layers. Therefore the rate of which 
the outer layers are heating is increased compared to the situation before the extra layers where added. 
As with the standard fire in section 9.2.2.1 the charring depth have been investigated for a number of cases 
with different amounts of insulations applied, and with different cases of delamination. In table 9.7 the 
calculated monodimensional charring depths from each case is listed. As mentioned earlier 2 x 15 mm fire-
proof gypsum boards of type F is applied for fire protection of all timber elements. Since all relevant timber 
materials got a monodimensional charring rate of Ñ2 = 0.65 %%/%Æ‚ a monodimensional charring depth 
of ¼8y,2 = 60.8 %% is applicable for all timber elements included in the project. The value is obviously 
only valid as long as delamination is avoided by use of heat-proof adhesives. The calculations of the 
charring depths can be found in appendix AE.7-9 for cross-laminated timber and appendix AE.11 for glued-
laminated timber and laminated veneer lumber.

The effect from delamination matches the results listed in table 9.3 in section 9.2.2.1 obtained from the 
analysis when applying the standard fire. The effect increases with an increased number af layers included 
in the charring depth, and elements with thin layers is more sensitive to delamination compared to 
elements with thicker layers. Especially when using uninsulated elements or elements protected by only a 
thin layer of insulation the delamination have a very large effect and could possibly double the charring 
depth. This would in most cases decrease the residual load bearing capacity of the element significantly. In 
general the results may imply that delamination should not be combined with low levels of fire protection, 
while high levels of fire insulation could compensate for some of the extra charring depth as a consequence 
of delamination.

9.2.3. Steel 
Steel is a material that got a very large thermal conductivity, e.g. Ð = 35 −60 ©/%/, thus it is assumed 
that the temperature of steel members are constant across the cross-section. The temperature of 
uninsulated steel almost follows the temperature of the fire, which is why steel members are almost always 
insulated. The cross-section of steel members are not decreased during fire, as it was the case with timber, 
but the properties reduce significantly at high temperatures. When steel is in contact with timber, charring 
due to heat transfer from steel to timber should be considered. When the steel reaches the ignition 
temperature of 280oC, the surface of the timber in contact with the steel starts charring. This makes 
connections between steel and timber critical in the fire situation, since these connections often contain 
several steel parts, which initiates charring of the timber from multiple direction. To avoid this a maximal 
allowed temperature for the steel to reach in the case of fire is defined as –,B' = 2805_. Calculation 
methods for calculating the extend of charring in steel to timber connections exist, but the method of 
limiting the maximum steel temperature is assessed to be the most effective and robust way of verifying 
the design. The limit temperature is complied with by applying the required amount of fire resistant 
insulation. Fire-proof gypsum boards of type F with the parameters listed in table 9.8 is used. A design 
temperature of –
•	 = 5005_, corresponding to the approximate temperature of the fire, is used when 
determining the properties. Values from table 4 in [Brandon, 2018] is used for the calculations. All three 
sizes of steel cross-sections described in section 6.8 are analyzed according to the maximum temperature.

The development of the steel temperature is analyzed by both applying the standard fire and the

standard fire and the parametric fire is illustrated in figure 9.9 and 9.10, respectively. The calculations can

support parts of the floor structure above the 7th floor. The requirements for these are that the steel 
temperature must not exceed the temperature limit of –,B' = 2805_ after 120 minutes of the standard 
fire, corresponding to resistance class R 120, and neither exceed the limit during the full duration of the 
fully developed parametric fire. The steel cross-section of the type HE140B are used as cantilevered beams 
to support the balconies. The requirements for this cross-section is that the steel temperature must not 
exceed the temperature limit of –,B' = 2805_ after 60 minutes of the standard fire, corresponding to 
resistance class R 60, and neither exceed the limit during the first 60 minutes of the fully developed 
parametric fire. The reason why the steel temperature is allowed to exceed the limit temperature after 60 
minutes, is because the consequences of a single balcony collapsing is assumed small, and it is relatively 
easy to replace the affected balconies after a fire. All steel beams are exposed to fire from two sides, the 
bottom and from one of the sides. From the results of the analysis the time when the steel temperature of 
the HE140B cross-section in the case of the parametric fire exceeds the limit temperature is determined as: 
PR2805_S = 105 %Æ‚. From the results in table 9.9 it is concluded that the requirements are complied 
with, and no non-expected charring will initiate.

10. 
Heat and moisture 
The enclosure is an important part of a building and serve as a protective barrier between the outdoor and 
the indoor environment. Often differences in temperature, air pressure, and moisture content are present, 
which should be considered when designing the enclosure. Assemblies including cross-laminated timber 
panels should be designed by use of the same principles as all other building materials. However, CLT as a 
building system got a number of unique characteristics, including good properties regarding thermal 
insulation, heat storage and airtightness. CLT is highly sensitive to moisture and should be protected by 
properly designed wall and roof assemblies [Karacabeyli, 2013]. In this chapter some of the most important 
heat and moisture related topics when designing with CLT are studied. 
 
10.1. Thermal insulation 
In most cases a temperature difference between the outside and the inside environment is present. This 
causes heat from the warmest side to flow through the enclosure to the coldest side. To achieve and 
maintain a satisfying stabile indoor temperature it is often necessary to implement heating or cooling 
which requires energy. To limit the heat loss and the energy consumption, the enclosure should be 
designed with a thermal insulation at an acceptable level. The thermal insulation of a building component is 
defined by the thermal transmittance, õ [©/% ], calculated by use of equation 10.1. The lower the 
thermal transmittance, the better the thermal insulation. 
õ = 1
½ =
1
½¼/Ð 
(10.1)
Where: 
õ: 
Thermal transmittance of the building component [W/m2K]. 
½: 
Summarized thermal resistance [m2K/W]. 
Ð: 
Thermal conductivity [W/mK]. 
The thermal conductivity defines the amount of energy that passes through a material under a gradient in 
temperature. The thermal conductivity of timber depends on many variables including moisture content, 
density, and grain orientation. For enclosure application, the heat flow is generally perpendicular to the 
grain direction, while a moisture content in most cases can be assumed of 12 percent [Karacabeyli, 2013]. 
In table 10.1 the thermal conductivities of selected materials are listed [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. Cross-laminated 
timber got a thermal conductivity that are approximately 13 times lower than for concrete and only 
approximately 3 times higher than for insulation. This indicates that cross-laminated timber can add a fair 
amount of thermal insulation to a building part, and in this sense is superior to concrete. However, to 
comply with the limits for the maximal permitted values of the thermal transmittance listed in the Danish 
Building regulation [BR18], additional insulation must be added to the enclosure. Regarding new buildings 
the limit values for the exterior walls and the roof structure is õ = 0.30 ©/%  and õ = 0.20 ©/% . 
Table 10.1 - Thermal conductivity of selected materials [Svenskt Trä, 2017].

224 
 
10.2. Heat storage 
Test and simulations have shown that buildings made by CLT got a low energy consumption for heating, 
and at the same time are pleasant to live in for the occupant. One of the reasons for these results is the 
high specific heat capacity of timber, q [ù/O ], which define how much energy is required to increase the 
temperature of one unit of mass by one degree [Karacabeyli, 2013]. All types of materials adapts to the 
temperature of the surroundings by either absorbing or emitting energy, depending on the heat balance. 
This contribute to a stabilized room temperature, without large fluctuations. If a material got a large 
specific heat capacity it are able to store large amounts of heat and release it slowly to the surroundings if 
the temperature decreases, and thus help keep the temperature at a satisfying level. The same applies if 
the temperature of the surroundings increases, just with the opposite direction of the heat transfer. As a 
consequence of this, surfaces of CLT is often pleasant to touch, since their temperature is fairly constant 
with the temperature of the indoor environment [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. In table 10.2 the specific heat 
capacity and periodic heating depth of specific materials is listed. The periodic heating depth indicates the 
active depth of a material, that takes part in a temperature cycle of one day [Svenskt Trä, 2017].  
Table 10.2 – Specific heat capacity and periodic heating depth of selected materials [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. 
Material 
Specific heat capacity, c [J/kgK] 
Periodic heating depth [mm] 
Cross-laminated timber 
1600 
70 
Gypsum 
720 
90 
Masonry 
1350 
110 
Concrete 
1000 
140 
Mineral wool 
120 
160 
 
The results in table 10.2 indicates that cross-laminated timber is a superior material regarding heat storage 
and stabilization of the temperature fluctuations in the indoor environment. By utilizing CLT elements 
instead for other material like concrete a more pleasant indoor environment should be achieved, while the 
energy consumptions should be decreased. The periodic heating depth indicated that a thinner elements of 
CLT compared to concrete is required to stabilize the temperature deviation. If extra thermal Insulation is 
added to the enclosure, it should be placed on the exterior side of the CLT panel to utilize the heat storage 
properties as much as possible.   
 
10.3. Moisture 
As mentioned earlier, timber products, including CLT, is sensitive to moisture and should be protected 
against the outdoor environment. Many physical and mechanical properties of wood vary with the 
moisture content. In building service, wood is exposed to both seasonal and daily changes in the 
temperature and relative humidity, which results in a fluctuating moisture content. To match the expected 
indoor environment a moisture content of 12 % ± 3 % is required for CLT at the time of manufacturing 
[Karacabeyli, 2013]. This is to avoid unwanted moisture in the construction, and on the same time limit the 
moisture related dimensional changes of the material. These dimensional changes are described in section 
10.4. Wood is a hygroscopic material with an inherent moisture-storage capacity, which can be 
advantageous since it can accommodate short-term changes in the humidity and temperature. 
 
==PAGE:237==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
225 
 
When designing the enclosure, moisture transfer and formation of dew should be considered. Hot air are 
apple to contain a larger amount of vapour compared to cool air. This means that dew are formed at a 
lower vapour pressure in cold air compared to in hot air, simply because the saturated vapour pressure is 
lower. Two major strategies regarding moisture control in building enclosure exist: to minimize the 
moisture accumulation within the building enclosure, and to maximize the drying capability of the 
enclosure. When applying the first strategy materials with a low vapour permeability is used, while 
materials with a high vapour permeability is used when applying the second strategy. Vapour permeability 
 [O/% ) /&] describes the rate of moisture transfer through a material under a gradient in water vapour 
pressure [Karacabeyli, 2013]. 
The vapour permeability of timber is dependent on relative humidity and the grain direction. The vapour 
permeability is much larger in the direction of the grain compared to perpendicular to the grain. In CLT 
panels this may be advantageous since the moisture can be distributed around the element resulting in 
faster drying. Perpendicular to the grain, cross-laminated timber got are relatively low vapour permeability, 
which means that panels with minimum five orthogonally oriented layers and a minimum thickness of 70 
mm, may be used as the only vapour resistant barrier to prevent moisture diffusion through the structure 
[Svenskt Trä, 2017]. However, joints between the elements should be sealed properly. When CLT is 
combined with other materials it may be necessary to apply an actual vapour resistant barrier to avoid 
formation of dew in the structure. Values for the vapour permeability of CLT range from  = 0.1 ∗
10¥ [O/% ) /&] at 10% relative humidity to  = 8.3 ∗10¥ [O/% ) /&] at 90% relative humidity 
[Karacabeyli, 2013]. 
When utilizing the first of the two moisture strategies it would be beneficial to use exterior insulation with 
a low permeability like extruded polystyrene (XPS), possibly combined with an impermeable vapour barrier. 
When utilizing the second strategy, permeable insulation like mineral wool would be a better choice, to 
allow the CLT panel to dry from the outside. In both cases permeable interior surface cladding should be 
ensured to allow the CLT to dry from the inside [Karacabeyli, 2013]. 
To avoid moisture from entering the ground floor walls of CLT from the concrete below a product called 
CONNECT BAND [Rothoblaas, 2017b, p. 142] from Rothoblaas is used to seal the joints in this project. The 
product is illustrated on detail drawing 504 and 505 in the associated drawing folder.    
 
10.4. Dimensional changes 
The dimensions of timber changes both due to thermal expansion and due to changes in the moisture 
content. Timber expands when heated and contracts when cooled. The effect from thermal expansion is 
very low compared to the effect from the changes in the moisture content and is therefore assumed 
insignificant in most cases [Karacabeyli, 2013]. 
Due to capillary suction, wood absorbs liquid water when it is exposed to wetting sources like rain and 
condensation. The ability to absorb water is dependent on the species, the history of wetting and drying, as 
well as the grain direction. The absorption rate in the direction of the grain is significantly larger than across 
the grain. Liquid water absorption can lead to rapid increase of the moisture content, which should be 
avoided to prevent impacts on the durability as well as formation of mold and general decay of the 
material. Exposure to excessive liquids should be limited during transport, storage, construction and 
building service. Special attention should be made to the end grain due to the high absorption rate in the 
direction of the grain [Karacabeyli, 2013]. 
==PAGE:238==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
226 
 
Timber swells when the moisture content increases and shrink when the moisture content decreases, as 
long as the moisture content is below the fiber saturation point. The dimensional changes depends on the 
direction, with very small changes in the longitudinal direction (parallel to the grain), and with larger 
changes in the radial (across the annual growth rings) and tangential (in the direction of the annual growth 
rings). Due to the build-up of CLT the dimensional changes in the plane of the panel is slightly larger than 
parallel to the grain [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. The exact value depends on how many layers are oriented in which 
direction. The swelling and shrinkage of the individual boards are minimized significantly due to the cross 
layered build-up and the good longitudinal stability of the adjacent layers. Experience from multi-story CLT 
buildings in Europe have shown that the vertical shrinkage of the walls is typically only around 3 mm per 
story, which is in general assumed acceptable [Karacabeyli, 2013]. Transverse to the plane of the CLT panel 
the dimensional change is a weighted mean value of the changes in the tangential and the radial direction. 
In table 10.3 the values for the moisture related dimensional changes are listed for the timber species pine 
and spruce, which CLT often is made of [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. The dimensional changes is defined as the 
percental change in dimension due to a change in the moisture content of 1 percent [Karacabeyli, 2013]. 
Table 10.3 - Moisture related dimensional changes for pine and spruce [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. 
Direction 
Percental change in dimension due to a change in the moisture content of 1 percent [%] 
Longitudinal 
0.01 – 0.02 
Radial 
0.19 
Tangential 
0.36 
Transverse mean 
0.24 
In plane of CLT panel 
0.016 – 0.023 
   
10.5. Airtightness 
It is important to ensure that the enclosure is made airtight since moving air transport heat and water 
vapour. Uncontrolled air leakages may cause to unwanted heat loss, heat gain or moisture accumulation, 
thus leading to decrease in energy performance, durability, and indoor comfort. To ensure airtightness a 
continuous air barrier with a low air permeability is required. The air permeability describes the rate of air 
flow through a material under a gradient in air pressure. Differences in the air pressure is often due to 
outdoor airflow, differences in air density, and presence of mechanical equipment for heating, ventilation 
and air-condition (HVAC) [Karacabeyli, 2013]. 
Cross-laminated timber panels with at least three orthogonal layers are assessed as airtight. According to 
[Stora Enso, 2015], 3-layered CLT panels and the associated joints have been tested, and the volumetric 
rates of airflow was concluded to be outside the measurable range. Other measurements performed of air 
permeability of CLT specimens got similar results and assessed the air permeability of CLT panels to be 
negligible [Karacabeyli, 2013]. Based on this CLT panels with proper sealed joints can be used as an air 
barrier. This is however not recommended by [Karacabeyli, 2013] since gaps and cracks in the panels can 
develop during building service due to shrinkage. Instead another air barrier should be added to ensure the 
airtightness of the enclosure. If a vapour resistant barrier is added for control of moisture, this barrier could 
preferable be made airtight, and function as an air barrier too.      
In this project different products from Rothoblaas is used to ensure airtightness of the separating building 
parts. These products include the adhesive tape called FLEXI BAND [Rothoblaas, 2017b, p. 116], and the 
sealing gasket called CONSTRUCTUON SEALING [Rothoblaas, 2017b, p. 145]. 
==PAGE:239==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
227 
 
10.6. Glaser method 
Since the scope of the project is to redesign an original building, the enclosure is kept as close to the 
original as possible. This means that all part of the enclosure that do not depend on the structural elements 
are kept in the original material and if appropriate in the original thickness. The thickness of the insulation 
is however modified to equalize the difference in the thickness of the structural elements in the original 
and redesigned building, and thus ensure that the total thickness of the enclosure is retained. Interior 
surface cladding is however accepted as an addition to the total thickness. In case of both the exterior walls 
and the roof structure, the load-bearing CLT panels in the redesigned project got a larger thickness than the 
load-bearing concrete elements in the original project. Consequently the thickness of the insulation layer is 
slightly reduced to equalize the difference. To investigate the performance of the enclosure a method 
called the Glaser method is used. The Glaser method is a stationary calculation method of the water vapour 
diffusion in a concerned enclosure. The method is primarily intended for analysis of the moisture content 
and to assess if accumulated water occur in the structure. However the method also include calculations of 
the thermal transmittance and the temperature development inside the structure. The method is described 
in chapter 6.3 in SBI-anvisning 224 [Brandt, 2013], and reference is made to this for further information 
about the method. A total of eight cases have been investigated, including the exterior wall and the roof of 
both the original and the redesigned building, with and without applying a vapour resistant barrier. The 
following boundary conditions have been determined: 
• 
Humidity class: 3 - Residential housing with unknown occupant density. [Table 3, Brandt, 2013]. 
• 
Concerned month: February (Month with the lowest average outdoor temperature in Denmark). 
•

•

Even through the thickness of the insulation in the redesigned enclosure have been decreased to make 
room for the increased thickness of the load-bearing elements, the enclosure still generally performs better 
in terms of thermal transmittance and accumulated water. Regarding the thermal transmittance the 
performance is slightly improved, which in the long term may decrease the energy consumption of the 
building significantly. This improvement is solely due to the much lower thermal conductivity of timber 
compared to concrete. Only in case of the exterior wall in the redesigned project a vapour resistant barrier 
could be avoided, due to the low vapour permeability of timber. However a vapour resistant barrier is 
applied for the final design of both the exterior wall and the roof to achieve a more robust design. The 
applied vapour resistant also functions as an air barrier as recommended by [Karacabeyli, 2013]. Regarding 
the amount of accumulated water in the structure without a vapour resistant barrier, both the exterior wall 
and the roof in the redesigned project accumulated at least 50 percent less water compared to the 
corresponding structures in the original design. 
The analysis with the Glaser method of all eight cases of enclosures can be found in appendix AH.1-8. The 
appendix include all the used values and equation, as well as illustrative graphs of all relevant properties.

11. 
Acoustics 
Acoustics is an important subject for all buildings and should be considered early in the design process. 
Unsatisfying acoustics in buildings may be expensive to improve in the construction or service phase, thus 
the aim should be to include a substantiate and appropriate solution early in the design phase. 
Measurements in finished buildings have shown that a good acoustic environment can be achieved in 
buildings made of cross-laminated timber panels and plates [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. 
Sound derives from pressure variations in the air and are measured in pascal. To simplify, a logarithmic 
decibel scale is used to classify the sound level. A complete design of acoustics in a building should include 
airborne sound insulation, impact sound insulation, room acoustics, traffic sounds, and sound from 
technical installations [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. In this project focus will only be on the airborne sound insulation 
and the impact sound insulation. In residential housing the acoustic conditions are divided in a number of 
acoustic classes defined in DS 490. The acoustic conditions of a residential building should at least comply 
with acoustic class C, to meet the requirements in the Danish Building regulations [BR18, 2018]. 
Airborne sound insulation is a measurement of how much sound from an airborne sound source, like 
human speech, that are transferred from one room to another, as illustrated in figure 11.1. The airborne 
sound insulation of a building component is measured in sound champers by placing a speaker in a room 
next to the room where the measurements of the sound level is performed. The higher airborne sound 
insulation level, ′c, the better airborne sound quality [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. The requirement for the 
airborne sound insulation between a room inside the building and a room outside the building is ′c ≥
55 ¼- to comply with acoustic class C [BR18, 2018]. Airborne sound insulation is relevant to both horizontal 
and vertical structures. 
 
Figure 11.1 - Airborne sound [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. 
Impact sound insulation is a measurement of much sound is transmitted from one room to another due to 
an impact source, like human walk, acting on the structures, as illustrated in figure 11.2. The impact sound 
insulation of a building component is measured in sound champers by placing a standardized impact device 
on the floor above the measured room. The lower impact sound level, ®′	,c, the better impact sound 
insulation [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. The requirement for the impact sound level in living rooms and kitchens is 
®′	,c ≤53 ¼- to comply with acoustic class C [BR18, 2018]. The impact sound insulation is only relevant to 
horizontal structures where impact sources could occur. 
==PAGE:244==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
232 
 
 
Figure 11.2 - Impact sound [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. 
Both the airborne and the impact sound insulation includes contributions from direct and flanking 
transmissions, as illustrated in figure 10.1 and 10.2. The direct transmissions is the sound that are 
transmitted by direct paths through the structures. The flanking transmission are structure born vibrations 
that are transferred through the joints in the building. Mainly the vertical joints should be a concern. The 
flanking transmissions can be limited by vibration absorbing resilient profiles, as illustrated in figure 10.3, 
which do not compromise the structural load-bearing capacity. In this project vibration resilient profiles 
have been used for wall to slab joints and for the most of the applied steel brackets, as is illustrated on the 
drawings in the associated drawing folder. Two products from Rothoblaas is used: ALADIN STRIPE SOFT 
[Rothoblaas, 2018, p. 112] for steel brackets, and the stronger product XYLOFON [Rothoblaas, 2018, p. 90] 
for wall to slab joints. 
 
Figure 11.3 - Vibration absorbing resilient profiles [Rothoblaas, 2018]. 
The biggest challenge regarding acoustics when designing with timber, including CLT, is the relatively low 
mass, which result in sound of low frequencies that are difficult to remove, and thus leads to bad acoustic 
conditions [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. In general the sound insulation of a structure can be improved by three 
methods: Adding more mass, Adding decoupled claddings like suspended ceilings, or using double 
structures. In this chapter only the floor structure and the partition walls have been assessed regarding 
acoustic conditions. In subsection 11.1 and 11.2 these two building parts are described. 
==PAGE:245==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
233 
 
11.1. Floor structure 
Both the airborne and the impact sound insulation should be considered when designing the floor 
structure. By referring to the three ways of improving the acoustic properties of a structure, three different 
types of floor structure designs is presented in table 11.1. 
Table 11.1 - Floor structure designs. 
Double construction
By separating the structural elements in two 
individual CLT panels with insulation between 
the direct sound transmissions are 
significantly decreased, while the flanking 
transmission is close to eliminated. Since the 
ceiling and floor structure is separated the 
vibrations won’t be transmitted from room to 
room. By only considering the acoustic 
conditions this is the optimal solution. 
However, since the solution is very space 
consuming and changes the requirements to 
the static system the solution is not used for 
the final design. Additionally the solution is 
very different from the original project, which 
decreases the possibility to compare the 
redesigned project with the original project.  
 
 
Suspended ceiling
By adding a suspended ceiling that in terms of 
vibrations is decoupled from the rest of the 
floor structure, the sound properties may be 
improved. The solution works as the double 
construction solution by encapsulating the 
concerned room. The method is not used for 
the final system since the suspended ceiling 
decreases the room height, which is a limiting 
factor in the project.  
 
Screed
By adding a screed layer the mass of the floor 
structure is increased. This increases the 
natural frequency of the structure and 
improves the sound insulating properties. The 
method is the least space consuming solution 
and is used for the final design of the 
redesigned project. 
  
As mentioned in table 11.1 the solution of adding a screed layer is chosen for the final design of the floor 
structure. The solution as it is presented in the table consist of 80 mm concrete screed, 30 mm impact 
sound insulation, and a CLT panel with a thickness of 200 mm. With this composition the structure got an 
airborne sound insulation level of ′c  63 ¼-  55 ¼-, and an impact sound level of ®′	,c  52 ≤
53 ¼-, which comply with the requirements in acoustic class C [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. 
==PAGE:246==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
234 
 
This composition is used as a base for the final compositions of the floor structures in the redesign project. 
The composition of the final designs is illustrated in figure 3.8 in section 3.2.2.2. When modifying the base 
composition to match the final designs the following assumptions have been made based on examples of 
building components in [Stora Enso, 2015]. 
• 
Since the mass have a large impact on the sound insulating properties, it is assumed that an 
increase of mass of one material can equalize the effect from a decrease of mass with the same 
magnitude of another material. This means that if the screed layer with a density of … = 24 O/%3 
is decreased by 5 mm, the sound insulation properties are maintained if the thickness of the CLT 
panel with a density of … = 4.2 O/%3 is increased by at least ∆P = 5 ∗24/4.2 = 28.6 %%. 
• 
By increasing the thickness of the CLT panel with 20 mm the sound insulation properties are 
assumed to improve by 1 ¼-. 
• 
For each added layer of gypsum board to the bottom side of the CLT panel the sound insulation 
properties are assumed to improve by 1 ¼-. The effect from adding one layer of gypsum board are 
assumed to equalize the effect from decreasing the thickness of the screed layer by 5 mm. 
Two different designs of the floor structure is used for the final design, one with a CLT panel with a 
thickness of ¼ = 260 %%, and one with a CLT panel with a thickness of ¼ = 300 %%. Two layers of 
gypsum boards are added to the bottom side of both variants. The thickness that the screed layer can be 
reduced to for the 260 mm and 300 mm variants is calculated in equation 11.1 and 11.2, respectively.

boards.

screed, 30 mm impact sound insulation, 300 mm CLT panel, and 2x15 mm fire-proof gypsum 
boards. 
The design of the floor structures are as mentioned based on assumptions related to the acoustic 
performance of the materials in relation to each other. The precise values for the airborne sound insulation 
and the impact sound insulation can only be determined by tests of the structures in vibration champers.

11.2. Partition walls 
Partition walls are used to separate the individual apartments in the building. Only the airborne sound 
insulation should be considered when designing the partition walls. By referring to the two of the three 
ways of improving the acoustic properties of a structure, two different types of design for partition walls is 
presented in table 11.2. 
Table 11.2 – Partition wall designs. 
Double construction
Similar to the floor structure a partition 
wall with two separate CLT plates with 
insulation between, has good sound 
insulation properties since the 
transmitted vibrations are very limited. 
If combined with a floor structure 
designed as a double construction, very 
good acoustic conditions can be 
achieved. The solution is however not 
chosen for the final design due to the 
same reasons as for the double floor 
construction described in table 11.1. 
 
 
Wooden battens on spring clips
By mounting the cladding and wooden 
battens on spring clips on both sides of 
the CLT plate the amount of 
transmitted vibrations from the room 
to the load-bearing elements is limited. 
If combined with a floor structure with 
a suspended ceiling an almost 
encapsulated room is achieved, which 
work as an independent unit in terms 
of acoustics. This design is chosen for 
the final design since it keeps the 
design as close to the original design as 
possible, while making it possible to 
perform the comparison between the 
two projects.    
 
 
  
As mentioned in table 11.2 the solution of using wooden battens on spring clips is chosen for the final 
design of the partition walls. The solution as it is presented in the table consist of a 120 mm thick CLT plate 
with 2x12.5 mm fire-proof gypsum boards mounted to wooden battens on spring clips on both sides. The 
cavity between the CLT plate and the gypsum boards are filled with 70 mm mineral wool. With this 
composition the structure got an airborne sound insulation level of ′c = 58 ¼- ≥55 ¼-, which comply 
with the requirements in acoustic class C [Svenskt Trä, 2017]. Except from an increase of the thickness of 
the CLT plate from 120 mm to 200 mm and an increase of the thickness of the gypsum boards from 12.5 
mm to 15 mm, the composition in table 11.2 is used for the final design of the partition walls.

12. 
Sustainability 
The attention on the sustainable aspects of construction and building materials have increased significantly 
during the last decades, and assessments of the sustainability may be a part of the documentation of new 
buildings in the future. The term sustainability includes many topics but is generally associated with the 
impact on the environment, in particular the emission of _÷ to the atmosphere. The building industry is a 
large contributor the total emission of _÷, thus many entrants in the industry have an increasing focus on 
the sustainability of new buildings. 
Timber, including cross-laminated timber, is generally assessed to have a beneficial footprint on the 
environment, compared to functionally equivalent non-wood alternatives like concrete. According to 
[Karacabeyli, 2013] CLT got the following advantages regarding the sustainability of the material: 
• 
Carbon storage: When trees grow they absorb _÷, which is then stored in the timber until it is 
abolished, e.g. by burning. This indicates that the stored _÷ is retained for decades in the building, 
thus removing it from the environment. This is beneficial on the short-term since an increased level 
of _÷ in the atmosphere is a present day problem. 
• 
Carbon offsets: Timber, including CLT, requires less energy and _÷ to produce compared to other 
products like steel and concrete, thus buildings made of CLT got a smaller environmental footprint. 
• 
Renewable product: If the wood used to manufacture the CLT elements comes from forest that are 
run sustainably and depletion is avoided, CLT can be classified as a renewable product. CLT 
manufacturers can obtain chain-of-custody certification for sustainable forest management. 
• 
Local materials: Since trees grow in most parts of the world, CLT manufacturers may be able to 
obtain the timber from forest close to the factories. The location for the final destination of the CLT 
elements may change the conditions. As more factories for production of CLT elements are built 
around the world, the possibility of applying locally produces CLT for a concerned building project 
increases. 
• 
Reusability: Due to the panelized system of CLT, it got a good potential for disassembly and reuse. 
This however requires that the building, and particular the connections, are designed with this in 
mind. 
• 
Operating energy savings: As described in section 10.2, CLT is a massive product with a large 
thermal mass, which enables the product to store heat. This is identified as a contributor to overall 
savings of energy due to decreased need for heating and cooling. 
The above list is not complete, and only includes the subjects that are assumed to be the most important in 
relation to the project. The sustainability of a building also depends on an efficient use of resources. From a 
sustainable point of view the exterior walls could have been designed in a more efficient way. Since the 
load-bearing part, of the exterior walls consist of continuous panels of CLT, the openings for doors and 
windows result in material waste of the cutouts. A more sustainable solution would have been to use a 
system of beams and columns instead, thus avoiding the excess material. This however would remove the 
continuity of the structure, which is needed for the robustness of the building, due to the chosen structural 
system.         
 
==PAGE:250==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
238 
 
12.1. LCA - Life Cycle Analysis 
This project is based on a building originally designed by use of prefabricated concrete elements. The scope 
of the project is to redesign the original building by use of timber structures, to investigate what it would 
take, and which challenges would arise. The original geometry of the building have been maintained 
throughout the project, which enables a comparison of the redesigned project with the original project. 
Since the two projects are based on two different materials, a comparison of the environmental impact of 
the buildings would be ideal. A common way to assess the sustainable impact of a building is to make a life 
cycle analysis, shortened LCA. An in-depth LCA is too comprehensive to fit with the boundaries of this 
project, due to the limits of the project period. Instead a simplified LCA is made using the program LCAByg 
which is developed by Statens Byggeforskningsinstitut, Aalborg Universitet København. The program is able 
to calculate the potential environmental profile and resource consumption for the building. The full life 
cycle of the building is included in the analysis as illustrated in figure 12.1. Five phases are included in the 
life cycle analysis and includes among other things the following [LCAByg, 2020]: 
1. Production phase: This phase includes extraction of raw materials and material production. 
2. Construction phase: This phase includes installation and transport of building components. 
3. Service phase: This phase includes maintenance, renovation and consumption of energy and water. 
4. End of life: This phase includes demolition, waste treatment, and disposal. 
5. Next production system: This phase includes the potential for recycling. 
 
Figure 12.1 - Life cycle of building materials [LCAByg, 2020]. 
12.1.1. Input 
The simplified LCA includes the build-up of the floor structure, the roof structure, the exterior walls, and 
the load-bearing interior walls. All other building parts are not relevant to include either because they have 
not been redesigned (Non load-bearing interior walls, interior in general, windows, doors), they have not 
been the focus of the project (Structures below the ground floor level, stairs), or information from the 
original project is lacking (Balconies). All Materials used for connections, including stringer reinforcement in 
the original product and steel brackets in the redesigned project, is not included in the analysis, and is 
assumed to have a similar effect on the results for both projects. The effect from the energy consumption is 
not considered in the analysis. Openings have not been subtracted from the elements surface areas, since 
the material included in these may be a part of the production. The build-up of the building parts from the 
original project is according to the original project documentation and the associated critical detail 
drawings attached in appendix A.1 and A.2, respectively. 
==PAGE:251==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020

12.1.2. Results 
The lifetime of a building parts depends on the materials and the function. According to LCAByg timber in 
general have an average lifetime of 100 years while concrete in general have an average lifetime of 120 
years. To make the most reasonable comparison of the original project and the redesign project a 
calculation period of 100 years is used. All input values together with the results from the analysis can be 
found in appendix AI.1 and AI.2 for the original project and the redesigned project, respectively. 
Then comparing result from life cycle analyzes with focus on the environmental performance of building 
products and components, the Global Warming Potential, shortened GWP, is often the most common and 
relevant indicator to focus on. GWP is an indicator of the equivalent mass of _÷ that the concerned 
building, component, or material releases to the surrounding environment. The value for GWP is 
[O _÷ ²p./%] if the desired value is the release per %. 
 
 
==PAGE:254==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
242 
 
By looking at the diagram in figure 12.2 it is seen, that the GWP of all the analyzed building parts in the 
redesigned project is lower than the GWP of the corresponding building parts in the original project. The 
largest difference are found for the floor structure, which is due to the large amount of concrete in the 
original design. The difference found for the other three analyzed building parts are relatively small, but still 
significant when considering large structures. The difference, when the global warming potentials from the 
individual building parts is summarized, is approximately ∆Š©/ = 60 O _÷ ²p./%, which is an 
reduction of 23.5 percent. Even through large amounts of environmental burdensome fire-protection 
gypsum boards are added to protect the CLT elements, the change from concrete to timber improves the 
environmental impact of the building. 
In the diagrams in figure 12.3 and 12.4 the GWP of the original project and the redesigned project is divided 
in material types and stages, respectively. From the diagrams it is seen that the largest contribution to the 
GWP of the original project is due to the mineral material type, which mostly consist of the concrete, that 
can be categorized as a very burdensome material when considering the environmental impacts. Most of 
the GWP from the original project are included in the production phase, which indicate that the production 
of concrete is causing large releases of _÷ to the surrounding environment. 
When considering the redesigned project, the largest contribution to the GWP is due to the CLT elements, 
even though the timber absorb _÷ during the production phase. The reason for the high GWP of timber is 
that the stored _÷ is released to the environment when the material is burned during the waste handling 
stage. Another large contributor to the GWP of the redesigned product is the fire-proof gypsum boards. If a 
more in-depth analysis of the fire-safety of the structural elements was performed, or a solution with a 
smaller safety margin was chosen, the amount of gypsum boards maybe could be reduced, thus leading to 
a decrease of the GWP. 
Based on the results from the life cycle analysis it may be assessed that the redesigned building, with load-
bearing structures of cross-laminated timber, to a certain degree performs better than the original concrete 
building when considering the global warming potential. This simplified analysis however includes many 
pitfalls, thus a more in-depth analysis should be performed before definite conclusions can be made.   
 
Figure 12.2 - GWP [kg CO2 eq./m2] comparison divided in building parts.

13. 
Cost estimate 
When choosing what material to use for the construction of a building, the costs is an important factor. In 
this chapter a simplified cost estimate is made of both the original concrete structure and the redesigned 
timber structure, which makes it possible to compare the costs. The cost estimates only include the raw 
production cost of the load-bearing elements of the same four building parts as was analyzed regarding 
environmental impact in chapter 12: The floor structure, the roof structure, the exterior walls, and the 
load-bearing interior walls. In contrast to chapter 12 no area weighted thicknesses of materials is used, thus 
the costs is calculated for the actual thicknesses of the elements. The costs for the cross-laminated timber 
elements is based on the price list from Stora Enso attached in appendix AJ.3, while the costs for the 
prefabricated concrete elements is based on the cost estimates from Betonelement foreningen attached in 
appendix AJ.4. The cost estimates have been made under the following terms: 
Cross-laminated timber 
• 
Transport costs is assumed included in the prices listed in the price list. 
• 
Non-standard C-panels are assumed to have the same costs as the corresponding standard L-
panels. 
• 
Costs for all cutouts are included in the additional costs for panel processing. 
• 
Charging dimensions are not considered. Extra costs may be added due to material waste, from 
panels not fitting with the standard charging dimension. 
• 
Exchange rate of 750 is used for conversion of the prices listed in Euro to DKK. 
• 
Cost do not include VAT. 
Prefabricated concrete elements 
• 
All elements included in the cost estimate are assumed to consist of prefabricated concrete wall 
elements and hollow core slabs. 
• 
The listed price for a hollow core slabs with a thickness of 215 mm is assumed to be valid for hollow 
core slabs with a thickness of 220 mm. 
• 
Prefabricated concrete walls are assumed to be heavily reinforced, due to the height of the 
building. 
• 
Costs for cutouts and extra reinforcement is not included in the cost estimate. 
• 
The costs are including one variant per 50 m2, as well as lifting of the elements and delivery up to 
75 km from the production site. 
• 
Cost do not include VAT. 
Since the terms for the two types of elements are different, the costs can’t be directly compared, but only 
used as an indication of the relation between the costs. However, it has been attempted to adjust the 
terms as much as possible, to achieve the most realistic comparison. Only additional cost for services that 
are area based and should be carried out for all elements, used for at least one building part, is included in 
the cost estimate. Piece based additional cost are not included since the calculation of the necessary 
amounts have not been included in the scope of the project due to limitations of the project period. 
The calculations of the estimated costs for the cross-laminated timber elements and the prefabricated 
concrete elements are listed in table 13.1 and 13.2, respectively, while the results are compared in the 
diagram in figure 13.1.  
==PAGE:258==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
246 
 
 
Regarding the wall elements the cost estimate is fairly even, and the raw costs for the CLT elements is even 
a little lower than for the corresponding concrete elements. This indicates that CLT could be profitable as 
substitute for the environmentally burdensome concrete elements, when focusing on the wall elements. 
Regarding the slab elements the cost estimate for the CLT elements is more than three times higher than 
the cost estimate for the corresponding concrete elements. This large difference is probable due to the 
very low raw price of hollow core slabs in Denmark. The difference would inevitably decrease if the cost for 
openings and extra reinforcement was including in the cost for the hollow core slabs. Denmark got a long 
tradition of production and use of hollow core slabs, which have resulted in a very effective and cheap 
production. At the present time CLT can’t compete financially with hollow core slabs in Denmark, thus 
other topics as the environmental impact should be included to make the CLT panels competitive. 
The difference are reflected in the total cost estimates of the two buildings, with the CLT solution being 
approximately 38 percent more expensive compared to the concrete solution. This may indicate that 
further development of CLT including the efficiency of raw material extraction and element production is 
needed to make the product more financially competitive.  However, these simplified cost estimates 
includes many pitfalls, and more in-depth cost estimates should be performed before any conclusions can 
be made. Due to the lower weight of CLT compared to concrete the addition of costs for trucks, cranes and 
installation would favorize the CLT elements. The calculation of the cost estimates for the cross-laminated 
timber elements and the prefabricated concrete elements can be found in appendix AJ.1 and AJ.2, 
respectively.
