14. 
Discussion 
In present day Denmark, timber is seldom used as a structural material in highrise buildings. Only the roof  
and the associated structural elements are sometimes constructed in timber since the effects on the overall 
structural system from this part of the building is often of less importance. 
Even through timber was the most used building material in the past, the trust to the material and the 
knowledge of how to use it in the construction industry have been significantly decreased through the last 
couple of centuries. The missing trust of the material is mainly due to the large city fires that occurred in 
the 18th and 19th century, that in particular had a great impact on the history and composition of the 
building mass in the Danish capital, Copenhagen. The city went through three large city fires in the period, 
which lead to increased demands in the regulations and changed the conditions for the building industry. 
The experiences from the past does still have a large impact on the general opinion on timber as a 
structural material, and especially the requirements regarding fire is consequently very straight, also when 
comparing with other countries in the world. New knowledge about the structural capabilities of timber, 
particularly in relation to fire, is being gathered from research and building projects around the world, and 
it could be argued that the general view of timber as a structural material in Denmark may be too skeptical. 
In many other countries, like Canada, Austria, Sweden, and Norway, a tendency of a more positive point of 
view on the material is in general the case. In these countries, timber structures are implemented in many 
new buildings, either as the only primary load bearing material, or in equal arrangement with other 
building materials like concrete and steel. 
By combining different types of structural materials, and using the right material where their advantages 
are, a generally more structurally effective building is achieved compared to buildings where the design 
have been fixed on one type of material. When incorporating structural elements of other materials like 
concrete and steel into a building mainly designed by use of timber elements, a concept called a hybrid 
wooden structure is achieved. As clarified in this project, timber as a structural material got both strengths 
and weaknesses due to the composition of the material. The same apply for all other structural materials 
like steel and concrete. Timber got decent properties in the direction of the grain but is relatively sensitive 
to forces perpendicular to the grain. Additionally the stiffness of timber is relatively low compared to other 
materials like steel or properly reinforced concrete. Steel got excellent strength in both tension and 
compression, but is a costly material in terms of volume, which often leads to slender profiles prone to 
stability issues when exposed to large loads. Concrete got a decent strength in compression but got almost 
no strength when exposed to tension forces. If elements made of concrete is exposed to bending or tension 
forces, reinforcement steel is most commonly applied. In that case the concrete is used to resist the 
compressive forces, while the steel is used to resist the tensile forces, and thus the materials are used to 
what they are good at. This is an example of how different structural materials are combined to achieve an 
effective solution. When considering buildings with structural elements of timber, it would generally be 
advantageous to incorporate steel and concrete where the timber otherwise would be exposed to large 
forces perpendicular to the grain or large deformations and vibrations from forces acting out of the 
element plane. By incorporating other materials, the timber in the considered elements could either be 
substituted completely or used in a combination, depending on which solution is the most effective.        
 
 
==PAGE:262==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
250 
 
One of the largest challenges with buildings of timber is the relatively low stiffness of the material. In 
particular for slab elements with long spans, this could lead to problems regarding deformations and 
vibrations. From the results achieved in this project, it could be argued that limits for the maximal length of 
the spans could be favorable to incorporate in the design phase. This could avoid many of the challenges 
occurring due to the limited stiffness and limit the need for elements with large cross-sections. If a floor 
structure made of CLT elements is desired, a maximal span length of about 6 meters could be suitable for 
residential housing. This off course depends on the demands from the building owner and the future 
occupants. Often a screed layer is casted on top of the CLT elements with the purpose of adding weight to 
the floor structure. This has a positive effect on both the vibrations and the sound insulation. Additionally 
an increased weight of the floor structure could help stabilizing the building by adding more vertical load on 
the stabilizing walls. However this leads to larger compressive forces which have to be distributed through 
the slabs as compression perpendicular to the grain, thus increasing the risk of a compressive failure. When 
adding weight to the slabs from a screed layer, which is not considered when calculating the stiffness, the 
vertical deformation will increase. This can be avoided by structurally connecting the screed layer to the 
surface of the cross-laminated timber panel. A proper connection between the concrete and the timber 
could be ensured by fastening small protruding steel stringers in the upper surface of the CLT panel and 
then casting the concrete directly on top of it. In all cases it should be ensured that the connection between 
the screed layer and the CLT panel got the sufficient shear capacity to distribute the forces between them. 
If proper designed the stiffness of the floor structure could be significantly increased, depending on the 
thickness of the screed layer in relation to the thickness of the CLT panel. The most effective relation 
between the thickness of the materials would be to ensure that the height of the compression zone in the 
bending members is equal to the height of the screed layer, thus enabling the utilization of the total 
compressive strength of the concrete. By combining the concrete screed layer with the CLT panel, the 
compression strength of concrete and the tension strength parallel to the grain of timber is utilized, while 
steel is added as the joining material, thus optimizing the use of all three materials. This could possibly 
increase the appropriate span length for CLT panels by a one or two meters, while still complying with the 
project depending serviceability limits for the deformations and vibrations. This is only one example of how 
to combine different structural materials to improve the general properties and expand the possibilities for 
the applications. 
Often the motivation for choosing timber as the structural material is either the architectural expression of 
the building or the environmental impact of the material. Due to the strict regulations regarding structural 
elements of combustible materials, fire protecting insulations is often required. The insulation cover the 
surface of the timber and spoils the intentions of using timber due to the appearance. Additionally 
suspended ceilings and cladding on clips is often necessary to comply with the requirements for the 
acoustic conditions, which in general is a weakness for timber due to the low mass. This could be avoided 
by using double constructions, but this is often a comprehensive and expensive solution. In general further 
research should be made in the fields of fire safety and acoustic conditions related to timber, including CLT, 
before the use of non-protected or non-cladded elements become a more reasonable solution. 
 
 
==PAGE:263==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
251 
 
When the motivation, for choosing timber as the structural material, is the sustainability and the 
environmental impact of the material, the aim is often to use timber elements for as many elements as 
possible and only use other materials when strictly necessary, e.g. concrete for the structures below the 
terrain. From a pure sustainable point of view this is the best solution, but when considering other topics 
like structural efficiency, durability, acoustics, and material optimization, a hybrid solution where different 
materials are incorporated where they are most suitable is generally much better. The choice of fixing the 
structural material to timber only, should probably only be made if the layout of the building is designed 
properly according to the properties and limitations of the timber elements. A design with fixed grids and 
limited spans, would often result in a structurally efficient building. A simple design may seem too ordinary 
to many architects, even though simple buildings, still can be designed in an optimal way when considering 
the occupants. Instead some architects may complicate the design to an extent where cross-laminated 
timber and other timber materials is not a good solution anymore, which may lead to a change of the 
preferred material to steel or concrete. When considering the building, which was redesigned in this 
project, the geometry is rather simple, however the location of the walls supporting the slab elements, and 
the amount and length of stabilizing shear walls is not optimal when applying cross-laminated timber 
elements. This is due to the building originally being designed for reinforced concrete elements, which have 
better properties related to stiffness and bracing. If an optimal timber building should be designed it would 
require a slight change in the interior design to fit with the material properties of cross-laminated timber. 
An important reason why the knowledge and use of timber is still limited in Denmark, compared to many 
other countries, is the long tradition of using concrete and prefabricated elements. Therefore concrete is 
often assessed as the easiest and safest solution, thus complicating the initializing of an increased use of 
structural timber elements like cross-laminated timber in the Danish building industry. Generally more 
knowledge about how to build with timber in highrise projects is needed. In many cases the knowledge can 
only be obtained by experience from full size projects constructed after the applicable regulations of the 
concerned country. The first projects will include increased risks both financially and structurally but is 
necessary to accumulate the required knowledge of timber as a structural material used for highrise 
buildings.   
   
 
==PAGE:264==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
252 
 
 
 
==PAGE:265==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
253 
 
15. 
Conclusion 
Timber has been used as a building material from the earliest known years of human existence, and the use 
of the material for structural purposes have evolved through the years. Due to the large city fires, that 
occurred in many densely populated cities in the 18th and 19th century, stricter regulations concerning the 
use of timber as a building material was introduced. This together with the invention of reinforced concrete 
and the increased popularity of wrought steel, decreased the use of timber in new buildings. Even through 
the reputation of wooden structures decreased due to the severe city fires in the past, the invention and 
development of new building techniques and systems with wooden structures have continued. Nowadays 
the use and reputation of timber structures are increasing, which mainly is caused by a larger focus on the 
sustainability of buildings and materials. Timber is our only renewable material and one of the most 
sustainable materials available. The advantages of timber includes the high strength to weight ratio, its 
ability to transfer both tension and compression, its insulating properties, and is high durability.   
Timber as a structural material is divided in solid timber and engineered wood products (EWPs). Solid 
timber is the base material cut in the desired dimensions. The use of solid timber is limited by the available 
lengths and cross-section sizes. To exceed the limits of solid timber EWPs have been invented. Often EWPs 
consist of lamellas or small pieces of timber glued together with adhesives. The most popular of the EWPs 
for use in highrise buildings is cross-laminated timber panels, also shortened CLT. The product consist of 
multiple lamellas bonded together in an orthogonally fashion, creating panels and plates with good 
strength and stiffness properties. In particular the large shear strength in the plane of the element is 
advantageous for use as stabilizing diaphragms in highrise buildings. 
The scope of the thesis have been to redesign an original concrete building by use of timber structures. 
Theo original building consists of 9 stories with residential apartments and is constructed by use of 
prefabricated concrete elements. The overall design of the building is maintained throughout the thesis to 
allow the most precise comparisons of the original building in concrete structures with the redesigned 
building in timber structures. The building is mainly redesigned by use of CLT elements, which is applied for 
all load-bearing slabs and walls. Other materials are used where it has been found necessary. 
The structural system of the building is made as a plate system with solid horizontal and vertical elements. 
The floor and the roof structures are carried by assumed one-way spanning CLT panels, which distribute the 
vertical load to the load-bearing walls. The horizontal load acting on the building is distributed to the 
stabilizing walls by assumed infinite stiff horizontal diaphragms. The building includes six continuous load-
bearing and stabilizing shear walls made of CLT plates, three walls in each main direction of the building. 
The horizontal load are distributed to the shear walls according to their stiffness. The direction of the slab 
spans is chosen with the aim to distribute the total vertical load as evenly as possible to the stabilizing 
shear walls, thus creating as much favorable stabilizing load as possible. 
The structural system and the structural elements are investigated regarding the load-bearing capacity in 
the ultimate limit states (ULS), and regarding the stiffness against deformations and vibrations in the 
serviceability limit states (SLS). In the ultimate limit states the persistent design situation, the accidental 
design situation, and the seismic design situation are addressed. The vertical loads acting on the building 
consist of self-weight from the building parts, imposed load on the floor structures, and snow and wind 
load on the roof structure. The horizontal loads consist of wind load, seismic load, and forces due to 
geometric imperfections. Wind load combined with a contribution from the geometric imperfections have 
been determined as the critical horizontal load case. The CLT elements are designed in strength class C24. 
==PAGE:266==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
254 
 
To verify the bracing system of the building, the stabilizing shear walls made of CLT plates is verified against 
overturning, compressive failure, sliding, and deformation. The largest horizontal forces acting on the shear 
walls is due to wind load from west, which is the critical wind direction. This means that the walls located 
parallel to the wind from west, denoted the x-direction, are exposed to the largest horizontal loads. 
Unfortunately, the total stiffness of the walls in the x-direction is lower than the total stiffness of the walls 
in the y-direction, thus the walls in the x-direction is clearly the most critical.  
To secure the shear walls against overturning and compressive failure, tension anchoring is added where it 
is necessary. Compression failure can both occur in the shear wall and in the slab below. The compression 
strength perpendicular to the direction of the fibers in timber are significantly lower than the compression 
strength parallel to the direction of the fibers, thus the slabs are the most critical when considering the 
compressive failure. If only considering the overturning, all three shear walls in the x-direction require 
tension anchoring on all floors, while only one shear wall in the y-direction require tension anchoring on 
the lowest three floors. For two of the shear walls in the x-direction, tension anchoring could be avoided on 
some of the intermediate floors, but since the wind suction on the roof causes the shear walls on the upper 
floor to overturn, it have been chosen to apply tension anchoring on all floors. 
Compression failure in the slabs is occurring on the lower floors at many of the shear walls. To avoid this a 
toothed joint with 250 mm wide teeth alternating between wall and slab teeth are used for the wall to floor 
junctions for the slabs from above the ground floor to above the 5th floor. This means that the compression 
force then is distributed directly from wall to wall, without exposing the slabs to large forces perpendicular 
to the grain. However, with this solution only half the width of the compression zone can be used to 
transfer the loads. Results show that compression failure now occurs in some of the shear walls on the 
lower floors. This is avoided by increasing the tension force to be anchored, and thus increasing the length 
of the compression zone. By summarizing the results from the investigation of overturning and 
compression failure, tension anchoring is required on all floors for all three shear walls in the x-direction, as 
well as on the four lowest floors for one of the shear walls in the y-direction. In general the shortest shear 
walls are the most critical regarding overturning and compression failure. 
For tension anchoring it has been chosen to use continuous tension rods, which is anchored to the 
foundations. The tension rods receive the tension force from the shear walls via steel plates placed in 
recesses on top of the shear walls. If the tension force allow it, the diameter of the tension rods is reduced 
on each floor with use of reinforcement reduction couplers. The tension rods are applied in pairs with 
dimensions from Ø10 to Ø40 depending on the magnitude of the tension force. 
Vibration absorbing profiles are used to limit transmission of flanking sounds in the connections between 
walls and slabs. This means that friction in the connections between the shear walls and slabs can’t be 
relied on. Therefore the stabilizing contribution from the self-weight of the structure is neglected when 
investigating the shear walls in the case of sliding. As a result all shear walls should be anchored against the 
horizontal shear forces acting on the building. Different steel brackets are used to anchor the shear walls 
against sliding. The type and number of required brackets depends on the geometry of the joint and the 
magnitude of the shear force. Since the shear forces are distributed to the shear walls according to their 
stiffness, the magnitude of the load and the required amount of bracket increase with an increased length 
of the shear wall. 
 
 
==PAGE:267==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
255 
 
Due to the large stiffness of CLT when exposed to plate forces, the horizontal deformation of the shear 
walls mainly consist of the contribution from expansion of the tension rods and sliding of the shear 
brackets. Both the local deformations on each floor and the global deformations of the shear walls have 
been investigated. In both cases the deformations is far below the limits, thus the design is sufficient to 
avoid excessive deformations. The deformations of the shear walls have not been a designing factor for the 
shear walls, which is due to the neglecting of friction when verifying against sliding. The load-bearing 
capacity of the shear walls is verified against three failure mechanisms: net shear failure of the boards, 
torsional failure of the glued surfaces, and gross shear failure of the whole plate. The most critical of the 
failure mechanisms is the torsional failure of the glued surfaces. By using five layered CLT plates with a total 
thickness of 200 mm and boards with a width of 160 mm, the utilization rate for the most critical of the 
shear walls is õ = 0.80 in the most critical load combination, which is acceptable. The most critical shear 
wall is the longest wall in the x-direction located on the lowest floor in the building, which is also the shear 
walls which is exposed to the largest horizontal loads. 
CLT panels are used as the load bearing elements in both the floor, roof, and balcony structures. In general 
the designing factor have been the deformations due to the relatively long spans of up to 7.17 meters. For 
the floor slabs the rolling shear strength have also been a designing factor due to the toothed joints that 
decrease the shear capacity with 50 percent. Additionally for both the floor slabs and the balcony slabs the 
vibrations criteria is also a designing factor due to the long spans and the low weight of the CLT panels. 
Regarding the floor slabs it have been investigated what effect it would have on the vibration properties if 
either the stiffness of the screed layer was included in the overall stiffness of the floor, or the transverse 
load distribution due to support on four sides was included. The results indicated that both approaches 
improved the values for both the first natural frequencies and the accelerations. However the results highly 
depend on multiple variables including the length of the span, the width of the slab zone, the thickness of 
the screed layer and the bending stiffness of the CLT panel. In general the largest improvements was found 
when both the stiffness of the screed layer and the transverse load distribution was included. Sufficient 
capacity is achieved by using CLT panels with a thickness of 140 mm for the balconies, 220 mm for the roof 
structure, and either 260 or 300 mm for the floor structure. The thickness of 300 mm is used in the area of 
the building where the bathroom cabins are located unfavorable regarding the support conditions. 
Additionally it have been investigated what the length of the spans should be fixed to, if a CLT thickness of 
200 mm was the goal. The results vary from ® = 5.68 % to ® = 3.50 % for the floor slabs depending on the 
presence and location of the bathroom cabins. This indicates that the high self-weight of the bathroom 
cabins is a very critical factor when designing the floor slabs. 
To ensure that the theory of infinite stiff horizontal diaphragms is valid, the joints between the slabs must 
be designed properly. The best solution for this have been determined as crossed screws distributed 
uniformly along the joints. At the walls where a toothed joint is used, which limit the number of screws that 
can be applied, tension boards of solid timber fastened to the CLT-panels with screws is used to resist the 
tension forces from the moment and normal force. The tension boards are placed in the outer slab teeth, 
while crossed screws, used to resist the shear forces, are placed in the remaining slab teeth. All four 
facades and all six interior partition walls are vertical load-bearing, even though some does not support the 
slabs. All the load-bearing walls are verified against buckling, which have been the designing factor. By 
using CLT plates with a thickness of 200 mm with the outer layers orientated vertical the sufficient load-
bearing capacity is achieved for all investigated walls. To avoid failure in the accidental design situation of a 
fire, two-sided exposure should be avoided by proper fire sectioning. Failure in the case of one-sided fire 
exposure can be avoided by installing 2 x 15 mm fire-proof gypsum boards on the wall surfaces. 
==PAGE:268==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
256 
 
The vertical load transfer from wall to wall through the slabs have been investigated, both regarding 
compressive failure and compressive deformations. At the wall resisting the largest vertical forces, 
compressive failure occurs in the slab above the lowest three floors. By using a toothed joint the 
compression can be transferred as compression parallel to the direction of the fibers in the vertical layers in 
the walls, instead of perpendicular to the direction of the fibers in the slabs. Compressive failure is then 
avoided, and the utilization rates is decreased by approximately 45 percent. When transferring the load 
through the slabs the summarized final vertical compressive deformation of the slabs is 	 = 38.80 %%, 
which is relatively much. By using a toothed joint in the lowest six slabs the deformation can be decreased 
to only 	 = 7.98 %%, which is assumed to be favorable. 
At the facade, the slabs are mounted on the inside of the exterior walls to achieve as much height of the 
lintels above the openings in the facades as possible. The slabs are fastened to the exterior walls by use of 
aluminium connectors, which is concealed to ensure that they are not exposed to heat in case of a fire. The 
aluminium connectors can’t resist horizontal and tensile forces, thus angle brackets are used to anchor the 
exterior walls to the slabs. The brackets are both used to secure the exterior walls against suction from the 
wind, and to brace the walls against buckling with a length of more than one floor height. Angled screws is 
used to secure a safe joint between the wall element above and below the slabs.   
Except for the lintel above the main entrance on the ground floor, all lintels are made of CLT integrated in 
the wall elements. Above the main entrance an LVL lintel is used, since a higher strength is required here, 
due to the lintel resisting vertical loads from more than one floor. Steel beams are used to carry the 
balconies and some of the slab elements in the floor structure above the 7th floor. Steel is only used 
because timber does not have the sufficient load-bearing capacity to match the loading conditions and the 
geometric limitations. An alternative solution by using glulam beams instead of steel beams in the floor 
structure above the 7th floor have been investigated. For all four glulam beams the required height of the 
cross-section exceeded the geometric limits, and therefore they are not used for the final design. 
The robustness of a structure must be considered to avoid a progressive collapse in a case where one or 
more structural elements fail. By using the loss of element method to verify the robustness of a building it 
must be ensured that the maximal floor area that collapses in a situation where a three meter long wall 
fails does not exceed 15 % of the total floor area. In a worst case scenario a floor area of 41.6 m2 collapses, 
which is less than 15 % of the total floor area which is equal to 43.2 m2. Additionally it is assured that the 
walls above the removed wall piece have the sufficient load-bearing capacity to redistribute the forces to 
the remaining structural elements. 
Regarding fire both the exposure from a standard fire and a parametric fire have been considered. The 
purpose of the standard fire is to ensure proper conditions for the evacuation of occupants, while the 
parametric fire simulates a full fire cycle, and can be used to ensure that the structure maintain the 
sufficient load-bearing capacity to avoid both local failures and global progressive collapses. For both design 
fires it can be concluded that delamination due to the use of non-heat-proof adhesives may increase the 
charring depth in timber elements made by lamellas glued together. The increase is most significant for 
lightly insulated elements consisting of thin lamellas. The more lamellas that are included in the charring 
the larger is the increase of the charring rate due to delamination. For all timber elements 2 x 15 mm fire-
proof gypsum boards are used, both to limit the charring depth, but also to ensure that the level of 
combustible material are limited in the initial phase of a fire. The fire insulation used to protect the steel 
beams in case of a fire is designed to ensure that the temperature of the steel does not reach a 
temperature of 280o in the concerned period, since this is the temperature where the surrounding timber 
may initiate charring. 
==PAGE:269==
Andreas Juul Larsen 
Master Thesis 
Documentation report 
S144609 
DTU Civil Engineering 
27-06-2020 
257 
 
From the investigation of material properties related to heat, moisture, and airtightness, it can be 
concluded that timber and CLT got many good characteristics regarding these topics. The thermal 
conductivity of cross-laminated timber is approximately 13 times lower than for concrete and only 
approximately 3 times higher than for insulation. This indicates that cross-laminated timber can add a fair 
amount of thermal insulation to a building part, and in this sense is superior to concrete. Cross-laminated 
timber also got a high specific heat capacity, which means that the product helps to limit the fluctuations of 
the room temperature, thus decreasing the energy consumption for heating and cooling. Cross-laminated 
timber are in general considered airtight if the joints are concealed properly, but due to gaps and cracks 
developing during building service due to shrinkage, it is recommended to install a continuous air barrier. 
Timber in general are sensitive to moisture, even though the material can accommodate short-term 
changes of the humidity and temperature. Especially in the direction of the fibers the vapour permeability 
of timber is large, which lead to large dimensional changes in case of changes in the moisture content. Due 
to this timber should be protected against water exposure, and extra focus should be on the exposed front 
faces. An analysis of the thermal insulation and moisture content in the enclosure have been performed by 
use of the Glaser method. Both the enclosure of the original project and the redesigned project have been 
analyzed. Generally the results indicated that cross-laminated timber performs slightly better than concrete 
and could make the use of a vapour resistant barrier in the exterior walls unnecessary.  
The sound insulating properties of timber is generally a weakness of the material, which is mainly due to 
the low weight. To comply with the requirements in the Danish building regulation, additional layers of 
material have to be added both in case of the floor structure and the partition walls. Due to limitation of 
the floor height an addition of a screed layer to the floor structure is used to achieve the required sound 
insulation. The solution works by adding more mass to the structure, which is of high importance for the 
sound insulating properties. The required sound insulation is reached for the partition walls by adding a 
cladding system on each side of the wall, which is decoupled from the load-bearing elements with clips, 
thus limiting the transmitting of vibrations through the wall. 
Two highly relevant topics for comparison of the original building with the redesigned building, is the 
environmental impact and the expenses. For both topics only the four most relevant building parts have 
been included: the floor structure, the roof structure, the exterior walls, and the load-bearing partition 
walls. In the case of the environmental impact the buildings have been compared by use of a simplified life 
cycle analysis, which considers the full life cycle of the materials when calculation the environmental 
impact. The results from the analysis indicates that the redesigned building got a total global warming 
potential, shortened GWP, that is approximately ∆Š©/ = 60 O _÷ ²p./% lower than the GWP 
calculated for the original building. Even through a lot of burdensome gypsum is added to protect the 
timber elements in case of fire, the redesigned building still performs significantly better than the original 
building when considering the environmental impact. This is mainly due to timber performing far better 
environmentally than concrete, which is considered as a very burdensome material regarding the 
sustainability. Regarding the expenses, only the raw production cost have been compared. A simplified cost 
estimate indicates that the cost of cross-laminated timber may be financially competitive when considering 
the wall elements. This is however not the case for the slab elements, which is mainly due to the very low 
production costs of hollow core slabs in Denmark. The country got a long tradition of production and use of 
hollow core slabs, which have resulted in a very effective and cheap production. At the present time CLT 
can’t compete financially with hollow core slabs in Denmark, but if other topics like the environmental 
impacts is included in the assessment of the materials, cross-laminated timber may be more competitive. 
Both the life cycle analysis and the cost estimates are very simplified and include many pitfalls, thus more 
in-depth analyzes and calculations should be performed before any proven conclusions can be made.
